==PAGE:3==
Abstract
In this thesis, we address the challenge of Machine Unlearning (MU), which is the process of removing
specific data from a trained Deep Neural Network (DNN) without costly retraining.
Motivated by
privacy regulations like the ’right to be forgotten’, we explore the failures of existing methods and
propose novel solutions. Framed by the scenario of a ’red elephant’ that wants to be forgotten as it no
longer identifies as such, our research focuses on approximate, model-agnostic, sample-level unlearning.
Our investigation reimplements and empirically analyzes established MU techniques, including Sharded,
Isolated, Sliced, and Aggregate (SISA), Amnesiac Unlearning (AU), Selective Synaptic Dampening
(SSD), and SCalable Remembering and Unlearning unBound + Rewind (SCRUB+R), through exper-
iments on a custom synthetic dataset for qualitative decision boundary analysis, and on benchmark
datasets MNIST and CIFAR-10 using a Feed-Forward Neural Network (FFNN) and a Vision Trans-
former (ViT), respectively.
We make three primary contributions. First, we diagnose the failure of SSD due to hyperparameter
sensitivity and propose BO-SSD, a framework using Bayesian Optimization (BO) to automate parameter
selection. Second, we demonstrate SCRUB+R’s inherent instability and its convergence to catastrophic
forgetting, and in response, we develop Teacher Ascent (TA): A fine-tuning objective, inspired by
Elastic Weight Consolidation (EWC), that preserves performance on the data to retain while effectively
forgetting. Third, to address polysemanticity, we introduce a novel method using a Sparse Autoencoder
(SAE) to unlearn by dampening features in a more disentangled, ’monosemantic’ representation.
Our work provides a critical evaluation of existing methods and contributes with empirically validated
approaches that advance the field. While we conclude no single solution is perfect, our development
of TA and the novel SAE-based approach offer significant steps towards making MU a reliable and
practical tool, ultimately enabling models to ’forget the red elephant’.

Preface
This Master’s thesis was prepared at the Department of Applied Mathematics and Computer Science at
the Technical University of Denmark (DTU). It is submitted by August Brogaard Tollerup (s204139) in
fulfillment of the requirements for the degree of Master of Science in Engineering in Business Analytics,
and by Michael Alexander Harborg (s204138) and Andreas Lyhne Fiehn (s204125) in fulfillment of the
requirements for the degree of Master of Science in Engineering in Human-Centered Artificial Intelli-
gence. The project was done in collaboration with Eriksholm Research Centre under the supervision of
Tommy Sonne Alstrøm (DTU) and Niels Henrik Pontoppidan (Eriksholm).
Kongens Lyngby, June 30, 2025
August Brogaard Tollerup
Michael Alexander Harborg
Andreas Lyhne Fiehn

Acknowledgements
First of all, we are profoundly grateful for the mentorship of our main supervisor at the Technical Uni-
versity of Denmark (DTU), Tommy Sonne Alstrøm. His willingness to engage in countless conversations
about Machine Unlearning and beyond was instrumental to the success of this project.
A special thanks is extended to Niels Henrik Pontoppidan, our industry supervisor from Eriksholm
Research Centre. He especially helped ground the project and discussions in industry needs. We hope
this research proves to be a valuable step forward for the application of Machine Unlearning in the
hearing aid sector. We also wish to acknowledge Paula López Diez from Eriksholm Research Centre for
her positive presence in our earlier meetings.
We would also like to thank Stefan Schöpf PhD candidate at Cambridge University and student re-
searcher at Google Deepmind for taking the time to talk to us about his work on SSD [FSB24] and
Adaptive SSD (ASSD) [SFB24]. His clarifications were very valuable.
Additionally, we extend our gratitude to Gemini, Claude, and ChatGPT. Their assistance with proof-
reading, refining our phrasing and ”vibing-coding” some nice plots and boilerplate code has been in-
valuable. It is a curious, yet appreciated, irony that these language models aided a study dedicated to
understanding and implementing methods to make them ”forget”.

Notation
Our notation is mostly based on Goodfellow, Bengio, and Courville [GBC16]. Below, we have listed the
notation almost exactly as phrased in the book [GBC16], along with few additions and some notation
that is more aligned with this exact thesis and machine unlearning, e.g. forget sets and retain sets.
Most of the notation is standard calculus, set theory, etc.
Symbol
Description
Scalars, Vectors, Matrices, and Tensors
a
Scalar.
a
Vector.
A
Matrix.
diag(a)
Square diagonal matrix with a as diagonal entries.
a
Scalar random variable.
a
Vector random variable.
A
Matrix random variable.
Linear Algebra Operations
AB
Matrix product of A and B. (Assumes dimensions match for multiplication,
e.g., A ∈Rm×n, B ∈Rn×p)
⟨a, b⟩
Dot product of vectors a and b.
ab or a⊺b
Dot product of vectors a and b. (Often used when a is a row vector and b is
a column vector, or simply for brevity when the operation is clear from con-
text).
A⊺
Transpose of matrix A.
A ⊙B
Element-wise (Hadamard) product of A and B.
det(A)
Determinant of A.
tr(A)
Trace of matrix A.
A−1
Inverse of matrix A.
∥a∥p
Lp norm of vector a.
∥a∥
L2 norm (Euclidean norm) of vector a.
Indexing and Contextual Modifiers
ai
The i-th element (a scalar) of vector a. Subscripts are primarily used for in-
dexing.
ai
The i-th vector in a sequence or collection of vectors (e.g., the i-th data sam-
ple).
a(k)
A label for states or versions of a scalar, not an exponent (e.g., value at itera-
tion k).
a(k)
A label for states or versions of a vector (e.g., activation vector for layer k).
Ai,j
Element i, j of matrix A.
Ai,:
Row i of matrix A.
A:,i
Column i of matrix A.
ai
Element i of the random vector a.
Calculus
dy
dx
Derivative of y with respect to x.
∂y
∂x
Partial derivative of y with respect to x.
==PAGE:12==
x
Notation
Symbol
Description
∇xy
Gradient of y with respect to vector x.
∇Xy
Matrix derivative of y with respect to matrix X.
R
f(x)dx
Indefinite integral of f(x) with respect to x.
R
S f(x)dx
Definite integral of f(x) with respect to x over the set S.
Sets
A
A set. Other symbols can be used and will be explicitly specified.
{0, 1}
The set containing 0 and 1.
{0, 1, . . . , n}
The set of all integers between 0 and n.
N+
The set of positive integers {1, 2, 3, . . .}.
[n]
The set {1, 2, . . . , n} for n ∈N+, i.e. the standard indexed.
[a, b]
The real interval including a and b (closed interval).
(a, b]
The real interval excluding a but including b (half-open interval).
A \ B
Set subtraction, i.e., the set containing the elements of A that are not in B.
Probability and Information Theory
p(a)
A probability distribution. This can be a probability mass function (PMF) if
the random variable a is discrete, or a probability density function (PDF) if a
is continuous. The nature of the variable and the context (e.g., use of summa-
tion P vs. integration
R
) will clarify which is being used.
a ∼p(a)
The distribution of a is given by p(a).
Ex∼p(x) [f(x)]
Expectation of f(x) with respect to the distribution of x given by the PDF
p(x).
Var(f(x))
Variance of f(x) under p(x).
Cov(f(x), g(x))
Covariance of f(x) and g(x) under p(x).
DKL(p(x)∥q(x))
Kullback-Leibler divergence between probability distributions p and q.
N(x; µ, Σ)
Gaussian distribution over random vector x with mean µ and covariance Σ.
1(condition)
Indicator function; 1 if ’condition’ is true, 0 otherwise.
Unlearning Specific Notation
D
The full training dataset of a model prior to unlearning.
Dr
The subset of the training set D to retain after applying unlearning.
Df
The subset of the training set D to remove via unlearning.
Dval
A holdout validation dataset that a model has not seen during its training.
θ
A set of model parameters.
Mθ
A parameterized mapping function that maps from some input space to some
output space. An example of its use could be when referring to a neural net-
work with the weights θ.

CHAPTER 1
Introduction
You suddenly realize that a widely held belief about you is false: You are not a red elephant. Yet, every-
one you meet today treats you as if you are. You repeatedly try to correct them, but despite your efforts,
the idea seems to stick. In some cases, your attempts to clarify only reinforce the original misconception.
This simple narrative highlights a core challenge in both human and machine intelligence: the diﬀi-
culty of forgetting. Once information is learned, it tends to become deeply embedded and hard to
remove. In machine learning systems, the lack of built-in forgetting mechanisms creates serious obsta-
cles to data privacy, individual control, and compliance with legal regulations.
In recent years, machine learning has undergone a dramatic shift toward increasing both model and
dataset sizes. This trend has enabled the development of powerful, general-purpose models across mul-
tiple modalities. However, this has also raised critical concerns around data sovereignty, model security,
and the governance of machine learning systems. Can personal data ever be fully removed from a model,
or do remnants persist and remain potentially vulnerable to malicious extraction? Are organizations
using data in transparent, fair, and controlled ways, or could it be repurposed in ways that are opaque,
biased, or even harmful to the individual?
In some cases, the need for unlearning arises from legal requirements, but for this to be meaningful, it
must be economically feasible and implemented in a way that does not stifle innovation.
Furthermore, as data collection pipelines become more automated and large-scale, a growing concern
is the lack of transparency in how and by whom data is collected. Individuals, or corporations, often
have limited visibility into whether their data has been ingested into a model, under what terms, and
for what downstream purposes. Increasingly, large models have been found to generate outputs con-
taining proprietary or restricted content. A notable example is Stability AI, which has been reported
to generate images containing watermarks resembling those of Getty Images [BB23]. Cases like this
illustrate the diﬀiculty of tracking whether specific data has been used for training and the complexi-
ties involved in responding to large-scale unlearning requests, with large and complex models requiring
costly compute and time to retrain from scratch.
One promising direction for addressing these challenges is the emerging field of Machine Unlearning
(MU). Progress in this area could grant individuals more fine-grained control over their data, while
alleviating the financial burden on companies striving to comply with data protection regulations. At
the same time, it contributes to the broader goal of developing and implementing ethical AI systems.
While this thesis primarily focuses on deep neural networks rather than large language models and
classical machine learning models, the underlying motivation remains the same: Enabling models to
forget you as a red elephant reliably, eﬀiciently, and verifiably.
1.1
Motivational Challenges
Whether driven by privacy laws, security threats, or the need for trustworthy systems, Machine Unlearn-
ing (MU) is emerging as a key capability. We explore its potential through three critical dimensions:
privacy and regulatory compliance, model security, and model fidelity. We have illustrated these differ-
ent motivating challenges of MU in Figure 1.1.
Privacy laws, Artificial Intelligence (AI) regulations, and intellectual property protection present signif-
icant compliance challenges. Model security requires preventing or securing against adversarial attacks
==PAGE:18==
2
1 Introduction
and data poisoning. Model fidelity demands mitigating biases and addressing outdated or incorrect
data. The following sections will explore these issues with examples and further motivation.

Figure 1.1: Overview of motivational challenges
1.1.1
Privacy and Regulation
Governments worldwide have introduced regulations to address data privacy and control. Examples
include Article 17 of the EU’s General Data Protection Regulation (GDPR), known as the ”right to
be forgotten” [EC16], the California Consumer Privacy Act [Cha18], the California AI Transparency
Act [Bec24], the Brazilian Data Protection Law (LGPD) [Bra18], and the South Korean Personal
Information Protection Act (PIPA) [Nat11]. All of these give the user the right to have their data
deleted to some extent.
Other regulations, such as Canada’s Personal Information Protection and
Electronic Documents Act (PIPEDA) [Par00], do not explicitly give citizens the ”right to be forgotten”,
but it does allow for users to request corrections to personal data and limit how long companies can
retain data.
Complying with requests to remove user data from a database is straightforward. Machine Learning
(ML) models pose a bigger challenge as they often ”memorize” training data [Car+19; FJR15] and can
be treated as outputs derived from their training data [JRJ23]. Additionally, the stochastic nature of
many ML implementations makes it especially challenging to identify if and how the model weights are
affected by the training data [JRJ23]. To further complicate this, changes in the model representation
might not result in changed model behavior, i.e., functional equivalence does not imply representational
equivalence [Kla+23]. This makes it diﬀicult to erase specific personal information from the network,
and document that the information is deleted as required by some privacy laws.
Apart from privacy regulation, the European Union has also recently implemented the Artificial Intelli-
gence Act, which defines risk levels for AI systems and regulates their behavior, such as a requirement
to ”prevent or minimize harmful or otherwise undesirable behavior” [EC24]. This could be LLMs gen-
erating instructions to create bombs, suicide advice, trying to manipulate users, or generating incorrect
information. Here MU could be useful as it can potentially remove information about such subjects
from a model. It has to be mentioned that removing the knowledge about a harmful subject does not
necessarily ensure that the model cannot generate outputs about it, i.e. removal of information does
not ensure output suppression [Coo+24].
Other considerations include copyright infringement. As introduced earlier, we see an increase in copy-
right infringement lawsuits such as the Getty Images case [BB23], or The New York Times against
OpenAI [CG24]. The claim is that OpenAI is breaking copyright laws as ChatGPT memorizes and
regurgitates material owned by the newspaper. The authors argue that if the model can reproduce
copyrighted material, it must be stored in the model weights, which makes it a copy in a legal sense.
MU would enable model owners to delete content requested by copyright holders. This could increase
the confidence of the public in ML deployers and distributors of model security.
1.1.2
Model Security
It is a well-known challenge that the output of neural network models can be sensitive to small per-
turbations of their inputs [Sze+14]. This behavior can be exploited to attack the model by creating
adversarial samples with the goal of getting the model to act in an undesired way, e.g., a misclassifica-
tion or a failure to detect malicious activity in security applications [Big+13; GSS15; Dal+04; FFF18;
KGB17; Wan+23]. Real-world examples of adversarial attacks include attaching small stickers to stop
==PAGE:19==
1.1 Motivational Challenges
3
signs whose motif is learned such that it makes a classifier confuse it with a speed limit sign [Eyk+18].
Another example is the identification of words that lead to reducing the accuracy of phishing email
classifiers [Che+22], misclassification of financial fraud data, and confusing voice assistants by perturb-
ing the voice input [Wan+23]. While these adversarial attacks at inference time or the generation of
adversarial test samples do not directly motivate MU, some cases include actively updating the model
with samples from the inference queries, i.e., online learning [Hoi+21]. The ability to remove the effect
of such samples without retraining could be beneficial.
Another form of attack on ML models is the manipulation of training data, which poses a significant
threat to ML models, particularly those highly sensitive to individual data points [Hua+11]. As demon-
strated in [KL17], even a single, visually imperceptible perturbation to a training image can drastically
impact a model’s performance. Specifically, they showed that perturbing just one image out of 1800
in the training set of an image classifier (initially achieving 591/600 correct classifications) resulted in
a 57% flip rate of those correct classifications. Furthermore, perturbing only ten training images led
to all but one of the correct classifications being flipped. A related attack on training data is ”poison-
ing”, where adversarial samples are deliberately introduced into the dataset to compromise the model’s
integrity [Yan+17; Jag+18; BNL12; PML18].
A recent study investigates the effects of four distinct data poisoning attacks on language models
[Zha+25]. The first attack, belief manipulation, biases the model’s responses in specific situations. The
second, a denial-of-service attack, causes the model to generate nonsensical output. The third attack,
known as jailbreaking, manipulates the model into complying with harmful requests. Lastly, the prompt
or context extraction attack enables the attacker to access private information. The study demonstrates
that three out of these four adversarial attacks can be successfully executed with a data poisoning rate
of just 0.1% of the total pre-training data [Zha+25]. Furthermore, the denial-of-service attack was
achieved with an even lower poisoning rate of 0.001% of the pre-training data. These vulnerabilities
are particularly concerning when an attacker has access to the training data, an increasingly common
scenario for large-scale models, as their data is often crowdsourced, such as through web scraping.
The ability to remove or ”forget” these problematic samples could mitigate these risks and potentially
eliminate the need for costly retraining. Furthermore, the ability to forget problematic samples would
allow model owners to remove data points that are outdated, mislabeled, or no longer trustworthy.
1.1.3
Model Fidelity
Samples in the training data do not necessarily have to be poisonous or harmful for us to want to
remove them. Another motivation for MU is to maintain model fidelity, meaning the model remains
faithful and trustworthy in its behavior. One example is when a subset of the data a model is trained
on is outdated because of recent events in the world, e.g., ”Biden is president”, ”France is the reigning
champion of the World Cup”, ”The Queen of Denmark is Margrethe II”. Retraining a very large model
each time a new major world event happens might not be feasible, and a favorable approach could be to
remove or edit the concept or data point from the already existing model. It has also been shown that
some of the most widely used benchmarking datasets have an estimated 3.3% of label errors [NAM21].
Furthermore, the ability to ”forget” samples that bias ML models is a key motivation for MU. Numerous
public datasets and ML models in production exhibit biases. For instance, the COMPAS tool, used by
US courts to predict recidivism, showed racial bias by being more likely to classify black individuals as
recidivists compared to white individuals [Cor+16; Kir17]. Similarly, beauty.AI, an ML-judged online
beauty contest, was found to be biased against contestants with darker skin tones [Lev16].
In the text domain, the Microsoft COCO image captioning dataset [Che+15] demonstrates a bias to-
wards men, leading models trained on it to adopt gender-stereotypical biases [ZWR21; Hir+24; HNG22].
The popular ImageNet dataset [Den+09] also presents a significant geographical bias, with 45% of its
data from the US and only 3% from China and India combined, a stark contrast to their share of the
global population, potentially creating cultural biases in trained models [ZS18].
Since bias often originates from the training data, the capability to selectively forget biased data offers
a potential advantage over the more cumbersome process of correcting the entire dataset and retraining
models from scratch.
==PAGE:20==
4
1 Introduction
1.2
Scope, Research Questions, and Contributions
As outlined in the preceding sections, the field of MU remains broad in scope, and this work therefore
focuses on one aspect: the privacy and regulatory motivations for unlearning.
To ground the discussion, we consider the following illustrative scenario: A team of ML researchers
has spent months developing and auditing a high-performing model trained on a large and diverse
dataset. With reviewers satisfied and the submission deadline approaching, everything appears to be
on track. Then comes the dreaded message: A data contributor invokes their right to be forgotten as
they apparently no longer identify as a red elephant. Chaos ensues. Even with full access to the original
data, retraining the model is computationally prohibitive. Yet the request is valid, and disregarding it
is neither legally nor ethically defensible.
This thesis is motivated by such situations where unlearning is not just a theoretical problem but a
practical requirement. We focus on the case of centralized learning, where a single model is trained
on all data, and assume full access to both the data to retain and the data to forget at the time of
unlearning. The methods we consider are approximate and model-agnostic, meaning they do not depend
on the original training algorithm and are applicable to the broad class of DNN. We also work at the
sample level, aiming to remove individual data points or sets of points rather than broader classes or
concepts. In this setting, where full retraining is expensive or infeasible, unlearning becomes a valuable
alternative worth exploring. Our study and evaluation will mainly be empirical in nature, as this is the
more practical approach, working with approximate unlearning, where theoretical guarantees are hard
to give.
An important requirement for MU is the ability to apply the process post hoc, meaning after a model
has already been trained. This is motivated by practical scenarios where the need for data removal was
not anticipated during the model’s development, e.g., the team of ML researchers did not anticipate a
request from a red elephant who does not identify as such any longer.
Our literature review in section 1.4 revealed two particularly compelling and practical paradigms for this
challenge. The first is the concept of selectively dampening parameters within the DNN, an approach
exemplified by methods like SSD [FSB24]. This technique is appealing as it conceptually resembles a
surgical intervention, aiming to remove the influence of specific, ”problematic” parameters. The second
paradigm involves a student-teacher knowledge distillation framework, such as SCRUB+R [Kur+23],
where a new model is fine-tuned using a carefully constructed objective to ensure the forgetting of
specific data while retaining overall knowledge.
While these methods operate at the parameter or objective-function level, a deeper understanding
of a model’s internal workings could enable more precise and reliable unlearning.
This motivates
our interest in the growing field of mechanistic interpretability, which seeks to reverse-engineer the
algorithms learned by neural networks. A central challenge in this field is polysemanticity, where a single
neuron can activate for multiple, unrelated features, complicating any attempt at surgical intervention.
We hypothesize that successfully addressing polysemanticity and achieving a more disentangled feature
representation could pave the way for novel and more effective unlearning techniques.
This leads us to the following three research questions:
RQ 1: What challenges and failure modes does parameter dampening in a neural network using SSD
present, and how can the method be improved to address them?
RQ 2: How does the existing fine-tuning objective in SCRUB+R perform in practice, and can a more
eﬀicient objective be developed for this unlearning paradigm?
RQ 3: How can insights from mechanistic interpretability, particularly regarding the challenge of pol-
ysemanticity in neural networks, inform the development of a novel unlearning approach?
To adequately answer these questions, we must also address the fundamental challenge of evaluation.
It is crucial to apply robust metrics that assess not only the completeness of forgetting but also the
preservation of model utility after unlearning. The ultimate goal is to achieve functional equivalence to
a model retrained from scratch without ever having been exposed to the forgotten data.
==PAGE:21==
1.3 Defining Machine Unlearning
5
Contributions: Based on our investigation into these questions, this thesis provides the following
contributions:
• We diagnose the practical failures of SSD and propose a novel method using BO to automate the
selection of its critical hyperparameters. While our BO framework did not conclusively outperform
the ASSD baseline in our experiments, we identify clear potential for future improvements with
a more refined optimization objective.
• We demonstrate a critical limitation of the SCRUB+R method, showing that its optimization
process is inherently unstable and converges toward catastrophic forgetting without a precise
early-stopping heuristic.
• In response to this instability in SCRUB+R, we propose TA, a novel fine-tuning objective that
builds on principles from EWC [Kir+17]. Our method demonstrates promising convergence prop-
erties, successfully forgetting target data while maintaining high performance on the data to
retain.
• We propose a novel unlearning method that leverages a Sparse Autoencoder (SAE) to extract
more disentangled, ”monosemantic” features from a network’s activations. Although this was a
later addition to our project and not as extensively analyzed, this approach allows for dampening
to occur in a more meaningful feature space, representing a promising direction for future research.
1.3
Defining Machine Unlearning
This section introduces the two main types of unlearning found in current literature. While our focus is
on approximate unlearning, we first need to cover the core concepts required to understand the broader
field.
The goal of MU can be distilled to removing the influence of undesired datapoints from a model. Let D
denote the original training dataset. The subset of data to be removed is referred to as the forget set,
Df, while the data we still want to retain is called the retain set, Dr = D\Df. Successful unlearning is
often determined by whether the unlearned model is indistinguishable from, or at least mimics, a model
retrained from scratch on the retain data, Dr.
The distinction between how precisely this removal is achieved leads us to two predominant approaches
within the field: Exact- and Approximate MU. These paradigms differ not only in methodology but also
in the trade-offs they present between computational eﬀiciency, model utility, and unlearning guarantees.
In the following sections, we outline the definitions of each approach.
1.3.1
Exact Unlearning
The strongest guarantee an unlearning procedure can offer is that of exact unlearning. As formalized
by Bourtoule et al. [Bou+21], the goal is to produce an unlearned model that is statistically indistin-
guishable from a model trained from scratch on the retained data. This implies that the unlearning
process must perfectly erase any influence of the forgotten data.
Because model training is stochastic in nature (due to random weight initialization, data shuffling, etc.),
training on the same dataset can produce a distribution of possible models. An unlearning procedure
is considered exact if the distribution of models it produces is identical to the distribution of models
produced by retraining. If we let p(θ|D) be the distribution of parameters that results from training
on a dataset D, then the condition for exact unlearning is:
p(θu|θorig, Df, Dr) = p(θr|Dr)
(1.1)
Here, the left-hand side represents the distribution of unlearned models, which is conditioned on the
original model and the data to forget Df. Note that we also condition it on the data to retain, Dr, as
some unlearning methods require access to both. More on this can be found in subsubsection 1.4.2.1.
==PAGE:22==
6
1 Introduction
The right-hand side represents the distribution of models retrained on the retain set. If these distri-
butions are identical, no statistical test on the model parameters can distinguish an unlearned model
from a retrained one.
This definition of exactness can be viewed from two primary perspectives:
• Model-based: The focus is on the model’s internal parameters.
The condition is that the
distribution of the unlearned parameters θu must be identical to the distribution of the retrained
parameters θr, as formalized in (1.1).
• Output-based: The focus shifts from the parameters to the model’s behavior. Here, the require-
ment is that for any given input, the distribution of outputs from an unlearned model must be
identical to that of a retrained model. Formally, for any input x:
p(Mθu(x)) = p(Mθr(x))
(1.2)
In summary, whether considering the internal model representation or its external predictions, exact
unlearning demands that the process of forgetting data leaves no detectable trace when compared to a
model that never saw that data in the first place.
To scope our project further we have chosen to look at the output-based definition of unlearning in the
more relaxed setting of approximate unlearning.
1.3.2
Approximate unlearning
The criteria for exact unlearning are highly restrictive. Currently, few methods suitable for deep neural
networks satisfy these criteria, as they typically impose significant constraints on the model architecture
or training process. Notable examples include SISA [Bou+21] and its variants [Yan+22].
For this reason, most literature focuses on the more relaxed definition of approximate unlearning. This
approach trades the perfect guarantees of exact unlearning for greater flexibility and eﬀiciency, making
it more applicable to complex models like deep neural networks.
One formal approach to approximate unlearning is ϵ-certified removal, introduced by Guo et al. [Guo+20].
The framework provides a probabilistic guarantee that an unlearned model is similar to a retrained one.
To align with the notation in this thesis, we present their core definition in a slightly simplified form.
Let θorig be the parameters of a model trained on a dataset D. The goal is to remove the influence of a
data point x ∈D. An unlearning procedure results in a new set of parameters, θu. The benchmark for
this is a model retrained from scratch on the dataset D \ {x}, which results in parameters θr. Because
training and unlearning algorithms can be stochastic, there is a distribution of possible outcomes for
both θu and θr.
An unlearning procedure is said to satisfy ϵ-certified removal if for any possible set of resulting model
parameters T , the following inequality holds:
e−ϵ ≤p(θu ∈T )
p(θr ∈T ) ≤eϵ
(1.3)
In simple terms, this equation means that the probability of the unlearning process producing a model
within any given set of outcomes T is nearly identical to the probability of the retraining process doing
so. The two processes produce statistically similar results, making it diﬀicult to tell whether a model
was produced by unlearning or by retraining from scratch. However, this formal definition is typically
constrained to the removal of a single data point x. As noted by Nguyen et al. [Ngu+22], extending
the definition or determining ϵ for larger forget sets remains an open research question.
A more pragmatic approach is to evaluate unlearning empirically. This involves comparing the behavior
of the unlearned model, Mθu, and the retrained model, Mθr. Common evaluation metrics include
model accuracy on the retain set (Dr), forget set (Df), and a validation set (Dval), as well as measures
of disagreement or distance between the models’ output distributions. While this empirical path does
not provide formal guarantees, it enables the development and practical use of a much wider range of
unlearning methods. It is this paradigm of empirically validated unlearning that forms the focus of this
thesis.
==PAGE:23==
1.4 Literature review
7
1.4
Literature review
With a clear motivation for machine unlearning and the field still in its early stages, several research
directions have begun to emerge. Our literature review will be based on the scenario outlined in the
scope in section 1.2. Although privacy and regulation are the primary practical motivations, there exist
a wide range of methods capable of addressing these challenges, each suited to different unlearning
scenarios.
For one, authors distinguish between guarantees on whether unlearning has taken place which we refer
to as unlearning completeness, aligning with definition of exact and approximate unlearning provided
above. Authors also consider different scenarios both regarding the information we have access to at
the time of unlearning as well as the nature of the data that we wish to forget. We broadly refer to
this as the unlearning setting. Some unlearning methods are also tied to specific model architectures
whereas others can be applied on a large class of model types. As defined in the scope of the project,
we will primarily consider model-agnostic approaches.
The following sections presents our review and categorization of the various branches of MU found in
existing literature, structured under a unified taxonomy. This overview is guided by the taxonomy
illustrated in Figure 1.2 where we also highlight our scope and focus. The following subsections should
thus be read as a summary or introduction of each branch within MU while we will only dive deeper
into some of the branches and methods presented. It has to be mentioned that this is a simplified view
of MU and many models do not necessarily fit into a single category.
Honourable Mentions: In Figure 1.2 we have honourably mentioned Differential Privacy and Output
Suppression. While these help control information leakage and may support regulatory compliance, they
do not constitute machine unlearning under our definition, as they obfuscate or restrict output rather
than remove data influence post-training. We therefore leave further exploration of these approaches
to the reader.
1.4.1
Unlearning completeness
The most popular categorization within machine unlearning concerns completeness of unlearning. Un-
learning completeness pertains to how much of the influence of the forget set is remaining after un-
learning. While this thesis employs approximate unlearning, understanding the trade-off between the
completeness of unlearning is essential.
Exact MU refers to provably removing all information in a forget set from a model. Approaches
include full retraining which is often considered the gold standard comparison method [CY15], data
partitioning methods to retrain more eﬀiciently [Bou+21; AMB21; Yan+22; Duk+23]. Other exact
unlearning approaches include summation-based methods that exploit the properties of Statistical Query
(SQ) learning [Fel08]. Here the learning process is structured as a summation form, enabling eﬀicient
unlearning by subtracting the contribution of the forgotten data [CY15]. Similarly, K-Means clustering
can facilitate exact unlearning due to its reliance on summations [Gin+19]. However, these methods are
model-intrinsic, meaning they are limited to specific models or types of models [Ngu+22], and they are
non-adaptive, requiring predefined structures rather than dynamically adjusting to unlearning requests
[Men+22; Ma+23].
Exact unlearning typically assumes that unlearning needs are anticipated and integrated during model
design. Since this is not true in our scenario, we instead turn to approximate methods that accommodate
post hoc unlearning by relaxing such assumptions.
Approximate MU describes methods which seek to unlearn the forget set but cannot give formal
proof that all information is removed. Some methods can provide direct bounds on the degree of un-
learning whereas others rely on evaluation metrics to assess unlearning effectiveness. Approximate MU
approaches include gradient-based adjustments such as influence functions to determine the influence
of a training point to forget on the model and remove this influence. Amnesiac unlearning [GNG21] is
another approach that rolls back gradient updates made with forget observations. The space complexity
of this method becomes noticeable when dealing with large models trained on massive datasets.
Another method focuses on learning and injecting an error-maximizing noise for a specific class that
we want to forget and subsequently repairing on the retain dataset [Tar+24]. [Zha+22] extends this

approach to specific samples in the context of image retrieval. We also see a branch of approximate MU
employing influence functions to estimate the ’importance’ of datapoints to a model, much in line with
how Foster, et al. [FSB24] utilises the FIM [War+23]. The idea is to find the effect of small changes
in inputs on the parameters of the model, and use these effects for controlled updates to the model to
negate the influence of specific inputs [War+23].
Honourable mentions: Variational Bayesian Unlearning [NLJ20] and Contrastive Finetuning [Kur+23;
Zha+22]
As outlined in the scope of this thesis, we consider scenarios where models have already been trained
and retraining is prohibitively costly, such as the case of the researchers confronted with a late-stage
data removal request. In this setting, the rigid constraints of exact unlearning leave little practical
room for action.
By contrast, relaxing these constraints allows us to explore more flexible, theory-
driven approaches. While the distinction between exact and approximate unlearning is foundational,
the empirical, post hoc nature of our problem makes approximate methods a more viable and relevant
choice. Consequently, our work focuses on developing and evaluating such approximate techniques.
1.4.2
Unlearning setting
With unlearning completeness defined, machine unlearning is studied under varying assumptions about
data availability and the level of granularity required. To clarify these variations, we divide the unlearn-
ing setting into two key dimensions:
==PAGE:25==
1.4 Literature review
9
• Access: What portions of the dataset are available at unlearning time?
• Data granularity: What is the scope of the unlearning request: individual samples, entire
classes, or broader data segments?
These dimensions are critical to specify when designing or evaluating an unlearning method. While we
outline the predominant settings found in the literature, this work specifically assumes full access to
both retained and forgotten data, and targets unlearning at the granularity of individual data points.
1.4.2.1
Data Access
We distinguish between several scenarios when considering access. Full access where the unlearning
algorithm has access to all the data, zero-glance where we only have access to the retain data, zero-shot
unlearning where there is not access to any of the data, and few shot unlearning where we have access
to a representative sample of the forget set.
In Table 1.1 and Figure 1.3 we provide a summary of the settings we explore.
Scenarios
Access to Forget Set
Access to Retain Set
Use Case
Full Access
Yes
Yes
Full data access for most eﬀicient unlearning

No
Yes
Privacy-preserving unlearning (e.g., facial recognition)
Zero-Shot
No
No
Secure environments with no data access
Few-Shot
Partial
Yes
Limited data access (e.g., data cleanup)
Table 1.1: Comparison of Unlearning Scenarios
Full access unlearning: The traditional approach within MU is to have access to all the data, both
the retain and forget set of data. Approaches include selective synaptic dampening [FSB24], as we
have to calculate the Fisher Information Matrix (FIM) for both the full dataset and forget set1, SISA
[Bou+21] as we need to be able to locate the forget datapoints, and SCRUB+R [GAS20b] as the
optimization objective when fine-tuning the model toward an unlearned model requires both the retain
and forget set [Kur+23]. AU also requires access to all the data, as we need to know which stored
gradients correspond to the data in the deletion request [GNG21].
Zero-glance unlearning (only retain set): One could imagine a setup where the forget set is
deleted, and then we later have to remove its influence from the ML model. In other words, we do not
have access to the actual forget set at the time of unlearning. This is described in [Tar+24], where
they propose a method called Unlearning by Selective Impair and Repair (UNSIR). The setting here

10
1 Introduction
is somewhat relaxed, as they have access to the class to forget [Ngu+22]. It works by impairing the
model through a learned error-maximizing noise for the forget class, which is then used, together with
a subset of the retain set, to fine-tune the model. Following this, a repair phase is performed where
the model is fine-tuned on a subset of the retain data to mitigate any catastrophic forgetting that may
have occurred as a result of the impair phase. In [BSZ22], they also consider a multi-class classification
problem where they argue that the model can be decomposed into a feature extractor, f : X →Rp and
linear transformation W ∈Rk×p, i.e. Rp →Rk, and then the logits are calculated as h(x) = Wf(x).
The idea is then to calculate a filtration matrix, Fz that can be absorbed into W to create and updated
transformation matrix, Wz = FzW One could argue that this is more of an output suppression approach,
as we filter W and keep the feature extraction part the same. So, similarly to [Tar+24], it operates on
the class level and does not need access to the actual forget set but rather the classes to forget.
Zero-shot unlearning (no access) In the Zero-shot unlearning setting, we do not have access to
any of the training data, neither the retain nor the forget set [Ngu+22]. This could be useful when
data access is limited or it costs money each time it is accessed, as is the case for many cloud services
[Chu+23a]. Chundawat et al. [Chu+23a] presents two techniques for unlearning specific classes from
a model without needing access to the original training data, only the class identities. Their ”Gated
Knowledge Transfer” approach utilizes a teacher-student paradigm.
The pre-trained model acts as
the fixed teacher, and a student model with identical architecture starts from random initialization.
Following Micaelli and Storkey [MS19], a generator creates informative pseudo-samples by maximizing
the teacher-student KL-divergence. Crucially, a ”band-pass filter” gates this process, blocking pseudo-
samples related to the forget classes from influencing the student’s training.
Few-shot unlearning (representative sample of forget set) Few-shot unlearning addresses the
practical challenge of removing a model’s knowledge of specific data when only a small sample of that
data is available. In this scenario, the unlearning algorithm must generalize from just a few examples
to remove the influence of all related data that should be forgotten [Yoo+22]. Few-shot unlearning is
valuable when complete access to all target data is impossible. Privacy regulations may restrict access
to data that needs removal, information may be scattered across different jurisdictions with varying
legal requirements, and gathering all instances can be costly and complex. This approach eﬀiciently
removes knowledge from models using just a few samples. This is especially useful in cases where we
wish to correct a model and do not necessarily have access to all the related samples.
In [Yoo+22], they propose a model-inversion approach, which is a type of model attack seeking to
generate samples that resemble the training data [FJR15; Fan+24; Zho+24]. The generated samples
are then partitioned into target-like samples similar to the subset of forget samples and retain-like
samples. Depending on the intention of the unlearning, e.g., privacy or mislabel corrections, they then
use these generated samples to either fine-tune on the retain samples or scrub the knowledge of the
forget samples by training on this data and applying random labels to ”confuse the model” [Yoo+22].
In line with the scope of this thesis, we adopt the full access setting, assuming that both the retain
and forget sets are available at the time of unlearning. This reflects the situation in our illustrative
example, where the researchers have access to the full training data, including the sample to be removed,
but retraining the model is no longer feasible. The full access assumption is also consistent with many
existing unlearning methods that require access to data for model updates or gradient estimation. While
alternative settings such as zero-shot or few-shot unlearning raise important and practical challenges,
they introduce additional constraints that lie beyond the focus of this work.
1.4.2.2
Unlearning Target
A key distinction among unlearning methods is the granularity of the unlearning target, i.e, what the
method aims to forget. While this project focuses on unlearning at the sample level, we also discuss
alternative targets that may arise in broader unlearning scenarios.
Table 1.2 outlines the different
unlearning targets that have been considered in the literature, while Figure 1.4 tries to give a visual
overview.
Samples: Most unlearning literature focuses on unlearning a randomly drawn subset of the training
data. While some authors [Gup+21] argue that unlearning requests are typically motivated by model
behavior, making the forget set model dependent.
==PAGE:27==
1.4 Literature review
11
Unlearning Target
Description and Use Case
Samples
Removing specific data points from the model.
Commonly used when a user
requests their data to be deleted (e.g., GDPR compliance).
Classes
Removing a whole category or label from the model.
For example, removing a
specific person’s face from a facial recognition model.
Features
Eliminating a specific attribute or characteristic (e.g., race, gender) from the model
to mitigate bias or comply with privacy regulations.
Tasks
Removing knowledge related to a complete task, useful in multi-task or lifelong
learning settings (e.g., unlearning a specific skill in a robotics system).
Streams
Forgetting data that comes from a continuous or streaming source. Suitable for
online learning systems where data arrives and needs to be deleted periodically.

Figure 1.4:
Simple figure giving an overview of the five different unlearning targets: Class level,
sample level, feature level, task level and stream level unlearning. It should be noted, that feature level
attempts to visualise the specific datapoints that carry data about a feature, such as the feature coding
for detecting ’smiling’ faces in the data. It is an overly simplified illustration.
Classes: Some parts of the literature [Tar+24] focus exclusively on forgetting entire classes. That
being said, class unlearning is typically used as a ”sanity check” in the context of an unlearning method
[FSB24; GNG21]. An example from [GNG21]: Even though the method is sample-based, they unlearn
all the samples in a class (3s in MNIST [YCC98]) to verify how well the model ”forgets”.
Features: As opposed to unlearning specific samples or classes, one could also be interested in unlearn-
ing features or concepts. This could be useful if one is to correct outdated information and mitigate
biases. A common approach in this context is to define an intermediary step converting the abstract
concept to be forgotten into specific data points and subsequently applying tools from sample-based
unlearning.
Another approach is that of [Guo+22] where the idea is to split their model into two main parts: One
that produces an intermediate representation which is used by a small auxiliary network to approximate
the mutual information between the representation and the target concept to unlearn and the mutual
information between the representation and the other concepts to retain. These are then used in a
contrastive loss function, which they call detachment loss to fine-tune the model towards forgetting the
concept. The second part of the network is a classifier that takes this intermediate representation as
input. See their figure 2 for a detailed explanation [Guo+22]. Using this approach, they can unlearn
concepts on the CelebA dataset, such as the concept of smiling, big noses, etc. Another approach to
concept unlearning is unlearning in diffusion models [Wu+25; Zha+24], where approaches have been
made to unlearn concepts such as Mickey Mouse or the style of Van Gogh. The idea here is to modify
the parameters of the diffusion model to suppress the generation of samples exhibiting the targeted
concept.
Tasks: While classes and features are more conventional unlearning targets, part of the literature
==PAGE:28==
12
1 Introduction
also abstracts these into tasks. These can vary in complexity but are generally regarded as domain-
specific objectives. Intuitive examples are stop sign detection or robot assistants making the bed. Task
unlearning is generally considered in Continual Learning, which has proven useful for mitigating ’cold
start’ and overcoming sparsity problems, where there is not enough data present for the model to
learn the task alone [Ngu+22; LLS22]. As with most other targets, unlearning in continual learning
seeks to remove the capabilities of the model to perform some specific tasks. One prominent issue for
the applicability of unlearning in CL is the potential downstream domino effect that unlearning could
inflict, as CL could be seen as a markov chain. It would hence necessitate the model to incorporate the
potential of links in its chain to be potentially removed at one point [LLS22].
An interesting mention, that is not directly unlearning, but touches upon continual learning, is EWC
proposed by Kirkpatrick et al. [Kir+17]. It seeks to protect the performance of a task A while learning
a new task B, thus preventing catastrophic forgetting.
Streams: Specific parts of the literature also consider the problem of handling streamed forgetting.
Here, the problem is phrased as an online learning problem where the unlearning algorithm receives a
sequential stream of update requests [Gup+21]. The objective in this unlearning setting is eﬀiciency
and a low cost per unlearning request. Examples include model intrinsic unlearning methods such as
k-means unlearning [Gin+19].
Honourable Mentions: Continual learning [LLS22], Concept unlearning [Wu+25; Zha+24] and in-
fluence functions [Wae+25]
Reflecting the illustrative example introduced in the scope, we focus on sample-level unlearning,
where the goal is to remove the influence of a specific data point, such as the red elephant, from a fully
trained model. This setting aligns with practical situations where individual deletion requests arise after
training. While the literature also explores broader targets like class-, feature-, stream-, or task-level
unlearning, these lie beyond the scope of this work and the challenges defined by our scenario.
1.4.3
Learning paradigm
Another key distinction among unlearning methods in the literature concerns the underlying learning
paradigm. Broadly, these methods fall into two categories: centralized learning and federated learning.
In our scoped case example, the researchers operate within a centralized setting, where a single model
is trained on a complete dataset. To provide context for this choice, we briefly outline the differences
between centralized and federated learning, as they have important implications for how unlearning can
be applied. The distinction between federated and centralized learning is illustrated in Figure 1.5.
Federated learning is a paradigm, first introduced by [McM+17], where a model is trained across
several devices or clients and coordinated by a central service provider. This means that data is not
shared across different devices; only the model parameters are shared with the central server [Rom+24].
Apart from the settings described in subsection 1.4.2, another goal of federated unlearning could be to
unlearn a whole client from the global model, removing their contribution. Approaches include knowl-
edge distillation techniques [WZM22; Ye+23; ALM24], gradient modifications [Hal+22], recalibration
Federated Learning
Centralised Learning
Global model
Client models
Figure 1.5: A simple illustration showing the core difference between centralized and federated learn-
ing.
==PAGE:29==
1.4 Literature review
13
of the global model using historical updates which are stored during training [Liu+21]. Some additional
challenges to consider for federated unlearning are where the unlearning should take place, whether it
happens client-side or on a global level [Rom+24].
Centralized learning is when the data is stored at a central location and training of the model happens
at this same location. This is also referred to as classical machine learning, and all the methods described
in subsection 1.4.1 are of this type. We will solely focus on classical or centralized machine learning
methods and will not go further into details with federated unlearning, other than recognizing that it
is a significant research area within MU.
While federated unlearning introduces unique challenges, such as client-level forgetting and decentralized
coordination, these lie outside the scope of this work. As established in the illustrative example, our
work considers a centralized learning setting, where all data is available in one location and the model
is trained accordingly. This choice simplifies the unlearning process by removing concerns related to
distributed data.
1.4.4
Evaluation of unlearning
A key aspect of MU is evaluating the methods.
In our illustrative scenario, the researchers must
ensure the model forgets the target data while maintaining performance on retained data, without
full retraining. This process differs based on whether we are working with an exact or approximate
unlearning method. While exact unlearning methods provably remove the influence of the forget set,
our focus on approximate unlearning shifts the goal to empirically assessing whether forgetting has
occurred and whether model utility is preserved. An additional concern is the risk of cumulative model
degradation from sequential or large forget requests, particularly for influence-based methods, due to
overlapping parameter importance and limited scalability.
This section provides an overview of key categories and representative methods used in the current
literature. While not exhaustive, it introduces the main evaluation metrics employed throughout the
literature.
1.4.4.1
Attack-based
Membership inference attacks A common approach to evaluating unlearning is membership infer-
ence attacks (MIA). This paradigm optimizes a surrogate model to assess whether a datapoint was used
to train another model or not. In the context of MU, the probability, under the MIA model, that the
unlearned model was trained on the forget set should match that of a retrained model [Sho+17]. In
most practical settings, the membership inference probability of the forget set for the original model is
higher than that of a retrained model.
Model inversion attacks Model inversions refer to learning a mapping from output space to input
space with respect to a target model. Hence, the goal is to learn to reconstruct the training data of
the target model based on its outputs and parameters. Constructing an inverse model is non-trivial for
most complex architectures, and therefore it is not as widespread in the context of unlearning evaluation
[Zho+24].
1.4.4.2
Eﬀiciency
Machine Unlearning is motivated by the premise that forgetting can be more eﬀicient than full retraining.
Eﬀiciency is typically measured by runtime [Guo+20] or theoretical speedup [Bou+21]. However, a
trade-off exists: stronger unlearning guarantees often incur higher computational costs. Eﬀiciency also
varies with request patterns; batch or sequential deletions can approach retraining costs, especially in
methods like SISA or amnesiac unlearning [GNG21].
1.4.4.3
Relearn time
Some papers evaluate their methods based on the eﬀiciency in maximising the difference in computing
time between retraining and applying their method. The relearn time can be considered as the number
of epochs it takes an unlearned model being trained on the forget set to reach a loss within a threshold
==PAGE:30==
14
1 Introduction
of the original unlearned model [Xu+24]. Hence, the relearn time is a metric that should be maximised,
and interpreted as the time it takes to revert the unlearning [GAS20a; Ngu+22]. This evaluation metric
can be diﬀicult to compare across methods as the scale and properties vary. Hence, a variation of relearn
time, the Anamesis Index (AIN), was proposed.
Anamnesis Index First introduced in [Chu+23a], the AIN is defined as:
AIN = rt
 Mθu, Mθorig, α

rt
 Mθr, Mθorig, α
 ,
α ∈R
With AIN ∈[0 : ∞[. Here rt denotes the Relearn Time and is computed with regards to the unlearned
model, Mθu, the original model Mθorig, the model retrained, Mθr. The hyperparameter α determines
the %-wise accepted margin of difference in model accuracy of both Mθu and Mθr compared to the
original model. Hence, a high α allows for the models to be considered relearned quicker, possibly
rendering the metric misleading. Chundawat et al. [Chu+23a] suggests the value to be between 5-10%.
With this definition, it follows that the optimal value is AIN = 1 since Mθu would be as ’slow’ to
relearn forgotten datapoints as a model trained from scratch. Given the versatility of this metric, we
see it employed in several papers [GAS20a; GAS20b; Tar+24].
1.4.4.4
Information bound
Some unlearning methods employ information-theoretic approaches to assess the extent to which the
model retains information about forgotten data. Formally, information bound evaluation aims to esti-
mate an upper bound on the residual information of unlearned data within the updated model. However,
in the literature, the notion of ”information” is often ambiguous. It can refer to either information-
theoretic concepts or to leakage measured through attacks, such as Membership Inference Attacks
(MIAs) [GAS20b]. We categorize the latter under attack-based evaluations (as discussed in subsec-
tion 1.4.4) and focus here on metrics grounded in information theory.
Golatkar et al.
[GAS20a]
introduced a post-hoc weight scrubbing technique for estimating an upper bound on residual informa-
tion. Given a converged model Mθ|D, they perturb the weights θ with Gaussian noise to move the
model away from Mθ|Df , while minimizing the KL divergence between Mθ|D and the scrubbed model
Mθ|Dr. The resulting KL divergence serves as an upper bound on residual information. This approach
assumes white-box access, which is uncommon in practice. To address black-box scenarios, Golatkar et
al. [GAS20b] later proposed a method using Neural Tangent Kernels (NTK) that enables “one-shot”
unlearning with minimal noise injection. They also suggest evaluating unlearning effectiveness by com-
paring entropy across model outputs-specifically between the original, retrained, and unlearned models.
This is used to detect the Streisand Effect2, where attempts to remove information inadvertently amplify
it.
1.4.4.5
Empirically Based
In case formal proof or bounds on whether unlearning has taken place cannot be provided, one has to
resort to evaluating it empirically. The vast majority of metrics require access to a retrained model.
Most prior work compute the accuracy of the unlearned model on the retain and forget data and
compare the results to those obtained when retraining.
Other methods utilise distributional comparison methods such as the Jensen-Shannon divergence (JS-
Divergence), that in contrast to the KL-Divergence between parameter distributions, measures the
distance between outputs distributions.
As we have seen, there are numerous ways to evaluate the effectiveness of unlearning methods. However,
for the researchers in our scoped example, who need a practical and timely way to verify that a specific
data point has been forgotten, empirical and attack-based evaluations are the most relevant. Accord-
ingly, this thesis focuses primarily on empirical evaluation metrics detailed in the theory chapter 3.
2https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Streisand_effect
==PAGE:31==
1.5 Methods overview
15
1.5
Methods overview
Having outlined the broader landscape of machine unlearning, we now narrow our focus to the specific
goals defined in the scope. While the preceding literature review was not exhaustive, it captures the
main trends in the field as of the writing of this.
Referring to our research questions presented in section 1.2, we focus on investigating and improving
specific unlearning methods. Specifically, we focus on evaluating and extending SSD and SCRUB+R.
Also, grounded in the hypothesis that SSD will fail when applied to polysemantic neurons, we experiment
with addressing this scenario using a SAE. To provide the reader with intuition about how these methods
function, we present the core ideas in the following section. Here, we also include the methods SISA
and AU, which both served as benchmarks due to their widespread use in MU. A rigorous treatment
of the aforementioned unlearning methods can be found in chapter 3.
1.5.1
SSD
The SSD method [FSB24] is an eﬀicient approximate MU method. The core idea behind this approach
is to compare how much information each parameter in a model contains about the forget and retain
data. If a parameter contains substantially more information about the forget data compared to the
retain data, then that parameter likely encodes highly specialized information about the forget data
that a fully retrained model would not be able to generalize to.
An extension to this method called Adaptive SSD (ASSD) [SFB24], was later discovered and provides
improvements to hyperparameter selection.
Information retained in a parameter with respect to a dataset is quantified using the Fisher Information
Matrix (FIM). The FIM is formally defined as the covariance of the score function evaluated at the
true parameters. Since this quantity is not attainable in practice, the empirical FIM is calculated as a
Monte Carlo sample over the dataset. Additionally, the diagonal of the FIM is used since this can be
eﬀiciently obtained from first-order derivatives alone.
1.5.2
SAE
We postulate that the SSD method will fail in case that the model on which it is applied has polysemantic
neurons [Elh+22].
As a result, we experiment with addressing this in part using SAE’s.
The key
idea behind SAE is to learn a monosemantic representation of a model’s activations in hidden layers.
To achieve this, an overcomplete dictionary is learned to reconstruct model activations. Additionally,
sparsity is encouraged such that the SAE should reconstruct the model activations using as few features
as possible.
1.5.3
SISA
SISA [Bou+21] is an exact unlearning method that follows a federated learning paradigm. As such,
several client models are trained on disjoint subsets of the training data, referred to as shards. Naturally,
retraining a client model on the retain data in its corresponding shard is more eﬀicient than retraining
a single model trained on the global retain set. The eﬀiciency can be further improved by creating
disjoint subsets within each shard, slice. When training a client model, it is first optimized for a fixed
number of epochs on the first slice and checkpointed. Hereafter, training is continued on the union of
the first and second slice, followed by checkpointing. This continues until the union of slices is equal to
the entire shard. In [Bou+21], the number of gradient steps to take at a given slice is determined by
the number of datapoints present in the union of slices at that time.
After training each client model, inference is performed by aggregating the predictions across client
models. The authors propose both majority voting and a weighted majority vote. The latter refers
to majority voting, where models are weighed higher if they are more confident based on their output
probability distributions.
When receiving an unlearning request, one simply has to locate the shards in which forget observations
fall and retrain the client models starting at the appropriate slice checkpoint.
==PAGE:32==
16
1 Introduction
1.5.4
Amnesiac Unlearning
Amnesiac unlearning [GNG21] is an approximate unlearning method that falls under a centralized
learning paradigm. The method works by storing the gradients associated with each step of model
training along with the corresponding batch of observations. Upon receiving an unlearning request, the
gradients associated with forget observations are located and subtracted from the model weights.
Since the gradient at a given time of model training is dependent on all preceding gradient steps,
information about forget samples will still be present in the model after applying unlearning. It should
be noted that in case the loss function is convex, amnesiac unlearning becomes an exact unlearning
method; however, this will generally not be true for deep models. As such, there is an inherent trade-
off in amnesiac unlearning between the batch size and degree of catastrophic forgetting. As such, the
authors propose to add a repair step after rolling back gradients where the unlearned model is fine-tuned
on the retain data.
1.5.5
Scrub+R
Scrub+R [Kur+23] is another approximate MU method. It uses a student-teacher scheme with the
student being the unlearned model and the teacher as the original model trained on all data, with its
parameters held frozen. The core idea is to optimize a contrastive objective. One term in the loss
function favors models whose output probability distribution on the forget set is as distinct from the
original model as possible. The other terms in the objective mitigate catastrophic forgetting on the
retain data.
One term fits the retain labels and the other favors models whose output probability
distribution on retain labels is as close to the original model’s as possible. These conflicting objectives
are updated in an alternating fashion akin to adversarial learning. After each epoch of minimization
and maximization, the resulting model’s weights are saved to a temporary file.
To avoid the Streisand effect, the authors propose a rewind phase. After optimizing the contrastive
objective, the authors construct a rewind dataset, sampled from the validation dataset, that is as close
in label distribution to the forget set as possible. The error on the rewind set is then calculated using
the loss function of the original model. The unlearned model is then chosen as the model whose error
on the forget set is as close as the error of the final model on the repair set as possible.
==PAGE:33==
CHAPTER 2

2.1
Synthetic: Rogue points dataset
Some of our initial experiments investigate how different unlearning methods affect the decision bound-
aries of the model under various unlearning scenarios. These scenarios are constructed by varying the
location of the forget set relative to observations from its original class. Specifically, we consider the
following settings:
Case 1: Forget set located near a decision boundary between two classes.
Case 2: Forget set located in a high-density region of the correct class.
Case 3: Forget set located in a high-density region of an incorrect class.
Case 4: Forget set located behind the correct class.
Case 5: Forget set located behind an incorrect class.
We refer to these relocated data points as ”rogue points” and distinguish our experiments as ”rogue
one” (for a single point) and ”rogue many” (for a cluster of points).

Dataset creation

dimensions. Each cluster i ∈0, 1, 2 was generated from a bivariate normal distribution as follows: The

2.2
Benchmark Datasets
To go beyond the qualitative assessment of the unlearning methods in controlled settings, we will test
them on two common benchmark datasets, [YCC98] and CIFAR-10 [Kri09].
2.2.1
MNIST
The widely popular MNIST dataset [YCC98] consists of handwritten digits from 0 to 9. It is is divided
into a training set of 60,000 images and a test set of 10,000 images. Each image is a 28 × 28 grayscale
representation of a single digit. For our unlearning experiments, we will only use 10,000 of the training
images. To construct the forget set Df, we draw a random sample from the training set. We will keep
the 10,000 images in the test dataset as our validation dataset, which will be used to validate model
performance on unseen data and also used to rewind to the best unlearned model in the SCRUB+R
method presented in subsection 3.3.3. An example of some of the images from the dataset can be seen
in Figure 2.2.

Figure 2.2: Samples from the MNIST dataset.
The preprocessing of the MNIST images involves three main steps. First, each 28 × 28 pixel image
is flattened into a 784-dimensional vector. Next, the pixel values of both the training and test sets
are normalized using the maximum and minimum pixel values found in the training data1. This is a
min-max normalization that scales the pixel values to a range of [−1, 0]. For each pixel x, the new value
x′ is calculated as:
x′ =
x −xmax
xmax −xmin
Finally, the integer labels for each digit (from 0 to 9) are transformed into 10-dimensional one-hot
encoded vectors.
2.2.1.1
Similarity preserving dimensionality reduction and qualitative forget set selection
A randomly sampled forget set is assumed to follow the same distribution as the retain set.
This
assumption is grounded in statistical theory, where the Law of Large Numbers2 supports the represen-
tativeness of sample averages, and the Glivenko-Cantelli theorem3 provides the stronger guarantee that
the sample’s entire distribution function converges to that of the population from which it was drawn.
To create a targeted forget set, our method relies on a qualitative visual selection process. First, we
generate a two-dimensional embedding of the dataset using t-SNE [MH08]. Let the original dataset
be D = {(xi, yi)}N
i=1 where each xi ∈Rd. The t-SNE function, ftsne : Rd →R2, maps each high-
dimensional point xi to a two-dimensional coordinate xi = ftsne(xi), producing a meaningful visual-
ization. Following this, we perform a visual inspection of the resulting 2D map and manually define a
rectangular boundary, which we term the forget set box, around a specific region of interest. This box,
B, is defined by its coordinate boundaries: B = {(z1, z2)|z1,min ≤z1 ≤z1,max, z2,min ≤z2 ≤z2,max}.
1We realised late in the project that we accidentally subtract the max value as opposed to the min value in the
numerator. We still achieve representative results for MNIST and therefore decided not to correct this.
2See: https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Law_of_large_numbers
3See: https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glivenko–Cantelli_theorem
==PAGE:36==
20
2 Data
The dataset is then partitioned based on this box: The forget set is composed of all samples whose 2D
representations fall inside the boundary, i.e., Df = {(xi, yi) ∈D | ftsne(xi) ∈B}, while the retain set
consists of all remaining samples outside of it, i.e., Dr = D \ Df. This approach allows us to curate a
forget set based on shared visual characteristics in the embedded space, as illustrated in Table 2.1. We
have created three different forget sets trying to create some distinct scenarios:
1. Forget set is located in a region where there is a big overlap between classes 4 and 9 suggesting
they share many of the same properties and might be harder to distinguish.
2. Forget set is located between numerous classes and has a very diverse class distribution.
3. Forget set is located at the edge of the cluster for class 6 with only a single outlier sample of class
4.

CIFAR-10
The CIFAR-10 dataset [Kri09] is a standard benchmark for evaluating image classification models. It
consists of 60,000 RGB images of size 32 × 32, evenly distributed across 10 classes.
These include

For our experiments, we use all 50,000 datapoints when training the original model. The forget set, Df,
consists of 100 randomly sampled images from the training set. Due to the limited scope of the thesis,
we do not consider targeted forget sets when experimenting on CIFAR-10. The full CIFAR-10 test
set of 10,000 images is used for validation when assessing model generalization and performing model
selection during rewind-based unlearning procedures (see subsection 3.3.3).
2.2.2.1
Preprocessing
Prior to training, several preprocessing steps are performed which we describe in the order of which
they are applied below. The pixels in each image are divided by 255 converting them to lie in the range
[0, 1]. Hereafter, we pre-compute the channel-wise mean and standard deviation over the entire training
set, denoted µ, σ ∈R3, which are later used for normalization. Following this, each image is resized
to 224 × 224 to match the input resolution expected by the pre-trained vision transformer used in our
experiments. This is done using bilinear interpolation.
When fine-tuning the original and retrained models, we apply image augmentation to improve gener-
alizability. Specifically, we use the AutoAugment policy for CIFAR-10 described in [Cub+19]. After
augmentation, each image is normalized using the channel means and standard deviations computed
earlier. Specifically, each image X ∈R224×224×3 is transformed as:
xi,j,c = xi,j,c −µc
σc
(2.2)
It should be noted that augmentations are only applied during fine-tuning and not at unlearning time.
Lastly, all labels are converted to 10-dimensional one-hot encoded vectors.

CHAPTER 3
Theory
This chapter outlines the theory surrounding the models and unlearning methods we consider. We
begin by covering the Feed-Forward Neural Network (FFNN), the primary model type we focus on
in this thesis. The ViT is also presented briefly, since this architecture is used when experimenting
on CIFAR-10. Background surrounding the interpretability of neural networks is also covered because
it is a prerequisite for understanding mechanistic interpretability and polysemanticity.
The Sparse
Autoencoder (SAE) and background knowledge on sparse coding and autoencoders are outlined to
show how polysemanticity is addressed in current literature and contextualize it.
Following this, we present the baseline unlearning methods covered in section 1.5 in depth. We then
present the tuning and optimization theory that underpins our improvements to SSD and informs the
design of TA (section 3.4).
Lastly, we describe the theory behind the selected evaluation metrics used for evaluating unlearning
(section 3.5).
3.1
Neural networks
3.1.1
Feed Forward Neural Networks
A Feed-Forward Neural Network (FFNN) or Multilayer perceptron (MLP) is a foundational deep learn-
ing architecture composed of an input layer, hidden layers, and an output layer, forming a complex
mapping from inputs to outputs [GBC16, ch. 6]. This thesis focuses on multi-class classification, where
the model maps an input x ∈Rd(0) to one of C class labels.
We define our model as a parameterized function Mθ : Rd(0) →RC, which is a composition of L
layer-wise functions:

3.1.2
Interpretability of Neural Networks
While the previous section detailed the architecture of the FFNN, its ”black box” nature likely poses a
challenge for precise model modification. Interventions, such as neuron dampening, are likely insuﬀicient
as they operate without addressing the underlying complexity of what individual neurons represent.
To address this, the following section introduces mechanistic interpretability, a paradigm focused on
reverse engineering the learned representations within a model.
A critical challenge within this field is polysemanticity, where networks encode features in a complex and
tangled manner, as described by the superposition hypothesis. To deconstruct these representations,
we will first introduce the concept of autoencoding (subsection 3.2.2) before detailing how Sparse Au-
toencoders (SAEs) are used as a powerful tool within mechanistic interpretability to isolate meaningful
features (subsection 3.2.3).
3.1.2.1
Mechanistic Interpretability
Interpretability within AI and machine learning is about understanding models in a way that humans
can understand [Mad+24]. According to Kim, Koyejo, and Khanna [KKK16] ”a method is interpretable
if a user can correctly and eﬀiciently predict the method’s results”. Another definition by Biran and
Cotton [BC17] says that ”systems are interpretable if their operations can be understood by a human”.
Interpretability is often divided into different paradigms: Behavioral interpretability examines how
models respond to inputs without accessing internal mechanisms. Attributional interpretability
traces predictions back to specific input features to identify what influenced the output, often through
the study of the gradients. Concept-based interpretability identifies high-level abstractions and
patterns within model representations, enabling the understanding and manipulation of emergent con-
cepts [BG24]. Lastly, mechanistic interpretability, which has gained popularity in recent years [Fri],
focuses on a bottom-up approach where the goal is to understand and identify parts of the network,
such as the neurons, features, network layers, and so-called circuits or motifs in the network. It has
been shown that, as seen in biological networks [Mil+02; Ves03], neural networks also learn the same
patterns across different models, i.e., the same features across networks [Ola+20].
We hypothesize that if we can get human-understandable explanations of a model’s inner workings, we
would also be able to unlearn specific samples or concepts from the model, as we would understand
the model fully. This is tied to the notion of enumerative safety, which is the idea that we could reach
a point where we understand every feature in the model and could check through all of these to look
for undesirable behavior [Hub+24; Nan23]. This is also the motivation for looking into mechanistic
interpretability from an unlearning perspective. The different interpretability paradigms are visualized
in Figure 3.1.
3.1.2.2
What is a feature?
Before continuing to explain some of the working theories and recent findings within mechanistic in-
terpretability, we need to establish a definition of a feature. In Elhage et al. [Elh+22], they define
features as properties represented by a single neuron in a suﬀiciently large neural network, i.e., it gets
1https://docs.pytorch.org/docs/stable/generated/torch.nn.CrossEntropyLoss.html
==PAGE:41==
3.1 Neural networks
25
