Figure 3.1: Interpretability paradigms. The figure is highly inspired by Bereska and Gavves [BG24].
a dedicated neuron. Many hypothesized features remain in superposition (the following Figure 3.2 will
explain superposition), and they hope that a suﬀiciently large network would dedicate a neuron to
these features for the definition to hold. This is a vague definition, and they even go so far as to cite
philosophy within mathematics to argue why one should not get too attached to definitions [Lak76]. In
Olah et al. [Ola+20], they describe features as fundamental parts of the neural network and say that
they correspond to directions, which they also argue in Goh [Goh16] and White [Whi16]. Based on
this and the definition from Bereska and Gavves [BG24], ”Features are the fundamental units of neural
network representations that cannot be further decomposed into simpler independent factors.”, we come
up with the following definition of a feature.
We define features as the fundamental units of neural network representations, corresponding to specific
learned properties or concepts. They are often understood as directions in the network’s activation space
and are considered irreducible in the sense that they cannot be decomposed into simpler independent
factors. Conversely, a property that is reducible into such factors will be denoted a composite feature
or a high-level feature.
It is important to acknowledge that proving a feature is truly fundamental (irreducible) is a significant
practical challenge. In practice, what we identify as a ”feature” is often our best hypothesis for one of
these fundamental units. Therefore, while we maintain this definition, our analysis may refer to observed
phenomena as features with the understanding that future work could reveal them to be composite.
The following section will give some more intuition on what a feature is and how features are often
represented in superposition in neural networks.
3.1.2.3
Thought vectors and superposition
In Goh [Goh16] they show that applying a generative model such as the Variational Autoencoder (VAE)
[KW14] that has an encoder f and a decoder g and introduces a bottleneck structure, on the CelebA
dataset [Liu+15] creates a latent representation or thought vector [GN17; Dc15] which has been shown
to possess some interesting properties2. If one takes all s images with a smile in them

Something similar has been shown for
word vector embeddings learned with a Recurrent Neural Network (RNN), where a famous example
is the subtraction of the embedding for man and the addition of woman to the embedding for king.
This would yield an embedding close to that of queen [MYZ13; Whi16]. It has also been shown that
polysemous words, i.e., words with multiple meanings, are placed in a superposition between features
with embeddings like word2vec and GloVe.
The thought vector is a representation (often a lower-dimensional one, like the output of an encoder)
that captures information about the input.
This thought vector can then be composed of multiple
features, also referred to as atoms. An image of a red elephant could be thought of as a composition of
different features, i.e., a composite feature, such as redness, ear-flap velocity, trunk length, friendliness,
etc.

(3.8)
Given that we have access to all possible features Z = [z1, . . . , zm], i.e. a dictionary of features, we
can describe the thought vector as a linear combination of the feature vectors, where y decides which
features are activated and how much.
f(x) ≈Zy
for some Z ∈Rn×m, y ∈Rm
∥y∥0 ≤k, ∥zi∥= 1
(3.9)
Here the l0 norm of y represents the number of non-zero elements in y and it is assumed that k is small,
i.e. y is sparse and we only need some of the total amount of features to represent our image. This is in
line with research on sparsity in natural signals, and it has been shown that images possess a structure
that makes them possible to represent sparsely [Fie87; OF96; SO01]. This is not unique to images, but
also other signals [Ela10], e.g. audio [Plu+10].
The features do not necessarily have to be interpretable, like with the image features described in the
previous examples [Goh16]. One thing that might seem impossible is the detection of the individual
features given only the thought vector. Intuitively, when we add numbers together, e.g. 1+2+3 = 6, we
cannot go back to the individual terms given the sum. This is because we mostly add numbers together
in one dimension, and the possible combinations ”collapse” onto the same line or interfere.
In a higher-dimensional space, we know from the theory of eigenvalue problems that if the feature
vectors zi are orthogonal to each other, we can detect the presence of any feature yi by taking the dot
product of the thought vector with zi:
z⊺
i f(x) ≈z⊺
i (Zy) = z⊺

(3.10)
This is because zjzi = 0 for j ̸= i due to orthogonality, and zizi = 1 since we assumed the features
are normalized. Thus, the dot product directly reveals the coeﬀicient yi, indicating whether feature zi
is present in the thought vector and to what extent.
However, in practical scenarios, we often have more features than dimensions (m > n), meaning the
feature vectors cannot all be orthogonal. This is where the assumption that the activation vector y
is sparse, i.e., ∥y∥0 ≤k with k being small, becomes important. If only a small subset of k features
are active for any given input x, then the thought vector f(x) is a linear combination of only these k
feature vectors:
f(x) ≈
X

yjzj,
where |active set| = k
(3.11)
==PAGE:43==
3.1 Neural networks
27
Even though the complete set of m feature vectors is not orthogonal, it is entirely possible for this
smaller subset of k active feature vectors to be linearly independent, especially if k ≤n. When these k
active feature vectors are linearly independent, they form a unique basis for a k-dimensional subspace
within the n-dimensional thought vector space. The specific linear combination (determined by the
non-zero values in y) uniquely defines the thought vector within this subspace.
3.1.2.4
Polysemanticity
The fact that a model is able to represent a higher-dimensional feature space than it has dimensions,
due to the inherent sparsity in the feature space, is said to lead to a phenomenon called polysemanticity
[Elh+22; Aro+18]. Polysemanticity is when a single neuron (or direction in activation space) activates
for multiple, seemingly unrelated concepts or features, i.e., the model is in a superposition [Elh+22].
Another way to think of it is that the trained neural network or observed model simulates a network
with enough dimensions to assign a neuron to each feature, which means the features are completely
disentangled.
Our observed model is then able to lossly simulate or approximate this model in a
much lower dimensional representation, leveraging the inherent properties of higher dimensional spaces
described in the previous subsubsection 3.1.2.3 to represent this. This is illustrated in Figure 3.2.
Hypothetical model (disentangled)
Actual trained model (entangled)
Figure 3.2: Illustration of the superposition hypothesis in neural networks. The diagram contrasts
a hypothetical disentangled model (left), where each neuron ideally represents a single, distinct feature,
with an actual trained model exhibiting entanglement (right), which is an approximation of the disen-
tangled hypothesis model. The figure is highly inspired by Elhage et al. [Elh+22].
In Huben et al. [Hub+24], they argue that features must be sparsely activating for superposition to
even be possible, as there would be interference between the non-orthogonal features if many of the
features are activated at once.
In Elhage et al. [Elh+22], they suggest two approaches3 to resolve superposition. (1) We could design
a model that does not have superposition.
This could be done by adding l1 regularization to the
activations in the network, enforcing sparsity in the activations. It is argued that the models benefit
from superposition, and in their domain of text features, seem to be sparse, thus not making this the
best solution. (2) Another approach would be to learn an overcomplete basis that describes how features
are represented. They propose sparse coding or dictionary learning to do this, and it has been explored
using SAE’s [Hub+24; Bri+23]
Our rationale is that if we can understand superposition and potentially disentangle or get some monose-
mantic representation of the activations in the neural network, we would also be able to better prevent
dampening or the modification of these activations from destroying the model. We could dampen the fea-
tures in this monosemantic representation. In section 3.2 we will introduce the concept of autoencoders
and describe how SAEs have been used to learn a monosemantic representation of the activations.
3.1.3
Vision Transformer
Apart from testing smaller fully connected networks, we also want to evaluate the various unlearning
methods on more complex benchmark datasets such as CIFAR-10 described in subsection 2.2.2. For
this, we will be using a ViT, and this section will serve as an introduction to the theory behind the ViT.
3They also suggest a hybrid between the two.
==PAGE:44==
28
3 Theory
It should be noted that the ViT is not a main focus of our thesis, and it could be treated as a black
box model that we wish to apply unlearning to.
The ViT architecture, first proposed by Dosovitskiy et al. [Dos+21], uses the very famous transformer
architecture [Vas+17]. The main contribution lies in formalizing how images can be preprocessed in
a manner compatible with the transformer architecture. The transformer expects a sequence of one-
dimensional tokens as input. To make this compatible with image data, they propose to split the images
into disjoint patches and subsequently flatten them. Formally, consider an image X ∈RH×W ×C where
H, W, C are the height, width, and number of channels, respectively. Let ph, pw denote the patch size
in the height and width dimensions, both of which are chosen hyperparameters. Consequently, splitting
an image into patches results in nh = H
ph patches in the height dimension and nw = W
pw patches in the
width dimension. For notational convenience, we denote the total number of patches as N = nh · nw.
Applying patching to x would result in an input of dimension Xp ∈RN×ph×pw×C. After obtaining Xp,
it is flattened into a 1D sequence Xf ∈RN×ph·pw·C. We have made an illustration of the patching and
flattening in Figure 3.3. After flattening the input image to obtain Xf, it is embedded into a latent

space of dimension dh. This is achieved via a learnable linear projection, Wemb ∈Rph·pw·C×dh. To
capture information about the relative locations of pixels, a learnable positional encoding, P E ∈RN×dh,
is added to the embedded input. Putting this together gives the following operation:
Z = XfW emb + P E
(3.12)
Where Z becomes a matrix consisting of embedding vectors, i.e. Z =
z1

⊺. Z is then
passed through a number of transformer blocks Bk for k = 1 . . . K along with a classification token
(CLS). The whole pipeline is illustrated in Figure 3.4 and the elements are described in the following
sections.
Each transformer block refines the token embeddings by constructing contextually-aware representations.
This is accomplished via a multi-head self-attention mechanism, where each token’s representation is
updated by aggregating information from the entire sequence of tokens [BB24, ch. 12]. This process
enables the model to learn complex relationships between different parts of the image. For a detailed
technical breakdown of the transformer block’s architecture, including its layer normalization, multi-
head attention, and feed-forward network components, see Appendix A.1.
Inspired by Devlin et al. [Dev+19], a special classification token, CLS, is prepended to the input sequence
before it enters the transformer blocks, as also shown in Figure 3.4. After passing through the final block,
the output representation corresponding to this CLS token is used as the aggregate image representation.
This vector is then fed into a downstream classification head, typically a two-layer FFNN which produces
the final class prediction.

Autoencoding
3.2.1

To understand the idea behind the SAE, we will give a brief introduction to sparse coding and dictionary
learning. In sparse coding, the idea is to decompose some data vector x into a basis or dictionary D

In sparse coding, we already know the dictionary D, as opposed to dictionary learning where we seek

which we will introduce by first introducing the autoencoder and then explaining the extension to get
a SAE.
3.2.2
Autoencoders
Before we can understand what a Sparse Autoencoder is and how it has been used to tackle polyse-
manticity in neural networks, it makes sense to first look at what an autoencoder is and what it does.
The autoencoder or auto-associative neural network [BB24] presented in Baldi and Hornik [BH89] and
Rumelhart, Hinton, and Williams [RHW86] is a model that seeks to create some sort of internal rep-
resentation, z, of the input sample, x, in an unsupervised manner. A neural network is trained to
generate an output, ˆx, that is close to the input, i.e., it tries to approximate the identity function
[Ng11]. This can also be thought of as copying its own input [GBC16]. We have illustrated this in
Figure 3.5.
The network is typically structured in two parts. First, an encoder network, f, maps the input x to a
latent representation z = f(x), which is referred to as a thought vector in subsubsection 3.1.2.3. Second,
a decoder network g that reconstructs x from the latent representation, ˆx = g(z).
==PAGE:46==
30
3 Theory
𝒙
𝒛
𝒙#
𝑓
𝑔
Figure 3.5: Structure of an autoencoder: Mapping the input x to a reconstruction ˆx through a latent
vector or thought vector. Figure inspired by Goodfellow, Bengio, and Courville [GBC16].
To ensure that the network does not just ”copy” the input, constraints are often applied to the network
or the loss. This could be lowering the dimensionality of the intermediate representation z compared
to the input dimensionality of x, which creates a type of bottleneck structure [BB24] as illustrated in
Figure 3.6. The network is often trained with a sum of squares objective, seeking to minimize the error
between the input and the reconstruction:
L(x, ˆx) = 1
2
N
X
n=1
||ˆxn −xn||2
(3.15)
𝒙
𝒙"
⋮
⋮
⋮
𝒛
Encoder
Decoder
𝑔
𝑓
Latent space
(bottle neck)
Figure 3.6: Autoencoder with bottleneck constraint. Figure inspired by [Fie23].
It has been shown that if the neural network has linear activations, i.e. the output of a unit in the
network is simply the a weighted sum of its inputs and potentially an added bias, then the error function
will have a global minimum, at which the network will project the data onto the M first principal
components, given that z has M dimensions [BH89; BK88], i.e. the autoencoder performs PCA [The15,
ch. 18]. If we add multiple non-linear layers, the optimal solution to the aforementioned loss can be
thought of as a Nonlinear PCA (NLPCA) [BB24] or the finding of principal curves or manifolds [HS89;
DC92]. A principal curve can be understood as a smooth curve that threads through the central region
of a data distribution in RD (D-dimensional space), effectively capturing the underlying pattern or
”spine” of the distribution [Hau16]. In fact, Scholz, Fraunholz, and Selbig [SFS08, ch. 2.2] describes
NLPCA as ”a MLP with an autoassociative topology”, which is the same as an autoencoder. It should be
mentioned that the optimization of Equation 3.15 becomes a significantly harder problem than its linear
counterpart, as it is now non-linear and not just quadratic [BB24]. Therefore, we have no guarantee
that the global minimum will be reached.
3.2.3
Sparse Autoencoders
Another way to constrain the autoencoder, other than introducing a bottleneck structure, is by imposing
a sparsity constraint on z by adding a regularizing term in the loss function of (3.15), Ω(z).
L(x, g(f(x))

From a Bayesian perspective, this l1 penalty is not arbitrary.
It arises naturally from assuming a
Laplace prior on the latent variables zn. This choice of prior encourages the activations to be sparse.
A full derivation showing how minimizing the negative log-likelihood under a Laplace prior yields the
objective in Equation A.21 is provided in section A.2.
What we seek to achieve with this sparsity-promoting term is to get a model that has extracted useful
features [GBC16]. Similarly to the autoencoder with a bottleneck, the sparse autoencoder will map
input features to fewer ”effective” feature vectors; the difference being that normal autoencoders have
a ”physical” constraint on the latent dimension, whereas the sparse autoencoder has a constraint on
how much of the latent space can be non-zero. The SAE can be thought of as learning both the sparse
codes and the dictionary in the dictionary learning problem described in subsection 3.2.1, and recently
it has been explored as a tool for decomposing the polysemantic features in neural network activations
[Hub+24].
3.2.4
Sparse Autoencoders to Enforce Monosemanticity
Recently, SAEs have been successfully applied to interpret the internal representations of large lan-
guage models (LLMs) by decomposing polysemantic activations into more interpretable, monosemantic
features [Hub+24],[Tem+24].
The core idea is to train an SAE on the activation vectors from an
intermediate layer, such as an MLP layer or the residual stream. This setup is illustrated in Figure 3.8.
One of the earliest successful applications of a SAE was proposed in [Hub+24].
Similar to subsec-
tion 3.1.1, we have a model M : Rd(0) →Rd(L) with L layers, d(0) is the dimension of the input layer
and d(L) is the dimension of the output layer or the amount of classes C.
Let a ∈Rd(ℓ) be the model activation at layer ℓcorresponding to an input x ∈Rd(0), i.e., h(ℓ) from (3.2).
Further, let W enc ∈Rm×d(ℓ), benc ∈Rm denote the encoder weight and bias, respectively where m is
the size of the latent space or dictionary. Hence, the encoder maps the model activations to the latent
space. Finally, W dec ∈Rd(ℓ)×m and bdec ∈RD(ℓ) are the decoder parameters which projects latents
back into the activation space. From the perspective of dictionary learning, W dec can be thought of as

3.3
Machine Unlearning
This section is meant to give an overview of some of the state-of-the-art unlearning methods that
dominate the literature at the time of writing. Furthermore, it can be seen as a theoretical elaboration
==PAGE:49==
3.3 Machine Unlearning
33
on the MU methods highlighted in section 1.5.
Unless otherwise specified, throughout the section we use the following notation: The complete training
dataset before unlearning is denoted as D = {(xi, yi)}N
i=1. Each data point (xi, yi) consists of a feature
vector xi ∈Rd(0), where d(0) represents the input dimension of the model described in (3.1), and an
associated class label yi ∈N. For the unlearning process, we partition the dataset D into two disjoint
subsets: the forget set, Df ⊂D, and the retain set, Dr = D \ Df. Consequently, these sets have no
overlap (Dr ∩Df = ∅) and their union reconstructs the original dataset (Dr ∪Df = D).
3.3.1
SISA
As mentioned in section 1.5, SISA [Bou+21] is an exact MU method that utilizes the idea of training
multiple client models from federated learning to make retraining more eﬀicient.
3.3.1.1
Sharding
The initial phase of SISA is sharding. Let D represent the original training dataset, which is a collec-
tion of data points. Sharding involves partitioning D into S ∈N disjoint subsets, denoted as shards
D1, D2, . . . , DS. These shards must satisfy two primary conditions. Firstly, they must be mutually
exclusive, meaning that for any two distinct shards Di and Dj, where i, j ∈{1, 2, . . . , S} and i ̸= j,
their intersection must be the empty set. This is formally expressed as:
Di ∩Dj = ∅
∀i, j ∈{1, . . . , S},
i ̸= j.
(3.24)
This ensures that each data point in D belongs to at most one shard. Secondly, the union of all S
shards must compose the original dataset D. This is expressed as:
S[
k=1
Dk = D.
(3.25)
This condition ensures that every data point from the original dataset D is included in at least one
shard. Collectively, these two conditions ensure that the set of shards {Dk}S
k=1 forms a true partition
of the dataset D. Each shard Dk will subsequently be used to train an independent constituent model.
Any given observation (x, y) ∈D has an equal probability of falling within any of the shards. Addition-
ally, the probability of unlearning specific datapoints is assumed to be uniform. Similarly, for analyzing
the performance of unlearning, it is often assumed that any data point in D has an equal likelihood of
being part of a future set of data points to be forgotten, denoted Df ⊂D.
Associated with each shard Dk is a constituent model, denoted Mθ(k), where θ(k) represents the model’s
parameters.
Each constituent model Mθ(k) is optimized in isolation using only the data from its
corresponding shard Dk. Upon convergence, the parameters θ(k) for each constituent model are stored,
effectively linking each trained model to the specific subset of data Dk it has learned from.
When an unlearning request for a set of data points Df is received, the SISA procedure involves
identifying the shard(s) containing these data points. Let Kf = {k ∈{1, . . . , S} | Dk ∩Df ̸= ∅} be the
set of indices for shards affected by the forget request. For each k ∈Kf, the corresponding constituent
model Mθ(k) must be retrained from scratch on the amended shard D′
k = Dk \ Df. Models associated
with shards Dj where j /∈Kf (i.e., Dj ∩Df = ∅) remain unaffected and do not require retraining.
Assuming that all forget observations belong to the same shard, the eﬀiciency gain over retraining a
single model trained on D can be expressed as
|D\Df |
|Dk\Df |. Here lies another assumption that training time
scales linearly with the number of observations.
3.3.1.2
Slicing
At the cost of increased space complexity, the eﬀiciency of SISA can be further improved. Concretely,
the observations within each shard Dk can be further split into R ∈N disjoint subsets, referred to as
slices, {Dk,i}R
i=1
==PAGE:50==
34
3 Theory
When distributing data within a shard Dk into its slices, it is assumed that any given observation (x, y) ∈
Dk has an equal probability 1/R of being assigned to any particular slice Dk,i for i ∈{1, 2, . . . , R}. We
denote the paramers for shard k and slice i, θ(k,i). The training procedure for the constituent model
M(k,0) (initially with parameters θ(k,0)) on its corresponding shard Dk = SR
i=1 Dk,i is as follows:
1. Train Mθ(k,0) on Dk,1 for e1 epochs and save the model weights, θ(k,1).
2. Continue training Mθ(k,1) on Dk,1 ∪Dk,2 for e2 epochs and save the obtained parameters, θ(k,2).
3. Repeat this process until the union of slices is equal to Dk and save the final model parameters
after training for eR epochs on Dk, θ(k,R).
This sequential training and saving of intermediate model parameters at each slice aggregation allows
for more eﬀicient unlearning if data points from specific slices need to be forgotten, as retraining
can potentially start from an intermediate saved state rather than from scratch. This will result in
checkpointing a total of S · R model parameters.
More on slicing: Bourtoule et al. [Bou+21] argues that choosing to apply slicing should come with
no additional training time. This was chosen to make reasoning about the eﬀiciency gains of using
slicing more straightforward. The authors assume that training time scales linearly with the number of
observations used for training. Using this, and letting e′ denote the number of epochs that would be
used without slicing, the total time without slicing becomes:
t′ ≜e′ · |Dk|.
(3.26)
Now, the constraint that the training time should remain the same regardless of whether slicing is used
can be expressed as:
t′ =

After training the S constituent models to convergence, they need to be combined to make a joint class

predictive performance across constituent models and should not involve the training data. The paper
proposes a label-based majority vote across constituent models, and experiments with a prediction-based
majority vote as well. Given an observed input x, let p denote a vector containing the most likley class
of x under each constituent model:

3.3.2
Amnesiac Unlearning
Amnesiac unlearning [GNG21] is an approximate unlearning technique that attempts to undo the effect
that forget observations had on the model. It does so by subtracting parameter updates from training
batches that included one or more observations from the forget set. Most techniques for training neural
networks involve starting at some initial parameter value θ(0) and then updating the parameters by
taking small steps in parameter space towards minimizing some loss function [BB24; Zha+23]. The
parameters at epoch e ∈{1, . . . , E}, where E is the total number of epochs, is then defined as:
θ(e) = θ(e−1) + ∆θ(e−1)
(3.33)
This is often done using stochastic gradient descent, where we calculate the gradient of some loss
function with respect to some parameter and take a step in the negative direction of this gradient.
==PAGE:52==
36
3 Theory
In practice, we take steps based on batches of the data. Let b ∈{1, . . . , B} be the batch number where
B is the total amount of batches and ∆θ(e)
b
be the parameter update at step e batch b. Then we can
define the update in (3.33) as the sum over all batches:
θ(e) = θ(e−1) +
B
X
b=1
∆θ(e−1)
b
(3.34)
Hence, the parameter value at the final step E can be seen as the sum of the updates over all batches
and all epochs starting from some initial parameters θ(0):
θ(E) = θ(0) +
E
X
e=1
B
X
b=1
∆θ(e)
b
(3.35)
Amnesiac unlearning utilizes this perspective, storing the parameter updates after each batch in each
epoch, Θ =
n
∆θ(e)
b
oB,E
b=1,e=1. One challenge to this is the storage space it requires, especially for larger
models [GNG21].
When an unlearning request is received for a forget set Df, we first identify which batches the samples
of Df occur in. Hence, a set of parameter updates related to Df, Θf ⊆Θ, is identified from the stored
parameter updates. To unlearn, we then subtract all the parameter updates related to the forget set:
θu = θ(E) −
X
s∈Θf
s
(3.36)
Here, it is important to state that since the parameter sum is not independent, but ordered dependently,
the subtraction makes the method approximate, i.e., the each update is dependent on all preceding
updates.
Graves, Nagisetty, and Ganesh [GNG21] argue that the difference between the unlearned parameter
weights θu and the parameters of a retrained model θretrain should be small when the size of Θf is
small. When the size of Θf increases, they state that a small amount of fine-tuning is required. This
also becomes necessary when the batch size used during model training grows suﬀiciently large. However,
they do not elaborate on how this is done. From their code, it can be seen that they simply take gradient
steps on the retain data Dr, which is what we will do as well 5. Additionally, for controlled experiments
where we know the forget set beforehand, we can run it only saving the updates related to the known
forget set.
3.3.3
Scrub+R
A different approach to unlearning is SCalable Remembering and Unlearning unBound (SCRUB)
[Kur+23], which reframes the problem using a teacher-student paradigm. In this framework, the origi-
nal model Mθorig, trained on the complete dataset, serves as a teacher. A new student model Mθu is
initialized with the teacher’s weights and is then fine-tuned with two goals in mind: To forget informa-
tion from the forget set Df while remembering knowledge from the retain set Dr. To achieve this, the
authors employ a contrastive loss function, which can be broken down into three key components.
First, let θorig and θu denote the parameters of the original (teacher) and unlearned (student) models,
respectively.
The KL-divergence between their output distributions for an input x is given by the
shorthand notation:
d(x, θu) = DKL
 softmax(Mθorig(x))∥softmax(Mθu(x))

(3.37)
However, a closer inspection of the oﬀicial source code, a detail discovered late in our research process,
reveals that the practical implementation uses a temperature-scaled version of this metric [HVD14].
This method modifies the softmax function in (3.3) with a temperature parameter T ∈R+:
softmaxT (z)i =
exp(zi/T)
P
j exp(zj/T)
(3.38)
5Their code can be found here: https://github.com/lmgraves/AmnesiacML
==PAGE:53==
3.3 Machine Unlearning
37
The actual divergence metric used in the SCRUB algorithm is therefore a “DistillKL”:
dT (x, θu) = DKL
 softmaxT (Mθorig(x))∥softmaxT (Mθu(x))

(3.39)
Using a temperature T > 1 softens the probability distributions, providing a more nuanced signal from
the teacher that can improve knowledge transfer [HVD14]. Given that this was an implementation
detail not prominent in the paper, we will not perform an extensive analysis of this hyperparameter.
Instead, we will adopt the authors’ practice from their provided code and set the temperature to T = 2
for our experiments 6.
The SCRUB objective is composed of two competing goals.
The first is to retain knowledge from
Dr, which is addressed by a loss component, Lretain. This component includes two terms: One that
encourages the student to mimic the teacher’s output distribution on the retain set (the KL-divergence
term) and another that directly optimizes for predictive accuracy on that same data (the cross-entropy
term). Together, these terms work to preserve the model’s utility on the data it is supposed to keep:
Lrepair =
α
|Dr|
X
(xi,yi)∈Dr
d(xi, θu) +
γ
|Dr|
X
(xi,yi)∈Dr
LCE(xi, yi; θu)
(3.40)
LCE denotes the cross entropy loss defined in (3.4).
The second goal is to forget information from Df. This is achieved by maximizing the KL-divergence
between the student and teacher on the forget set, effectively pushing the student to disobey the teacher
for the data it must unlearn. This objective is captured by the term Lforget:
Lforget = −1
|Df|
X
(xi,yi)∈Df
d(xi, θu)
(3.41)
The complete SCRUB loss function combines these two objectives:
Lscrub = Lretain + Lforget
=
α
|Dr|
X

Stay close to teacher on Dr

}
Move away from teacher on Df
(3.42)
In practice, optimizing this min-max objective is challenging.
SCRUB addresses this by iterating
between a step of updates on the forget set (the max-step) and a step on the retain set (the min-step).
We will refer to these two alternating steps as the min-max step or epoch from now on. To guard
against harming the retain performance, the authors also perform a sequence of additional min-steps
at the end of training to fully restore the model’s accuracy [Kur+23]. They do not specify how many
min-max and min-steps are needed, but they do show the issues of instability of the competing min-max
objectives and the need for running the right number of steps to not harm the performance [Kur+23].
3.3.3.1
The Rewind Procedure
A key challenge in user-privacy applications is that achieving a very high error on the forget set can be
counterproductive. It may lead to an unlearning-equivalent of the Streisand effect7, where an attempt to
remove information makes its absence more conspicuous. An uncharacteristically high error on certain
data points could reveal to an attacker that these points were part of a forget set, making the model
vulnerable to Membership-Inference Attacks [Kur+23].
To mitigate this, the authors propose SCalable Remembering and Unlearning unBound + Rewind
(SCRUB+R), an extension that incorporates a rewind procedure. The goal is to make the error on the
forget set just high enough to match that of a model retrained from scratch, without being so high as
to leak information [Kur+23]. Since retraining is computationally prohibitive, SCRUB+R proposes a
6https://github.com/meghdadk/SCRUB/blob/main/small_scale_unlearning.ipynb
7See: https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Streisand_effect
==PAGE:54==
38
3 Theory
method to estimate this target error. First, a special validation set, Drewind, is created to have the
same class distribution as Df. Algorithm 1 shows how this rewind dataset is created. The standard
SCRUB algorithm is then trained to completion, where the forget error is typically at its maximum.
The error of this final model on Drewind serves as a reference point for the desired forget set error.
Finally, the unlearning process is ”rewound” by selecting the saved model checkpoint whose error on
Df most closely matches this reference value.
This procedure fundamentally assumes that the unlearned model’s performance on Drewind is a valid
proxy for a retrained model’s performance on Df. The intuition behind this assumption is that, after
unlearning, any correct predictions on these held-out validation examples are due only to the model’s
generalization from the retained data, mimicking a model that never saw the forget set in the first place.
This implicitly requires that the model’s performance on Dr has not been degraded during unlearning;
that is, that catastrophic forgetting has been avoided.
Algorithm 1 Construct Rewind Validation Set for SCRUB+R
1: Input: Forget dataset Df, General validation dataset Dval.

2: For each class k, let c(f)

←|{(xi, yi) ∈Df | yi = k}|
▷Compute class counts in the forget set.
3: Drewind ←∅
▷Initialize the new rewind validation set.
4: for k from 1 to C do
▷Iterate over all classes.
5:

▷Get all samples of class k from Dval.

▷Return the newly constructed rewind set.

3.3.4
Selective Synaptic Dampening
The SSD approach [FSB24] is an eﬀicient post-hoc unlearning method. The goal is to quantify which
parameters are important for Df but not Dr. The authors hypothesize that a small set of parameters
captures information specialized to specific examples.
To quantify the importance of a parameter wrt. a dataset, the authors propose to use the diagonal of
the Fisher Information Matrix (FIM). Fisher information is a fundamental quantity in statistics and
information theory that encodes how much information an observed random variable carries about a
parameter. The following subsubsection 3.3.4.1 will define and give an introduction to Fisher infor-
mation and the FIM along with some intuition. This will be followed by a more application-focused
subsubsection 3.3.4.2 describing how this is used by Foster, Schoepf, and Brintrup [FSB24] in their
proposed SSD unlearning.
3.3.4.1
The score function and Fisher information
To understand how the FIM can be used to quantify the importance of model parameters with respect
to the data, it is instructive to first consider the score function and Fisher information for a single
parameter. The goal in parametric inference is often to maximize the likelihood function p(x|θ) with
respect to the parameter θ, or equivalently, to find the parameter value that best describes the observed
data x [Bis06]. The likelihood can thus be viewed as a function of the parameters θ. A common example
is the Gaussian distribution, for which the likelihood of observing x, given parameters µ and σ2 is:
p(x|µ, σ2) =
1
√
2πσ2 exp

−(x −µ)2
2σ2

(3.43)
For mathematical convenience and numerical stability, it is common to work with the log-likelihood
function, log p(x|θ). The logarithmic transformation is monotonic, so it preserves the location of the

of the log-likelihood function with respect to the parameter θ. It measures the gradient of the log-
likelihood function at a specific parameter value θ. We denote it s(θ) as done by Murphy [Mur23,
sec. 3.3.4]:
s(θ) = ∂
∂θ log p(x|θ)
(3.44)
The score function indicates how sensitive the log-likelihood function is to changes in the parameter θ
at a particular value of θ [Ly+17]. For the Gaussian in (3.43) the score function of µ would become:
s(µ) = ∂
∂µ log p(x|µ, σ2) = 1
σ2 (x −µ)
(3.45)
The full derivation can be found in Appendix A.3.1.
To provide intuition for the score function, Figure 3.10 plots its behavior using θ as a generic parameter
on the x-axis. The figure shows results for data sampled from three distributions: A Gaussian (with
mean θ = µ = 6), a Poisson (with rate θ = λ = 6), and a Bernoulli (with probability θ = p = 0.6). It is
seen that at the true parameter, the score varies around 0.
For a single parameter θ ∈R, the Fisher information Ix(θ) provides a measure of the amount of
information that an observation x carries about θ. It is formally defined as the expected value of the
squared score function. The expectation is taken with respect to the probability distribution p(x|θ).
This is a crucial point: it means we average over all possible observations x that could be generated from
the model p(x|θ) when the parameter governing that model is precisely θ. Therefore, the parameter
θ used throughout this definition, both within the score function s(θ) and in defining the distribution
p(x|θ) for the expectation, is assumed to be the true, underlying parameter value of the data-generating
process:
Ix(θ) = Ex∼p(x|θ)

Sampling Variation of Score Functions
Figure 3.10: Sampling variation of score functions for Gaussian, Poisson, and Bernoulli (formulas
shown in panels). Each colored line represents the average score from one dataset (n = 30) at each
parameter θ (100 values are sampled uniformly in the shown range). Ten realizations per distribution
illustrate sampling variation. Vertical dashed lines indicate the true parameter θ; the horizontal dashed
line indicates s(θ) = 0. The plot is highly inspired by Pawitan [Paw13, fig. 8.1].
In the case of discrete data, the expectation is a sum, while for continuous data, it is an integral [Ly+17].
Under certain regularity conditions, defined by Kullback [Kul97] and Cramer [Cra99], the expectation
of the score function is zero (which was also indicated by Figure 3.10):
Ex∼p(x|θ) [s(θ)] = 0
(3.47)
Given that much of the deep learning literature concerning Fisher Information [KAA19] and its appli-
cations, (e.g. natural gradients [PB14]), often omits detailed discussion of these regularity conditions,
we have chosen to leave this mostly up to the reader, while still presenting the conditions and the proof
of (3.47) in the Appendix A.3.2.
Given (3.47), the Fisher information in (3.46) can be expressed as the variance of the score function:
Varx∼p(x|θ) [s(θ)] = Ex∼p(x|θ)

(3.48)
The Fisher information Ix(θ) quantifies the amount of information that an observable random variable
x (from p(x|θ)) carries about the parameter θ. Intuitively, it measures the expected curvature of the
log-likelihood function around the value θ. A higher Fisher information indicates that the log-likelihood
function has, on average, a sharper peak around θ, implying that θ can be estimated more precisely
from the data, i.e. we have less uncertainty about it.
If we return to the score function of the mean of the Gaussian in (3.45), we see that the Fisher
information at µ becomes:
Ix(µ) = Ex∼p(x|θ)

information (see Figure 3.11).

The diagonal elements, [Ix(θ)]i,i = Varx∼p(x|θ)[si(θ)], represent the Fisher information for each pa-

inter-dependencies between the estimates of θi and θj.
An interesting fact about the FIM is that it is the same as the expected Hessian of the negative log
likelihood. This makes it even more evident that the Fisher information is a measure of the curvature
of the log likelihood around θ [Paw13].

3.3.4.2
Selective synaptic dampening using the FIM
With the previous introduction to Fisher information and the Fisher Information Matrix (FIM), we
can start looking at it in the context of deep learning. Here, the FIM and related second-derivative
information have been used to identify important parameters in the network [GAS20a; Kir17; Guo+20;
LDS89]. This has inspired the works of Foster, Schoepf, and Brintrup [FSB24] who proposed SSD as
an eﬀicient post-hoc unlearning method. Here, the goal is to quantify which parameters are important
for Df but not Dr, such that neurons can be dampened accordingly. They hypothesize that a small
set of parameters captures information specialized to specific examples. To achieve this quantification,
they propose to use the diagonal of the FIM since it captures how influential each parameter is for the
output distribution.
In practise we will calculate something called the empirical FIM [Mar20], first proposed by Schrau-
dolph [Sch02] as the instantaneous Fisher information. We will denote the empirical FIM ˆI(θ) for the
parameter vector θ over the dataset D = {(xn, yn)}N
n=1:
ˆID(θ) = E(xn,yn)∼D [∇θ log p(yn|xn, θ)∇θ log p(yn|xn, θ)⊺]
=
1
|D|
X
(xn,yn)∈D
∇θ log p(yn|xn, θ)∇θ log p(yn|xn, θ)⊺
(3.55)
It can be thought of as a monte carlo sample over the observed data evaluated at the parameters
obtained after convergence of gradient based optimization.
For the SSD method, we are only concerned with estimating the diagonal elements of the FIM, i.e. the
variance of the score function. We denote the diagonal elements of the empirical FIM ˆiD(θ). We can
then write it as follows using the Hadamard product8 or elementwise multiplication:
ˆiD(θ) =
1
|D|
X
(xn,yn)∈D
∇θ log p(yn|xn, θ) ⊙∇θ log p(yn|xn, θ)
(3.56)
Note that the two gradients are vectors of the same dimension as θ, and that ˆiD(θ) is also a vector
with the same dimension as θ. Informally, the n’th diagonal element can be thought of as quantifying
how much information θi carries about a dataset D. Hence, a diagonal entry that is large in magnitude
signifies that a smaller change in the parameter is needed to have an effect on the log-likelihood.

3 Theory
A significant drawback of SSD, as noted by Foster, Schoepf, and Brintrup [FSB24], is the challenge of
hyperparameter selection. These parameters lack an intuitive basis and are highly dataset-dependent.
This motivated our exploration of potential improvements, as detailed in section 4.1. During our work,
we also discovered and implemented a recent preprint on adaptive SSD from the original authors that
addresses this exact challenge [SFB24].
3.3.4.3
Adaptive SSD
Since the release of SSD, the original authors have proposed an extension to the framework. A key
limitation of the original SSD method is having to choose values of λ and α. Setting these parameters
too low leads to catastrophic forgetting, and too high values will yield the identity function. Crucially,
the appropriate hyperparameters should yield a model update such that it mimics the behavior of a
retrained model, which, naturally, is inaccessible.
To address this, the authors propose Adaptive SSD [SFB24], which automatically determines appropri-
ate hyperparameters. The method builds on three key assumptions:
Assumption 1 states that given an appropriate choice of α we can omit the choice of λ and select
λ = 1. Hence, if an appropriate threshold α is selected for how much more information Df should carry
than D about a parameter for an intervention to take place, then the dampening factor defined in (3.59)
for the j’th parameter can be reduced to ˆi(D)
j
/ˆi(Df )
j
.
Assumption 2 says that within a dataset, both the class frequencies and the frequency of subpopula-
tions within classes follow a long-tailed distribution. This is motivated by the observation that many
natural phenomena exhibit power law distributions [Fel20], where the frequency of an event is inversely
proportional to its rank9 raised to some power. Mathematically, this can be expressed
F(r) ∝r−α
(3.61)
Here r is the rank of the item and α is the scaling parameter. Such power law distributions are ubiquitous
in natural and social systems, from word frequencies in language (Zipf’s law) to city populations [Ven16]
and neural network activations [LDS89; FC19].
In the context of machine learning datasets, this
manifests as a small number of classes or subpopulations containing the majority of examples, while a
large number of classes or subpopulations appear rarely [Fel20].
Assumption 3 is that as the size of Df increases, the number of high importance parameters in ˆi
(Df )
will scale sublinearly. Thus, the total number of parameter updates when applying adaptive SSD to each
sample in Df individually is less than the number of updates when applying adaptive SSD to all samples
in Df at once. Hence, similar samples in a dataset will have overlapping parameter importances.
Combining these ideas, they propose to select α according to the distribution of relative parameter
importances.
α = pq

(3.62)
Where diag(·) transforms a vector to a diagonal matrix. The percentile, q, of the distribution is selected

perform element-wise division with elements of ˆi

denominator. We reached out to the authors, and they confirmed that this was a mistake. Their imple-
mentation aligns with the definition in (3.62). Additionally, the authors noted that the implementation
9In this context, rank r refers to the position of an item (e.g., a word or a class) when all items are sorted in descending
order of their frequency or occurrence count F [New05].

was meant for a management audience and is mostly based on empirical results, not mathematical rigor.
Basically, they winged it based on experience because, let us be honest, management just wants to see
the numbers go up, not the proof.
Although this method proposes a solution for one of the challenges with SSD, one prominent challenge
remains. Namely, the assumption of neurons coding for exactly one feature only. While this assump-
tion might hold for overparameterized networks, research has shown that polysemantic representations
emerge during training when the set of features in the dataset grows beyond the feature-modelling
capabilities of the model. This results in neurons coding for multiple, seemingly unrelated, features
simultaneously [Tem+24; Elh+22; Goh16; Bri+23; Hub+24] thereby potentially degrading the eﬀicacy
of the SSD method.
3.4
Tuning and Optimization
3.4.1
Elastic Weight Consolidation
One of our proposed methods, described in section 4.2, builds upon the principles of Elastic Weight
Consolidation (EWC). Proposed by Kirkpatrick et al. [Kir+17], EWC is a technique in continual learning
designed to mitigate the catastrophic forgetting of previous knowledge when a model learns new tasks.
The method involves selectively slowing down learning on the weights that are important for previously
learned tasks, an approach inspired by studies of synaptic consolidation in the human brain [CG15].
Formally, they define the problem as having trained a network on some task A and later want to train
the network on a new task B without forgetting about task A in the process. From Bayes’ theorem
[Bay63], we can write the posterior probability of the model parameters θ given a dataset D:
p(θ | D) = p(D | θ)p(θ)
p(D)
(3.64)
In a continual learning scenario with two independent tasks, the dataset is D = DA ∪DB. By assuming
independence, we can factor the likelihood term p(D | θ) = p(DA, DB | θ) = p(DA | θ)p(DA | θ) and
p(D) = p(DA, DB) = p(DA)p(DB). This allows us to express the posterior over the combined data in
terms of the posterior from task A:
p(θ | D) = p(θ | DA, DB) = p(DA | θ)p(DB | θ)p(θ)
p(DA)p(DB)
= p(DA | θ)p(θ)
p(DA)
|
{z
}
p(θ|DA)
p(DB | θ)
p(DB)
= p(θ | DA)p(DB | θ)
p(DB)
(3.65)
Taking the logarithm of (3.65) provides a basis for defining a loss function. Our goal is to find parameters
θ that maximize this posterior, which is equivalent to minimizing its negative logarithm [Mur22]:
log p(θ | D) = log p(θ | DA) + log p(DB | θ) −log p(DB)
(3.66)
Ignoring the term log p(DB) as it is constant with respect to θ, and defining the loss for task B as
LB(θ) = −log p(DB | θ), we arrive at the following loss:
L(θ) = LB(θ) −log p(θ | DA)
(3.67)
This objective combines learning task B (by minimizing LB) with a constraint imposed by the knowledge
from task A in the posterior p(θ | DA).
Here, the challenge is that the posterior distribution p(θ | DA) is intractable to compute for complex
models like neural networks. To overcome this, EWC employs the Laplace approximation [Mac92],
which approximates the true posterior with a Gaussian distribution.
==PAGE:62==
46
3 Theory
This Gaussian is centered at the parameters θ∗
A, which are the weights found after training on task A.
The precision of the Gaussian, which determines its sharpness, is given by the FIM, ˆIDA, evaluated on
the task A data. For computational eﬀiciency, EWC uses only the diagonal of the FIM, similar to SSD,
where each diagonal element ˆi(DA)
j
estimates the importance of the corresponding parameter θj for task
A. The log of this Gaussian approximation has the form:
log p(θ | DA) ≈constant −1

(3.70)
This loss function consists of the standard loss for the new task B, LB(θ), and a quadratic regularization
penalty. This penalty prevents catastrophic forgetting by penalizing deviations from the optimal pa-
rameters of the old task, θ∗
A. The penalty for each parameter θj is weighted by its estimated importance
ˆi(DA)
j
, thus protecting the parameters most critical for task A.
The intuition, visualized in Figure 3.12, is that this penalty constrains the parameters to remain within
a region of low error for task A while simultaneously minimizing the loss for task B. The framework
can be extended to a sequence of tasks by adding a quadratic penalty term for each previously learned
task [Kir17]. Highly inspired by this approach, we present a novel method for unlearning, which we call
Teacher Ascent, in section 4.2.
EWC
No penalty
Figure 3.12: Schematic illustration of EWC in parameter space. After learning task A, the parameters
are at θ∗
A, located within the region of low error for task A (green ellipse). When training on a new task
B, which has its own region of low error (orange ellipse), different training strategies lead to different
outcomes. Training with no penalty (dotted arrow) moves parameters towards the optimal solution for
task B but can catastrophically forget task A by leaving its low-error region. Standard l2 regularization
(dash-dotted arrow) constrains all parameters equally, potentially hindering the learning of task B
effectively if the constraint is too severe. EWC (solid arrow) selectively penalizes changes to weights
important for task A, allowing the network to find a good solution for task B that still maintains good
performance on task A. Figure adapted from Kirkpatrick [Kir17].
==PAGE:63==
3.4 Tuning and Optimization
47
3.4.2
Bayesian optimization
Another one of our contributions to literature includes selecting hyperparameters for SSD, which will be
powered by BO. Therefore, this section will serve as a primer on the concept. For a further explanation
of BO we refer to Murphy [Mur23], Agnihotri and Batra [AB20], and Garnett [Gar23], which this
explanation is mostly based on.
The goal is to find the optimal input parameters for a black box function, denoted as f : X →R. In
other words, we do not know the function’s underlying shape. Additionally, evaluating f is expensive
or time-consuming. Consequently, our primary goal is to find the optimal parameters by querying f as
few times as possible [Mur22].
The core idea of BO is to build a probabilistic model, often called a surrogate function or response
surface model [Mur23]. This surrogate model represents our current understanding of how f behaves.
Specifically, it defines a probability distribution, p(f(x)|Dt), over the possible output value of f(x) for
any input x in the search space X. This distribution is conditioned on the data Dt = {(xi, f(xi))}t
i=1
we have collected from previous evaluations of f up to time t.
Instead of directly evaluating the
expensive function f repeatedly, we use this cheaper-to-evaluate surrogate model to guide our search.
This guidance comes from an acquisition function, denoted as α(x). The acquisition function quantifies
the potential benefit or utility of evaluating f at any given point x ∈X.
A key feature of most
acquisition functions is that they strategically balance exploration, which involves investigating regions
of the parameter space where we are highly uncertain about the function’s behavior, and exploitation,
which focuses on regions where the surrogate model predicts high-performing function values [Mur23].
At each step, the next point to evaluate, xt+1, is chosen by finding the point that maximizes this
acquisition function:
xt+1 = arg max
x∈X
α(x)
(3.71)
After evaluating f(xt+1), this new data point (xt+1, f(xt+1)) is added to Dt, updating our belief and
refining the surrogate model for the next iteration. This iterative process continues until a stopping
criterion (e.g., a budget of evaluations) is met.
3.4.2.1
The Gaussian Process Surrogate Model
The most common choice for the surrogate model that defines p(f(x)|Dt) (as described in Section 3.4.2)
is the Gaussian Process (GP). A GP is a probabilistic model where the belief about the function value
f(x) at any point x ∈X, conditioned on the observed data Dt, follows a Gaussian distribution:
f(x)|Dt ∼N(µt(x), σ2
t (x))
(3.72)
Here, µt(x) is the posterior mean function, providing the model’s current best estimate of f(x). The
posterior variance function, σ2
t (x), quantifies the uncertainty associated with this estimate.
These
posterior quantities are computed using the data Dt and, crucially, a chosen kernel function (or
covariance function), k(x, x′). The kernel defines the assumed covariance between the function values
at any two points, k(x, x′) = cov(f(x), f(x′)). It thereby encodes our prior beliefs about the function’s
characteristics, such as its smoothness or periodicity [Tol23].
The effectiveness of a GP hinges on how well its underlying assumptions, which are encoded as prior
beliefs in the kernel function, match the true objective function. The most crucial of these assumptions
is smoothness, which posits that nearby input points x1 and x2 should yield similar output values
f(x1) and f(x2). This property, directly encoded by the kernel, is what allows the model to generalize
from observed data to unseen regions. For a comprehensive treatment of the other assumptions, such
as stationarity, we refer the reader to Garnett [Gar23, p.8].
The most common choice of kernel, and the one applied in our methods, is the Radial Basis Function
(RBF)
k(x, x′) = σ2
f exp

−||x −x′||2
2ℓ2

(3.73)
==PAGE:64==
48
3 Theory
The behavior of the RBF kernel is determined by its two hyperparameters σf and ℓ, which determines
how much points co-vary and how ’far’ away we let them co-vary. Kernel hyperparameters are typically
estimated from the data Dt. A realisation of a Gaussian process with the RBF kernel is visualised
in Figure 3.13(a). The resulting µt(x) and σ2
t (x) are updated as more data points are incorporated
into Dt, and these two values are precisely what acquisition functions use to balance exploration and
exploitation.
3.4.2.2
Aquisition function
With the Gaussian Process established as the surrogate model, the next step is to intelligently select
the next point to evaluate as described in (3.71). This decision is guided by an acquisition function,
α(x), which assesses the utility of sampling at any given location in the search space. While many such
functions exist, a common and intuitive choice, and the one we apply, is the Upper Confidence Bound
(UCB):
α(x) = µt(x) + λσt(x)
(3.74)
Where µt(x) and σt(x) are directly drawn from our GP. The key component here is λ that directs the
exploitation vs. exploration trade-off. A higher λ induces more exploration by increasing the influence
of the variance, where lower λ-values increase the reliance on the µt(x), the current best guess. In
Figure 3.13(b), we visualize the simple GP with an Radial Basis Function (RBF) kernel, and show how
the UCB acquisition function would operate, given a few observations.

Finally, in practice it is common to initialise the GP, by sampling a representative subset of datapoints
[Gar23]. This is done to promote a better search for the acquisition function, and is typically done using
a set of points that uniformly cover the space, such as those generated by a Sobol Sequence [Sob67].
3.5
Evaluation
In our literature review, we outlined several approaches to evaluating machine unlearning. Aligned with
the scope of this project, we focus on empirical and attack-based evaluation methods.
A key criterion for an unlearning method to be considered effective is that it should facilitate un-
learning. A common approach in the literature is to compare the performance of the unlearned model
with that of a fully retrained model on Df, Dr, and Dval.
Another desirable property is functional equivalence, meaning the unlearned model should behave sim-
ilarly to the original model on the retained data, Dr, and on in-distribution points from Df. This is
particularly important in settings where the original model has undergone extensive evaluation of its
decision boundaries, as redoing this analysis post-unlearning would be costly. As discussed in subsec-
tion 1.4.4, we assess this equivalence by comparing the output distributions of the original and unlearned
models.
==PAGE:65==
3.5 Evaluation
49
3.5.1
Fidelity and Utility Metrics
In a classification setting, a natural way of comparing models is to consider their accuracy. For a dataset
D = {(xi, yi)}N
i=1, we can define the accuracy as:
acc(Mθ, D) =
1
|D|
X
(xi,yi)∈D
1(arg max
j
Mθ(xi)j = yi)
(3.75)
Here 1(·) denotes the indicator function which evaluates to 1 if the input is true and 0 otherwise.
While the accuracy measure is simple and interpretable, it only considers the final model predictions.
Hence, the measure does not assess how similar the predicted distributions are. Reflecting this may be
desirable since it conveys a discrepancy in situations where both models share the same prediction, one
model’s output distribution has high entropy, and the other’s is sparse.
One way to generalize disagreement between two models in terms of their output distributions is the
Kullback-Leibler (KL) divergence, which, for continuous probability distributions p and q, is defined as:
DKL(p∥q) =
Z ∞
−∞
p(x) ln
p(x)
q(x)

dx
(3.76)
Some useful properties of the KL divergence, which make it suitable for measuring functional equivalence,
are DKL(p∥q) ≥0 and DKL(p∥q) = 0 ⇐⇒p = q. However, it should be noted that the measure is
asymmetric, unbounded, and undefined in case q does not have a probability density in a region where
p does. Hence, another measure that addresses these shortcomings of KL-divergence is the Jensen-
Shannon (JS) divergence. For probability distributions p and q it is defined as:
JS(p, q) = 1
2

By considering the average distribution between p and q, we ensure that the KL-divergence terms
are always well defined. Additionally, by computing the average of KL-divergences, the JS divergence
becomes symmetric. Lastly, the measure is bounded 0 ≤JS(p, q) ≤log(2). If computed in log2, the
JS divergence is now in bits, as opposed to nats, with the bounds 0 ≤JS(p,q)
ln(2)
≤1. When presenting
results on the JS divergence, its unit will be in bits unless otherwise specified.
3.5.2
Attack Based Evaluation (MIA)
The goal of Membership Inference Attacks (MIAs) is to infer whether or not a target model was trained
on some data. In the context of unlearning, we are interested in assessing the membership inference
probability of the unlearned model on Df. While many variations of MIAs exist in literature, we follow
the conventions of Foster et al. [FSB24] and Kurmanji et al. [Kur+23]. The method is a black-box
approach to MIAs. This was chosen to remain consistent with relevant unlearning literature.
The setup is as follows: Let P r ∈R|Dr| be a vector containing the Shannon entropy of the predicted
class probabilities on the retain set under the target model. Hence, the i’th row of P r is given as:
pi = H(softmax(Mθu(xi))),
xi ∈Dr
(3.78)
Where H is the Shannon entropy [Sha48; GSS15, ch. 5], given by:
H(p) = −
X
i
pi log pi
(3.79)
Similarly, let P val ∈R|Dval| denote a vector containing the entropy of predicted probabilities under the
target model for the validation data. We construct a labeled dataset for training the MIA model by
assigning y = 1 to samples from the retain set and y = 0 to those from the validation set. The resulting
dataset becomes:
Dtrain = {(P r,i, 1)}|Dr|
i=1 ∪{(P val,i, 0)}|Dval|
i=1
(3.80)

==PAGE:67==
CHAPTER 4
Methods
Based on the theory and the scope defined in section 1.2, we now turn to the practical contributions
of this thesis. Recalling our illustrative example from the scope, our goal is to support the researchers
by developing or improving approximate unlearning methods tailored to centralized learning settings.
In this setup, we assume full access to both the forget and retain sets, but no access to the original
training process.
We relist the four main contributions from section 1.2:
• We diagnose the practical failures of SSD and propose a novel method using BO to automate the
selection of its critical hyperparameters. While our BO framework did not conclusively outperform
the ASSD baseline in our experiments, we identify clear potential for future improvements with
a more refined optimization objective.
• We demonstrate a critical limitation of the SCRUB+R method, showing that its optimization
process is inherently unstable and converges toward catastrophic forgetting without a precise
early-stopping heuristic.
• In response to this instability in SCRUB+R, we propose TA, a novel fine-tuning objective that bor-
rows principles from EWC [Kir+17]. Our method demonstrates promising convergence properties,
successfully forgetting target data while maintaining high performance on the data to retain.
• We propose a novel unlearning method that leverages a Sparse Autoencoder (SAE) to extract
more disentangled, ”monosemantic” features from a network’s activations. Although this was a
later addition to our project and not as extensively analyzed, this approach allows for dampening
to occur in a more meaningful feature space, representing a promising direction for future research.
All methods are developed with empirical evaluation in mind and are evaluated in the context of sample-
level unlearning requests.
4.1
Improvements to SSD
While SSD offers a powerful framework for approximate unlearning, it remains a key practical limitation
that one has to select its hyperparameters manually. Based on our first experiments (see section 5.1)
we see that setting the hyperparameters λ and α without a principled heuristic, can result in catas-
trophic forgetting.
This motivated us to research how we could address this limitation and find a
principled approach to selecting appropriate hyperparameters. While working on this, we came across
the recent preprint on adaptive SSD [SFB24] addressing the same limitation, which we described in
subsubsection 3.3.4.3.
4.1.1
Layerwise Dampening
Based on the common understanding that different layers in neural networks learn features at varying
levels of abstraction, we suggest that dampening does not need to occur at all layers in the network.
An example is image data, where networks seem to learn more general features such as frequency filters
in the first layers, whereas later layers become more dataset specific [Yos+14; Yos+15].
Assuming
this behavior translates to other types of neural networks and dataset types, we suggest testing the
dampening of specific layers in the network. To formalize this, we define Θ = (θ(1), θ(2), . . . , θ(L)) as
==PAGE:68==
52
4 Methods
the concatenation of parameter weights θ(l) at each layer l in the neural network. We then define a set
of target layers that we want to dampen as a subset of the total amount of layers Ltarget ⊆{1, . . . , L}.
The update rule for the parameters will then be similar to the original in (3.60), with the condition
that we only dampen the layers in Ltarget:
θj =

FIM for the parameter θj with respect to the forget set Df and the full dataset D respectively, just as
in subsection 3.3.4. This formulation allows us to specifically target layers for dampening.
4.1.2
Bayesian Optimization to Find (α, λ)
We propose a novel method using BO (see subsection 3.4.2) to identify these parameters by optimizing a
specially constructed objective function, L(BO). The construction of L(BO) is inspired by SCRUB+R’s
rewind step (subsection 3.3.3, Algorithm 1). This step aims to make the unlearned model’s performance
on the forget set, Df, emulate that of a model retrained from scratch on Dr. We achieve this by using
the original model’s performance on a rewind dataset (Drewind) as a proxy. Drewind is a subset of the
validation set Dval specifically chosen to have the same label distribution as Df.
First, the original model Mθorig (trained on the full dataset D prior to unlearning) is evaluated on the
rewind set Drewind. Each sample in Drewind is a pair (xi, yi), where xi is an input feature vector and yi
is its scalar true class label. For each input xi, Mθorig(xi) yields a C-dimensional vector of raw output
scores (logits), where C is the total number of classes. We denote the k-th component of this vector as
Mθorig(xi)k.
We define the L(rewind) as a vector containing the model’s loss for each class:

(4.4)
Minimizing L(BO) encourages the unlearned model Mu to achieve a similar performance profile on Df
as the original model Mθorig does on Drewind.
==PAGE:69==
4.1 Improvements to SSD
53
We immediately see two potential limitations with this approach. Firstly, since this objective drives
Mu towards similar performance as Morig on Drewind we are exposed to adversarial cases where the
forget set is located in a wrong class. If all the adversarial samples are located in the forget set, then
no representational samples can be drawn from the validation set and we will have a discrepancy in the
distribution between Drewind and Df. This could lead to collapsing the decision boundary of the area
hosting the adversarial datapoints.
Conversely, if Drewind instead contains samples highly similar to those in Df, our proxy L(rewind) might
reflect good performance on data that an ideal retrained model would have fully forgotten. This could
guide Mu to retain some knowledge of Df-like samples. However, this is mitigated by two assumptions:
(1) If Df comprises truly unique data, such data (or close analogues) would likely not be present in
Dval (and thus Drewind), or would be removed as part of the unlearning desiderata. (2) If Df is similar
to much of the retained data, then Mu should exhibit some performance on Df-like samples, as this
reflects generalization from the data it is intended to remember.
With L(BO) defined, BO can eﬀiciently search for the optimal hyperparameters α and λ. A key ad-
vantage is that the FIM for D and Df needs to be computed only once. Subsequent BO iterations
then only require evaluating the effects of different dampening configurations on the unlearned model’s
scores/losses. Ultimately, BO seeks a dampening configuration that best aligns Mu’s performance on
Df with the retrained proxy. This careful alignment helps mitigate the Streisand effect, where overly
degrading performance on Df could inadvertently signal model tampering, potentially detectable by
attacks like MIA (see subsection 3.3.3, subsection 1.4.4).
4.1.3
Bayesian Optimization with Layerwise Parameters
Instead of applying the same values of α and λ to all network parameters, we hypothesize that it may be
advantageous to use different (α, λ) values for different layers. This is grounded in the observation that
mimicing a retrained model might be achieved optimally by protecting certain layers and dampening
more aggresively in others. To this end, we present a method for partitioning the layers of the network,
and assigning an α and λ for each partition. Let L denote the total number of model layers, and suppose
we wish to partition these into P disjoint groups. We choose which layer should be assigned with pair
of α, λ values as:

4.1.4
Bayesian Optimization Objective Smoothness
While implementing BO, we saw that the objective function L(bo) was discontinuous. This can be at-
tributed to the update rule of SSD seen in (3.60). To see this, suppose we collect the ratio ˆi(Df )
j

j

the objective function where changes in α will not result in any update to the model parameters.
As a consequence, L(BO) becomes piecewise constant in regions of the search space, which limits the ef-
fectiveness of Bayesian Optimization using Gaussian processes to estimate the objective function. This

The parameter λ controls the steepness or sensitivity of the transition around α. A larger λ makes the
sigmoid transition sharper, approximating a step function and closely mimicking the original indicator-
based SSD rule. A smaller λ yields a smoother curve, distributing dampening more gradually over a
broader range of importance scores.
4.2
Teacher Ascent (TA)
This method is inspired by the observation that existing approaches struggle with ’over-forgetting’ or
general instability. We draw inspiration from Elastic Weight Consolidation (EWC) [Kir+17], a technique
detailed in subsection 3.4.1. EWC aims to prevent catastrophic forgetting in continual learning scenarios.
When a network trained on a Task A is subsequently trained on a Task B, EWC helps retain performance
on Task A by adding a quadratic penalty to the loss function as seen in (3.70)). This penalty acts like a
”spring anchoring the parameters to the previous solution” [Kir+17], penalizing changes to parameters
identified as important for Task A.
We use these ideas to reinterpret machine unlearning through the lens of continual learning. The initial
task involves classifying the original dataset D. The subsequent ”unlearning” task is to make the model
”forget” a specific subset Df ⊂D, while crucially preserving performance on the remaining data, the
retain set Dr = D \ Df. To achieve this, we propose fine-tuning the model using a gradient ascent
objective on the forget set Df. Specifically, we actively seek to maximize the model’s entropy on Df,
thereby destroying its performance on this specific data. Concurrently, we employ the EWC penalty
term, calculated on the retain set Dr, to protect the model’s knowledge relevant to Dr, i.e. the weights
of the original model θorig are analogous to task A in subsection 3.4.1. Furthermore, instead of using
the FIM directly, we utilise the FIM ratio,
ˆi(Dr)
j
ˆi
(Df )
j
. This design choice was inspired by a conversation with
the author of Adaptive SSD [SFB24] Stefan Schöpf, following a correction to his work that highlighted
the utility of the FIM ratio for identifying generalised parameters. Finally, we help the model preserve
performance on the retain data by adding a single-batch retain loss. Our proposed loss function, LT A,
to be minimized during unlearning, is hence formulated as:
LT A = −1

We coin this method Teacher Ascent (TA). The ’ascent’ refers to maximizing the loss on Df via
gradient ascent. The “teacher” component refers to the EWC regularization and the auxiliary batch
loss on the retain set, which collectively help preserve crucial knowledge about the retain set Dr. The
abbreviation TA also serves as a mnemonic for ’Teaching Assistant’, reflecting this guided learning
==PAGE:71==
4.2 Teacher Ascent (TA)
55
aspect during the unlearning process. Note that in practice, we iterate over forget set batches, and for
each of these we draw a single retain batch.
4.2.1
How Did We Get Here?
The formulation of Teacher Ascent evolved through a combination of theoretical considerations and
empirical observations, as detailed in section 5.5. This section outlines the key steps and design choices
that led to the final loss function. The initial approach was grounded in EWC-regularized gradient
ascent and formulated as seen Equation 4.8:
LT A1 = −1
|Df|
X
(xi,yi)∈Df
LCE(xi, yi; θu) + λ
2
X
j
ˆi(Dr)
j
(θu,j −θorig,j)2
(4.8)
Where LCE is the cross entropy as defined in (3.4). Here, the first term maximizes the cross-entropy
loss on the forget set Df, effectively encouraging the model to assign low confidence to the correct labels,
i.e., to learn incorrect predictions. The second term is the EWC penalty, which regularizes changes to
parameters θu,j important for the retain set Dr, anchored to their values θorig,j from the original model.
λ is the EWC regularization strength, and ˆi(Dr)
j
is the importance weight of parameter j for Dr.
A key advantage of this approach is computational eﬀiciency, particularly when the forget set Df is
substantially smaller than the original dataset. Gradient updates based primarily on Df (complemented
by the EWC term) are significantly less resource-intensive than retraining the model from scratch or
fine-tuning it on the entire dataset.
However, one potential issue with directly maximizing cross-entropy on Df is the risk of the Streisand
effect, especially if Df is distributionally distinct from the retain set Dr. Aggressively pushing the
model away from correct classifications on Df might inadvertently make these specific samples more
salient or easier to identify.
Therefore, we suggest a variant where, instead of maximizing the cross-entropy loss, we aim to maxi-
mize the Shannon entropy of the unlearned model’s output probability distribution for samples in Df.
Maximizing entropy encourages the model to be maximally uncertain about its predictions for these
samples. The Shannon entropy was defined in (3.79), but for convenience we restate it here [Sha48;
GSS15, ch. 5]:
H(p(x)) = −Ex∼p(x) [log p(x)] = −

of the model’s output distribution for xi. Furthermore, to mitigate an observed catastrophic forgetting,

Note that during the initial optimization step, the EWC penalty evaluates to zero since θu = θorig.

getting. This could be mitigated using a gradual warm-up or a learning rate scheduler, but will not be
explored further.
4.3
Machine Unlearning in Monosemantic Feature Space Using
an SAE
Building on the interpretability of Sparse Autoencoders (SAE), we propose a novel, post-hoc unlearning
method that operates within the disentangled feature space of a network’s activations. Instead of directly
modifying the parameters of the original model as in SSD, this approach ”unlearns” by identifying and
suppressing the monosemantic features in the SAE dictionary that are disproportionately active on the
forget set, Df, compared to the retain set, Dr. One could argue that this is a form of output suppression
rather than unlearning, as the parameters of the underlying, Morig, remain unchanged.
Our method begins by creating a disentangled feature space for a chosen layer of the fully-trained model,
in our case the middle layer. To achieve this, we first train an SAE, as described in subsection 3.2.3,
on the activations produced by this layer using the full training dataset, D. Once this SAE has learned
a dictionary of what are presumed to be monosemantic features, the unlearning process can proceed
by calculating a dampening factor for each dictionary feature (i.e., each column of the SAE’s decoder
weight matrix W dec).
With the trained SAE, we next quantify how relevant each dictionary feature is to the retain and forget
sets. We begin by passing both datasets, Dr and Df, through the base model and the SAE’s encoder
to obtain their corresponding sets of sparse feature activations, ZDr = {zi}|Dr|
i=1 and ZDf = {zi}|Df |
i=1 .
To create a size-invariant measure of importance, we compute the mean activation value for each feature,
conditioned on it being active. This results in two mean activation vectors, µ(Dr) and µ(Df ) in Rm. For
a given dataset D ∈{Dr, Df}, the j-th element of its mean activation vector is defined as:
µ(D)

This vector, µ(D), provides a summary of how strongly each feature contributes to reconstructions for

The dampening factor, βj, for each feature j is then calculated based on the ratio of its mean activation
on the retain set versus the forget set. Given a hyperparameter α ≥0 that controls the sensitivity of
the dampening, the vector of dampening factors β ∈Rm is defined component-wise for each feature
j = 1, . . . , m:
βj =

retain set, it is completely removed (βj = 0). If a feature is not active on the forget set at all, it is left
unchanged (βj = 1).

reduces the magnitude of dictionary features that were strongly associated with the forget data, thereby
suppressing their contribution to the model’s output.

CHAPTER 5
Experiments
In this thesis, we have examined several established methods for Machine Unlearning, as well as intro-
duced novel approaches. Many of our contributions emerged through iterative experimentation, and as
such, the order of experiments reflects a degree of chronology.
Initially, we conduct a qualitative study of each method from chapter 3 on synthetic data to establish an
intuitive understanding of their behaviors with different forget sets. The subsequent investigation is split
into two primary tracks: First, an exploration of SSD leading to our proposed method for improvement
SSD with BO, and second, an analysis of SCRUB+R’s convergence that motivates our alternative
approach, Teacher Ascent (TA). The chapter concludes with a comprehensive ”pollice verso”1 evaluation,
where the methods are benchmarked against each other on the MNIST and CIFAR-10 datasets.
Most of the larger experiments were run on an Nvidia A100 or Nvidia V100 provided by DTU Computing
Center [DTU25] while the smaller experiments were conducted on a MacBook M3 Pro. Although not
a guarantee, we have focused on passing seeds through the pipeline for reproducibility.
All the code can be found on https://github.com/Maplewarrior/Masters-thesis

5.1
Exploring the Decision Boundary (Case-Driven Experiments)
This section investigates the functional differences of various machine unlearning methods by compar-
ing the decision boundaries of an original model to its unlearned counterpart under specific controlled
scenarios described in section 2.1. The experiment covers Sharded, Isolated, Sliced, and Aggregate
(SISA), Amnesiac Unlearning (AU), SCalable Remembering and Unlearning unBound (SCRUB), Selec-
tive Synaptic Dampening (SSD), Adaptive SSD (ASSD), and our proposed method based on the Sparse
Autoencoder (SAE), which are all described in the Theory (chapter 3) and Methods (chapter 4). As a
gold standard, we evaluate against a model retrained from scratch on the retain data Dr.
1The term refers to the moment when a defeated gladiator’s fate was decided by the twist of a thumb.
==PAGE:76==
60
5 Experiments
5.1.1
Experiment Setup
Our experimental procedure begins by training an original model on the full dataset. We then apply
each unlearning method to remove a designated forget set. Finally, we assess the functional impact
by comparing the decision boundaries of the original, unlearned, and a gold-standard retrained model.
The following sections detail the model architectures, training protocols, unlearning configurations, and
hyperparameter settings.
5.1.1.1
Model Architecture and Base Training
Our base model is a simple feed-forward neural network (subsection 3.1.1) with a single hidden layer and
ReLU non-linearity, as shown in Figure 5.2. For SISA, the ensemble of constituent models follows the
same architecture. Since the SAE expects its input to be a residual stream, we adapt the architecture
by incorporating skip connections as seen in Figure 5.3.
