5.1.1.2
Unlearning Configuration
Following the data generation process detailed in chapter 2, we reiterate the 5 different cases proposed
for our case-driven experiments:
Case 1: Forget set located near a decision boundary between three classes.
Case 2: Forget set located in a high-density region of the correct class.
Case 3: Forget set located in a high-density region of an incorrect class.
Case 4: Forget set located behind the main cluster of the correct class.
Case 5: Forget set located behind the main cluster of an incorrect class.
==PAGE:77==
5.1 Exploring the Decision Boundary (Case-Driven Experiments)
61
For conciseness and clarity, our primary analysis focuses on the scenarios we found most interesting.
These were determined to be Cases 1, 2, and 3 when unlearning a cluster of five points. The complete
results for all remaining scenarios are deferred to Appendix A.4.
5.1.1.3
Hyperparameter Settings
We use fixed hyperparameters for each unlearning method, detailed in Table 5.1. Due to the amount
of different hyperparameters to tune, we decided to omit lengthy parameter optimizations. Choosing
ideal hyperparameters in an unlearning context usually requires access to a retrained model, which is
an unrealistic assumption.
These experiments should not be seen as what capacity each of these unlearning methods has in ideal
circumstances. Rather, they should be viewed as what behavior emerges when applying the methods
for certain hyperparameter choices in specific unlearning scenarios.

Note that we use the same parameters for unlearning clusters and single samples.
5.1.1.4
Evaluation
For these methods, we primarily evaluate model behavior qualitatively through visual inspection of
decision boundaries. For each unlearning method, we plot the classification boundary before and after
unlearning, and compare it against the retrained model. These plots provide insights into how localized
or global the forgetting is, and whether the model exhibits unintended side effects (e.g., collapse or
over-forgetting). The most important qualitative metric is the ’closeness’ of the unlearned model to the
retrained model.
5.1.2
Results and discussion
We ran all experiments with both single and multiple forget samples. We refer to Appendix A.4 for an
overview of all the experiments. As expected, the cases with the cluster of five forget points resulted in
a larger effect on the decision boundary than the single forget point, and we found these experiments
to be of more interest. Across cases, we observed the most diﬀicult ones to be in the adversarial cases,
with case 3 proving the most diﬀicult. Intuitively, these cases should be more diﬀicult as well, since they
might be the result of mislabelling and contradict the general data distribution. Referring back to our
scope and the illustrative example, we are not necessarily focusing on identifying mislabelled datapoints,
albeit, since these adversarial cases seem to be a failcase for some methods, our initial analysis will focus
on case 3, along with the more conventional scenarios; case 2 where the forget set is located inside the
cluster of the correct class and case 1 where the forget set is located at the decision boundary between
the three classes.
To evaluate the functional equivalence via the decision boundary, we present the retrained model as a
baseline in Figure 5.4.

Figure 5.4: Decision boundaries after retraining the model for cases 1, 2, and 3.
The methods we observe to perform best on these scenarios are Amnesiac Unlearning and SCRUB. In
Figure 5.5 we see the decision boundaries for each of the methods across the cases. Comparing the
behaviour of Amnesiac Unlearning and SCRUB, we see that both methods exhibit similar behavior to
the retrained model across unlearning scenarios. Most interestingly, for case 3, both methods seem to
handle it considerably well, given the diﬀiculty of the forget set location, meaning both models exhibit
some robustness to the forget set location. Intuitively, this is expected behaviour for these two methods,
since SCRUB+R will pull the unlearned model to perform similarly to the original model on the retain
set, and push the model away from the forget set. Since the forget set is located adversarially and could
be considered a model correction case, all loss terms in SCRUB+R likely end up working ’towards the
same goal’.
One thing that is hard to judge from these experiments is, whether a method is truly unlearning or just
preserving the original model’s performance, since the retrained model behaves similarly to the original.
We suspect that this could be the case for SCRUB+R. We later examine how SCRUB+R responds
to different hyperparameter settings (section 5.4) and find that its performance is highly sensitive,
particularly to the number of training epochs.
For Amnesiac Unlearning, we observe a more pronounced effect on the model. Specifically, the decision
boundary of the yellow class contracts significantly in all three cases, whereas the retrained model
appears to only shrink it in cases 2 and 3, and by less. Recalling that the method subtracts parameter
updates of batches containing forget points, one plausible explanation the shrinkage would be that the
batches being removed contained disproportionately many observations from the yellow class. However,
further investigation would be required to verify this.
Comparing SSD’s behaviour to SCRUB+R and Amnesiac, seen in the top row of Figure 5.6, we observe
that SSD displays significantly degenerate performance. With hyperparameters of α = 1 and λ = 1,
the method induces catastrophic forgetting in all 3 scenarios. We hypothesise that this behaviour is
caused by a particular sensitivity to the choice of α. Recall that this parameter specifies how much more
information the forget set should carry about a parameter compared to the retain set, in order to apply
dampening. As such, setting α = 1 results in the update rule for the j’th parameter being dictated by
the proportionality directly, ˆi(Df )
j
> 1 ·ˆi(D)
j
. The pronounced change in the decision boundary suggests
that we are dampening parameters in charge of generalizing to the retain set.
We can explore this intuition further by looking at what neurons end up being dampened, as seen in
the lower row of Figure 5.6. The figure shows by colour the amount of dampening, β, applied to each
neuron in the network. Recalling that the amount of dampening is dictated by βj = min

λˆi(D)
j
/ˆi(Df )
j
, 1

,
setting λ = 1, we can easily infer how much more information each dampened neuron carries about the
forget versus the entire dataset set. Interestingly, we see a less aggressive dampening in case 2 compared
to the other cases. Here, the majority of dampening coeﬀicients are around 0.3, which translates to
the diagonal FIM for the forget set having entries 3 times larger than the corresponding FIM entries
for the entire dataset set. This also aligns with the slightly less degraded decision boundary in case 2
compared to the other cases. While we cannot draw direct conclusions based on these observations, it

indicates that SSD is highly sensitive to its hyperparameters, and notably, the method does not offer
any built-in heuristic for selecting them.
For SISA, we generally observe a weaker model, which can be attributed to its distinct architecture, an
ensemble of weak learners. As shown in Figure 5.7, the unlearned model differs significantly from the
original, likely due to the retraining procedure. The training set consists of 65 observations, and since
we use three shards, each will contain either 21 or 22 samples. This results in relatively few samples
per class within each shard, so the removal of even a small number of samples can substantially affect
performance. Due to the architectural differences, it is diﬀicult to make a direct comparison between
SISA and the other unlearning methods. Unlike the other methods, however, SISA constitutes exact
unlearning and therefore comparison becomes less critical.
Generally, SISA seems to handle the three cases reasonably well, and even improves performance in
Case 2. However, case 2 involves an in-distribution forget set, so the observed improvement may be
less about effective boundary correction and more about the chance of a stronger weak learner during
retraining.
Finally, as with SISA, the SAE also utilizes a different network architecture, which results in the
different initial decision boundaries seen in Figure 5.8. Therefore, the most appropriate benchmark for
its unlearning performance is a model of the same architecture retrained from scratch, which can be
seen in (d),(e), and (f) of Figure 5.8. When judged against this baseline, the SAE-based unlearning
shows limited success. The resulting decision boundaries are significantly less aligned with the retrained
model’s boundaries compared to how well other methods mimic their respective retrained counterparts.
In the adversarial scenario (Case 3), the method leads to a significant degradation of the boundary
for the red class. The reasons for this are likely multifaceted. One hypothesis is that the dampening
factor α = 0.9 when calculating βj (see (4.14)) is not chosen properly. Another reason could be that
the low-dimensional nature of the data does not allow the SAE to learn the kind of rich, disentangled
features where we hypothesize that it would excel.

5.1.2.1
Key takeaways
Below we list some of the main takeaways from this experiment, which will guide later investigations.
• The SSD method collapses the decision boundary on nearly all unlearning scenarios in this exper-
iment. Furthermore, the method seems highly sensitive to hyperparameter selection.
• SCRUB seems to perform particularly well, but it remains inconclusive whether this is due to
staying close to the original model or actually mimicking a retrained model.

5.2
Exploring SSD and Layerwise Dampening
Our initial case-driven experiments (section 5.1) revealed a critical failure mode in the SSD method.
With the default hyperparameters (α = λ = 1), SSD consistently caused a collapse of the decision
boundary, which we attributed to sensitivity to hyperparameter selection. Additionally, the method
provides no heuristic for selecting these parameters, making its application unreliable.
This section dissects this failure by investigating the mechanics of SSD more closely. We analyze the
effect of applying dampening to specific layers and the sensitivity to the hyperparameter α. Drawing
inspiration from literature suggesting that neural network layers learn features of varying generality
[Yos+14; Yos+15], we hypothesize that restricting dampening to select layers may prevent model de-
terioration.
The primary goal is to understand why the decision boundary collapsed in our initial
experiments and to determine if a more targeted dampening strategy can yield stable and effective
unlearning.
Each of the following experiments contains selected findings from our analyses, while the complete set
of results can be found in section A.5.
5.2.1
Experiment Setup
The model and data used for this experiment are identical to those in the previous experiment (sec-
tion 5.1).
==PAGE:82==
66
5 Experiments
5.2.1.1
Evaluation
This experiment follows the same evaluation methodology provided in the first experiment (section 5.1),
with a qualitative focus on the visual inspection of decision boundaries.
5.2.2
Results and Discussion
In our initial experiments, SSD with default hyperparameters (α = λ = 1) consistently caused a catas-
trophic collapse of the decision boundary. Figure 5.9(b) illustrates this failure in Case 1, where the
unlearned boundary deviates significantly from the retrained model’s in Figure 5.9(a). The correspond-
ing dampening plot in Figure 5.9(c) reveals that with these parameters, a large number of neurons
across all layers are dampened. This might suggest that the dampening is overly aggressive, leading to
catastrophic forgetting. This section explores this failure by first trying to restrict the dampening to
specific layers.

((c)) SSD dampening
Figure 5.9: Decision boundaries after retraining and SSD on Case 1 with multiple forget observations.
Based on the premise that early layers learn general features while later layers learn task-specific ones,
we applied dampening to each layer separately. As shown in Figure 5.10, this seems to mitigate the
boundary collapse. Of the three options, dampening only the input layer as seen in Figure 5.10(a)
yields a result closest to the retrained model in Figure 5.9(a), suggesting that a targeted adjustment at
the feature-input level is more effective than altering deeper, more specialized layers.
If we think of the input layer as a general feature extractor, while subsequent layers build a hierarchy
of increasingly complex and task-specific abstractions based on these features, it could make sense to
intervene at the input layer closer to the actual raw data, if we want to forget some of the data. It could
be thought of as pruning the problematic information before it is propagated through the network.
This is merely speculation, and one could come up with an argument if the opposite were observed.
Messing with the input layer could cascade through the network and have an amplified effect, and
destroy the model’s performance, analogous to how small adversarial perturbations to an input can
lead to complete misclassification [GSS15].
A parallel line of investigation is adjusting α, which sets the threshold for how much more influential
a parameter must be for the forget set versus the entire dataset to be dampened.
Specifically, if
ˆi(Df )
j
/ˆi(D)
j
> α the neurons will be dampened by a factor β as defined in (3.59). Figure 5.11 shows
that increasing α from 1 to 30 significantly reduces the number of dampened neurons across all layers.
Furthermore, Figure 5.11(a) reveals that the bias terms in the input layer have a significantly higher
Fisher Information ratio than other parameters. This could also help explain why dampening the first
layer had the greatest effect, as was shown in Figure 5.10(a).
Considering the model when we allow dampening across all layers again, setting this higher threshold
for dampening results in an improved decision boundary, as seen in Figure 5.12. Although it does not
fully match the quality achieved by only dampening the first layer with the original α = 1. This might
either suggest that we are still dampening too much with α = 30, or that it could be beneficial to target
dampening to specific layers.

We re-evaluated layer-wise dampening using the less aggressive α = 30 (Figure 5.13).
In contrast
to our first attempt, several configurations now produce results that closely resemble the retrained
model, including dampening only the middle or last layer. This demonstrates that the optimal layer for
dampening is not fixed but depends on the value of α. One caveat here is that we suddenly dampen very
little, and by selectively targeting some layers, one could argue that we do not properly unlearn, as most
of the information regarding the forget set could still be stored in parts of the network, easily recoverable.
Whether this is acceptable depends on the motivation for unlearning as described in section 1.1. While
the approach may fail to meet the strict data removal standards of a privacy deletion request, it might
be suﬀicient for use cases like copyright enforcement, where the primary goal is to functionally prevent
the model from reproducing an infringing output.
Finally, we find that the ideal strategy also depends on the forget data itself. When applying the same
configuration (α = 30) to the adversarial Case 3, dampening the first layer now appears to be the most
appropriate choice, judging from the decision boundaries in Figure 5.14.
Examining the FIM ratios for Case 3 in Figure 5.15 offers further insight. While some of the most
influential neurons now appear in the hidden layer, a large proportion of parameters in the input layer
still have a FIM ratio high enough to be selected for dampening. This could strengthen the hypothesis
that the input layer does play a great role in the dampening process. However, this analysis rests on
the assumption that FIM ratios are directly comparable across different layers, which may not be valid.
This uncertainty motivates a more flexible approach, such as implementing layer-specific α thresholds

5.2.2.1
Key Takeaways
• The decision boundary collapse observed in our initial experiments (section 5.1) is confirmed to
be a result of indiscriminate and overly aggressive dampening stemming from poorly selected
hyperparameters.
• While restricting dampening to specific layers can prevent this collapse, the ideal configuration is
not fixed. It seems that the optimal choice of which layer(s) to dampen depends on α and the
forget set.
These key takeaways motivate the need for a good approach to find optimal values for α and λ, which
we explore in the following section.
5.3
Automated Hyperparameter Selection for SSD (using BO)
From our previous experiments (section 5.1 and section 5.2), it was established that SSD’s effectiveness
is critically dependent on a manual, case-by-case selection of its hyperparameters. The method provides
==PAGE:86==
70
5 Experiments
no heuristic for this process, and the optimal choice appears sensitive to the forget set itself.
To address this limitation, we developed and proposed a novel framework to automate hyperparameter
selection using BO. The goal of BO-SSD is to find α and λ values that make the unlearned model
mimic the original model’s behavior on the rewind set, constructed as in SCRUB+R (Algorithm 1).
The rewind dataset is a subset of the unseen validation dataset with the same label distribution as
the forget set. The primary focus of this chapter is to detail the development and evaluation of this
framework.
During our investigation, it came to our attention that the original authors of SSD had already addressed
this same shortcoming in a follow-up preprint paper proposing Adaptive SSD (ASSD), which we have
described in subsubsection 3.3.4.3. As our work on BO-SSD was developed independently of their
work, we use ASSD as a non-optimization-based baseline for comparison.
This section, therefore, presents our BO-SSD framework and its iterative improvements. We analyze
its performance across our case-driven scenarios and compare it against the ASSD baseline.
5.3.1
Experiment Setup
5.3.1.1
Model Architecture and Base Training
To ensure a direct comparison with our previous findings, the experimental setup, including the simple
feed-forward neural network architecture, base training protocol, and dataset, is identical to that used
in the case-driven experiments section 5.1 and the SSD layerwise dampening analysis in section 5.2.
5.3.1.2
Unlearning Configuration
The unlearning scenarios are also repeated from section 5.1.
We evaluate the methods on the five
defined cases, focusing our primary analysis on Cases 1, 2, and 3 to observe performance on boundary,
in-distribution, and adversarial forget requests, respectively. We also include Case 4, where the forget
set is located behind the main cluster of its own class, to provide a second example of an in-distribution
forget request.
5.3.1.3
Hyperparameter Settings
This experiment evaluates and compares several automated approaches for selecting the SSD hyperpa-
rameters α and λ.
Adaptive SSD
As a baseline, we evaluate Adaptive SSD, a heuristic-based method proposed by
the original authors [SFB24]. The method programmatically selects α by identifying parameters that
carry highly specialized information about the forget set. It is effectively parameter-free from a user’s
perspective.
Bayesian Optimization Framework (BO-SSD)
We propose and evaluate a novel framework us-
ing Bayesian Optimization to find optimal hyperparameters. The general setup for this framework is
as follows: For BO we utilise the botorch Python package [Bal+20]. The objective is constructed as
the negative mean of the squared difference between the loss on the forget set and the constructed
validation set as described in (4.4). We use the RBF as our kernel function, and the UCB as our acqui-
sition function, both described in Equation 3.43. Furthermore, to promote stability in our parameter
space search, we use a Sobol engine2 to sample 10 initial datapoints for our GP. Sobol sequences are
low-discrepancy sequences that provide much better coverage of the parameter space compared to pure
random sampling. It should be understood as a high-dimensional linspace method. This means the
initial points will be more evenly distributed across the search space, giving the GP a better foundation
to model the objective function. For the UCB acquisition function, we use an exploration factor of 2.5.
We ran the BO for 100 steps, resulting in a total of 110 samples drawn from the GP, when including the
initial Sobol sequence. The parameter search space for α is bound to [0, 1000] while λ is bound to [0, 100].
2https://docs.pytorch.org/docs/stable/generated/torch.quasirandom.SobolEngine.html
==PAGE:87==
5.3 Automated Hyperparameter Selection for SSD (using BO)
71
We select the optimal set of parameters from the result of the BO. If multiple parameter sets achieve
the best objective score, we break the tie by choosing the one that minimally alters the original model.
We do this by computing the sum of the optimised parameter values and selecting the set with the
smallest total, assuming this corresponds to the least intrusive update.
Within this framework, we test three variants:
• BO-SSD: The standard implementation optimizing a single pair of (α, λ) for the entire network
as described in subsection 4.1.2.
• BO-SSD-paired: An extension described in subsection 4.1.3 that optimizes 3 pairs of (α, λ), one
for each layer of our network, motivated by the findings in section 5.2.
• BO-SSD-smooth: A proposed variant, described in subsection 4.1.4, that uses a modified,
smooth SSD update rule to better align with the assumptions of the GP.
5.3.1.4
Evaluation
This experiment follows the same qualitative evaluation methodology as the first experiment (sec-
tion 5.1). The primary goal is to visually compare the decision boundaries of the unlearned models
against the gold-standard retrained model to assess the effectiveness of each automated hyperparameter
selection strategy. Additionally, we look at the BO objective function as a function of the parameter
α, which leads us to the suggested smooth variant of the objective.
5.3.2
Results and Discussion
5.3.2.1
Adaptive SSD (Baseline)
Upon evaluating Adaptive SSD, the unlearned decision boundaries shown in Figure 5.17 generally
resemble the retrained baselines from Figure 5.16. Nonetheless, some deviations are apparent, such as
in Case 1, where the decision boundary moves in the opposite direction of the retrained model. For
convenience, we show the retrained models again.

An inspection of the neuron dampening across the three cases ((d), (e), and (f) in Figure 5.17) reveals
that very few parameters are ultimately modified. This is expected considering how α is chosen in the
ASSD procedure (3.63). Since α is chosen from the upper percentile of the ratio in FIM values (3.62),
we will always target a few model parameters using this unlearning method.
This could indicate that our previous manual threshold of α = 30 for Case 1, referenced in Figure 5.12
and discussed in section A.5, was likely still too aggressive, compared to the α = 98.6 provided by
the ASSD heuristic. Interestingly, Case 2 has a much lower α-value than Case 1, reaﬀirming that the
==PAGE:88==
72
5 Experiments
dataset plays a crucial role in the FIM ratios and what threshold to pick. We also keep in mind that we
are analyzing a single sample, and there might be some randomness in which neurons are dampened.
A potential concern, similar to the one raised for SCRUB+R in section A.4, is whether such minimal
changes to the network constitute meaningful unlearning. Conversely, given the simplicity of the dataset,
substantial alterations may not be necessary to forget the specified data. A clearer assessment of this
method’s eﬀicacy is therefore deferred to the benchmark experiments on the more complex MNIST and
CIFAR-10 datasets in section 5.6 and section 5.7.
For future analysis, exploring the representational difference between the retrained and unlearned mod-
els could offer deeper insights than observing parameter changes alone. The dampening plots, such as
the one in Figure 5.17(f), only illustrate the change from the original model, not the final state relative
to the ideal retrained state.
In summary, ASSD emerges as a compelling unlearning method. It effectively applies the principle of
targeted neuron dampening while providing what appears to be a reasonable, automated heuristic for
the threshold α.

5.3.2.2
BO-SSD
In contrast to the heuristic-based Adaptive SSD, our BO-SSD framework seeks to find optimal hyperpa-
rameters through a formal optimization process. We begin by evaluating the standard implementation,
which optimizes a single pair of (α, λ) for the entire network.
We first consider how BO-SSD behaves on unlearning cases with low uncertainty, namely Cases 2 and 4.
As shown in Figure 5.18, the optimization process correctly concludes that for these in-distribution forget
sets, no significant model update is necessary. This is confirmed in Figure 5.18(c) and Figure 5.18(d),
where we see that none of the neurons are dampened. For Case 2, this could indicate that the model
already behaves very similarly on the forget and rewind data.
It could also be attributed to the
objective function being diﬀicult to optimize. Further investigation into this could start by comparing
the Shannon entropy of rewind and forget predictions, but we defer this to future research. This could
==PAGE:89==
5.3 Automated Hyperparameter Selection for SSD (using BO)
73
indicate that our objective proxy is effective, in the sense that it sees no need for unlearning in this
case, where there is none, but not perfectly aligned with the gold standard.
Interestingly, the optimization yields vastly different parameters for the two cases (α = 355.23, λ = 77.54
for Case 2 vs. α = 0.85, λ = 0.67 for Case 4). This suggests that the BO framework is sensitive enough
to detect that the forget points within the dense cluster of Case 2 have a higher influence on the original
model’s parameters than those behind the cluster in Case 4.

Applying the same approach to the more challenging Cases 1 and 3 (Figure 5.19) reveals both the
potential and the limitations of this initial framework.
For Case 1, as seen in Figure 5.19(a), the
method successfully finds parameters that produce a reasonable decision boundary, and it is seen in
Figure 5.19(a) that the α found nearly results in dampening the same parameters as for ASSD. It has
to be noted that BO also finds the optimal λ along with the α, whereas λ = 1 for the ASSD. This
means that the BO-SSD approach will dampen the neurons above the threshold of α less than the ASSD
approach.3
However, in the adversarial Case 3 (Figure 5.19(b)), the method fails and the decision boundary col-
lapses. As seen in Figure 5.19(b), it is simply too aggressive when selecting neurons to dampen, i.e.,
setting α too low.
3Recall that the λ parameter dictates how much we weight ˆi(D)
j
over ˆi
(Df )
j
, so a larger λ means less dampening.
==PAGE:90==
74
5 Experiments
This outcome stands in stark contrast to the stability of ASSD in the same scenario. This failure reveals
a critical vulnerability in the BO-SSD framework: Its reliance on the rewind set, Drewind, as a proxy
for the retrained model’s performance. When constructed with the ground-truth (but mislabeled) data
for Case 3, this proxy becomes flawed, guiding the model to aggressively expand the incorrect class as
seen in. As this proxy dataset is also used in SCRUB+R, one could, based on this, question whether
it is a good proxy. We discuss this further in our analysis of SCRUB+R in section 5.4, where we do
indeed show some cases of forget sets from MNIST where it does not constitute a good proxy.
A potential solution to this adversarial limitation would be to construct the rewind set using the original
model’s predicted labels rather than the ground-truth labels, which would provide a correctly labeled,
in-distribution proxy. This is something that would require further investigation and development of
the method, and we defer it to future work.

In conclusion, the standard BO-SSD framework performs as intended on non-adversarial tasks but
is brittle in adversarial settings due to its objective formulation. This vulnerability, especially when
compared to the robustness of ASSD, motivates the iterative improvements we explore next.
5.3.2.3
BO-SSD-paired
Based on the results of layer-wise dampening in Experiment 2 (section 5.2), we hypothesize that op-
timizing separate (α, λ) pairs for each network layer would provide the BO framework with greater
==PAGE:91==
5.3 Automated Hyperparameter Selection for SSD (using BO)
75
flexibility. We suggested that it may be advantageous to dampen layers differently, even if their FIM
ratios are similar. To test this, we extended BO-SSD to a variant we call BO-SSD-paired as described
in subsection 4.1.3, which optimizes 3 pairs of (α, λ), one for each layer in our model.
The results, shown in Figure 5.20, demonstrate the value of this approach. For Cases 1 and 2, the
outcome is similar to the standard BO-SSD, where not much happens to the decision boundaries due
to limited to no dampening of the neurons. For the adversarial Case 3, however, the paired approach
yields a slightly better decision boundary, avoiding the complete collapse. This might be attributed to
the fact that it becomes harder to optimize the objective given that we have more parameters to tune.
Thus, it does not succeed in destroying the model’s performance on the red class completely. While we
proposed a potential solution to this earlier by constructing the rewind set based on predicted instead
of ground truth labels, this would likely not work for case 1, see Figure 5.20(a). This suggests that
future work should focus on improving the objective function.

Since SSD intervenes on a parameter only when a hard threshold is met, ˆi(Df )
j
> α ·ˆi(D)
j
, we can expect
the objective function to be flat in many regions. A change in α may not trigger a change in the set of
dampened parameters, and therefore no change in the objective value.
This creates a piecewise-constant objective landscape, which violates the smoothness of BO with GPs
(see assumptions in subsubsection 3.4.2.1). To verify this, we plotted the objective function across a
range of α values while keeping λ = 1 fixed. The results, seen in Figure 5.21, empirically confirm that
the landscape is indeed piecewise constant.

This discovery is a key outcome of this experiment. Since the objective function is piecewise constant,
it highlights a potential mismatch between the assumptions of Gaussian processes and the shape of our
objective function. We hypothesize that the BO-SSD paired resembled retraining more closely on case
3 in Figure 5.20 due to the objective becoming harder to optimize. This observation motivated our final
iteration of BO-SSD.
==PAGE:92==
76
5 Experiments
5.3.2.4
BO-SSD-smooth
Grounded in the discovery that the standard SSD update rule produces a piecewise-constant objective
landscape, we developed BO-SSD-smooth, a variant with a continuous update rule designed to be
more compatible with BO. The goal of this final iteration is to resolve the technical discrepancy between
the optimization problem and the assumptions of the GP model.
The primary purpose of this variant was to improve the optimization landscape. To investigate this,
we plotted the objective function against varying α values, keeping λ = 1 fixed for simplicity. The
results in Figure 5.22 clearly demonstrate the success of this modification. The smooth update rule
transforms the problematic piecewise-constant landscape into a continuous one, better aligning with the
core assumptions of our optimization strategy. These plots also reiterate that the landscape’s specific
shape is highly dependent on the forget set and the model’s internal data representation.

Evaluating this variant on Cases 1, 2, and 3 (Figure 5.23) shows decision boundaries similar to standard
BO-SSD in Cases 1 and 2, but a return to catastrophic forgetting in Case 3, resembling the original
SSD. Investigating the cause was considered out of scope, and since the benefits remained theoretical,
the approach was ultimately deemed unsuccessful.
As expected, the revised update rule resulted in different optimal hyperparameters, making direct
comparison with previous methods diﬀicult. In these controlled experiments, the smooth update offered
no clear improvement in unlearning performance or optimization speed. For supporting details, see
subsection A.6.4.
5.3.3
Key takeaways
• BO-SSD works well for general cases, but is severely limited in adversarial cases.
• BO-SSD-paired utilises ideas from Experiment 2 (section 5.2) and assigns hyperparameters to
each layer in the network. This method added flexibility and proved more resilient in adversarial
==PAGE:93==
5.4 Exploring the Convergence of SCRUB+R
77
cases, while performing similarly to BO-SSD on general cases. However, this added flexibility can
likely be attributed to the objective becoming harder to optimize.
• BO-SSD-smooth addressed an assumption violation of BO with its discontinuous update rule.
The proposed smoothened update rule proved to only provide assumption compliance, but at the
expense of practical outcome.
• Adaptive SSD is a comparative approach to avoid manually setting SSD hyperparameters, and
shows promising behaviour, generally outperforming BO-SSD and its variants. Albeit, for this
kind of evaluation, as with SCRUB+R, it is diﬀicult to determine whether the approach simply
stays close to the original model or mimics a retrained model.
5.4
Exploring the Convergence of SCRUB+R
While SCRUB+R demonstrates promising unlearning capabilities as seen in section 5.1, its practical
applications depend on the stability of the optimization process. This section provides an empirical
analysis of the method’s dynamics, showing that its performance is very sensitive to the number of
unlearning epochs. Our experiments show that the min-max optimization steps, if run for too long,
lead to catastrophic forgetting, where the model’s performance on both the retain and forget sets
collapses to that of a random classifier. Furthermore, we demonstrate that the final minimization phase
is unable to recover performance once this degradation becomes significant. While the original paper
proposes a stopping criterion, halting ”when the forget error has increased without harming the retain
set error” [Kur+23], our experiments reveal the practical diﬀiculty of this approach.
The onset of
catastrophic forgetting is often abrupt, making this optimal point diﬀicult to identify before irreversible
performance degradation occurs. This challenge, compounded by the fact that the ideal number of
epochs varies with hyperparameters and dataset composition, represents a significant limitation that
inhibits the practical application of SCRUB.
5.4.1
Experiment Setup
This section outlines the experimental configuration used to analyze the convergence properties of
the SCRUB method. All experiments were conducted on the MNIST dataset, as described in subsec-
tion 2.2.1.
5.4.1.1
Model Architecture and Base Training
The base model for all unlearning tasks is a fully connected neural network, trained once and then used
as the starting point for each experiment. Its architecture, illustrated in Figure 5.24, consists of: An
input layer that takes flattened MNIST images of size 784. Three hidden layers, each with 3136 neurons
(4×784) and residual skip connections. An output layer with 10 neurons, corresponding to the number
of classes. The initial model was trained using the ADAM optimizer [KB15] with a learning rate of
10−3 and a batch size of 16.

==PAGE:94==
78
5 Experiments
5.4.1.2
Unlearning Configuration
To comprehensively evaluate the unlearning process, we constructed forget sets using two distinct strate-
gies:
• Random Sampling: A baseline approach where the forget sets were chosen uniformly at random.
We experiment with different forget set sizes.
• Targeted t-SNE Selection: A more challenging scenario where forget sets were constructed from
specific clusters in a t-SNE embedding space, as detailed in subsubsection 2.2.1.1. The precise
coordinate boundaries and characteristics of the targeted sets are detailed in Table 2.1.
5.4.1.3
Hyperparameter Settings
Our configuration for SCRUB [Kur+23] is based on the authors’ oﬀicial implementation.
Fixed parameters: We identified a key detail from the source code not specified in the main paper:
the use of a temperature-adjusted Kullback-Leibler (KL) divergence for the distillation loss [HVD14].
We adopted this detail and, for all experiments, kept the temperature fixed at T = 2, a value sourced
from the authors’ provided example code 4.
The number of final minimization steps was typically
fixed at 40 to isolate the effects of the preceding min-max optimization phase. Additionally, we fix
the learning rate to 10−3 throughout these experiments as this was found to be a sweet spot between
updating aggressively and conservatively.
Variable parameters: To investigate the algorithm’s sensitivity, we systematically varied the following
key hyperparameters across different experiments5:
1. Number of Min-Max Epochs: Varied to observe convergence and stability, and to explore whether
running only min-steps in the end can restore model performance.
2. Retain Loss Weights: The coeﬀicients for the retain loss terms were explored using the following
values: α ∈{0.05, 2, 20} and γ ∈{0.05, 2, 20}.
5.4.1.4
Evaluation
To quantify privacy leakage, we employ black-box Membership Inference Attacks (MIAs) following the
setup of Foster, Schoepf, and Brintrup [FSB24] and Kurmanji et al. [Kur+23] as outlined in subsec-
tion 3.5.2. High MIA performance on Df suggests an adversarial attacker can still distill information
about forgotten samples. Our objective is for the unlearned model to approach the MIA score of a
retrained model trained without Df.
To assess functional equivalence, we compute the Jensen-Shannon (JS) divergence between the un-
learned and retrained model predictions (See Equation 3.77). We report this divergence across the
forget set Df, the retain set Dr, and the validation set Dval.
Ideally, the unlearned model should
remain close to the retrained model on all data subsets.
We track classification accuracy on Df, Dr, and the rewind set throughout training. These trends
provide additional insight into how unlearning affects different data partitions. In particular, we verify
that the method degrades performance on Df without triggering catastrophic forgetting on Dr. Con-
vergence behavior is illustrated across forget set sizes and forget set selection strategies (random and
t-SNE-based).
4https://github.com/meghdadk/SCRUB/blob/main/small_scale_unlearning.ipynb
5Note that due to computational limits and time constraints, we have varied these parameters for the randomly
sampled forget set, while keeping them fixed for each t-SNE selected forget set.

5.4 Exploring the Convergence of SCRUB+R
79
5.4.2
Results and Discussion
5.4.2.1
Investigating the Stability and Convergence of SCRUB
Our initial experiments focus on the convergence properties of the SCRUB algorithm. We begin by
analyzing a baseline scenario with a randomly sampled forget set of size |Df| = 100 (|Dr| = 9900) and
fixed retain weights (α = 2, γ = 2).’
The Onset of Catastrophic Forgetting
Our primary finding is that the SCRUB’s min-max optimization process is inherently unstable over
extended runs, leading to catastrophic forgetting. Figure 5.25 illustrates this dynamic. It also shows
that rewinding our model from epoch 90 results in the same collapsed state. We do, however, see that
if we assume the model ends up at the state of epoch 13, it would rewind to a reasonable checkpoint.
The unlearning process initially proceeds as intended, with forget accuracy slightly decreasing while
retain accuracy remains high. However, after approximately 25-35 epochs, the model’s performance
abruptly collapses, with accuracy on both the retain and forget sets plummeting towards that of a
random classifier. This collapse seems to have two critical consequences for the SCRUB framework:
Assuming we stop training at epoch  13
Assuming we run all 90 epochs
Rewind
Rewind
Figure 5.25: SCRUB accuracy on the retain, forget, and rewind set per epoch of unlearning. The
forget set is sampled randomly with size: |Df| = 100. Parameters γ = 2 and α = 2 are used. We have
zoomed in on two areas and shown which checkpoint, assuming all epochs are checkpointed, we would
rewind to, given we stopped at a certain epoch.
• Failure of the Repair Mechanism: The final 40 min-steps are insuﬀicient to recover the model’s
performance once this degradation has occurred, demonstrating that the damage to the model’s
parameters seems irreversible. We hypothesize that this is because the unlearning process has
forced the model into a poor region of the optimization landscape from which the gradients of
the retain-set fine-tuning cannot escape. This is analogous to the well-understood importance of
network initialization [He+15].
• Failure of the Rewind Proxy: The accuracy on the rewind set Drewind, which acts as a proxy for
the retrained model’s performance, also collapses. This renders the SCRUB+R rewind mechanism
ineffective, as it can no longer identify a meaningful checkpoint when the reference metric itself
is unreliable.
This performance degradation extends beyond accuracy. As shown in Figure 5.26, other key metrics
also break down. The JS-divergence between the unlearned and retrained models explodes, and the
==PAGE:96==
80
5 Experiments
Membership Inference Attack (MIA) probability becomes highly unstable, indicating a complete failure
to mimic the retrained model.
Interestingly, the MIA probability in Figure 5.28(b) alternates between 0 and 1 around epochs 30 to
50. This can, in part, be explained by the MIA model being trained on a near perfectly balanced
dataset. Also, the predicted probabilities of the unlearned model seem to be opposite of the true labels
around this time as indicated by the Jensen-Shannon divergence in Figure 5.28(a). Since the MIA
model is trained on the Shannon entropy of the predicted probabilities, very little signal is provided to
the MIA model around this time. So, a likely explanation is that it learns to always predict either 0 or
1, depending on its initial conditions.

The Criticality of Early Stopping
Given this observed instability, we investigated early stopping as a potential solution, a principle also
noted in the original paper’s appendix [Kur+23]. Figure 5.27 provides a comparison of outcomes based
on the stopping point. When the min-max phase is halted at 30 epochs (Figure 5.27(a)), just before
the drop in performance, the subsequent minimization steps successfully restore the retain accuracy
to its original level. This creates a window where the model could be effectively unlearned. In con-
trast, stopping just five epochs later at 35 epochs (Figure 5.27(b)) is too late. The model enters the
catastrophic forgetting phase, and the repair mechanism fails, mirroring the result of the longer run in
Figure 5.25. The success of this intervention at epoch 30 is confirmed by the corresponding privacy and
divergence metrics. As seen in Figure 5.28, both the JS-divergence and MIA probability are stabilized,
demonstrating that timely early stopping preserves desired properties of the unlearned model. We do
note that the label distribution is the same across retain, forget, and rewind, and thus the model should
be able to generalize across the different datasets. In this case, the gap we see between the retain and
forget accuracy on both the unlearned and retrained models would likely be the generalization gap.
Initial Conclusion
The clear takeaway from this initial analysis is that the SCRUB method’s success is critically depen-
dent on a precise early stopping point. Without it, the process converges to a state of catastrophic
forgetting that the framework’s own repair and rewind mechanisms cannot fix. This finding raises two
fundamental questions: How can one reliably determine the optimal stopping point, especially since
it may vary with other factors? How do these dynamics change when the forget set is not randomly
sampled but has a specific, targeted structure?
5.4.2.2

Building on our finding that the SCRUB process is unstable, we next investigate how this dynamic is
influenced by the size of the forget set, |Df|. We experimented with randomly sampled forget sets of

sizes 50, 100, 200, and 500. As illustrated in Figure 5.29, our results indicate a non-monotonic, U-shaped
relationship between the forget set size and the speed of convergence. Both the smallest (|Df| = 50) and
largest (|Df| = 500) sets resulted in a slower onset of catastrophic forgetting compared to (|Df| = 100)
and (|Df| = 200). We attribute this behavior to two distinct optimization challenges at either extreme:
At one extreme, a small forget set of Nf = 50 likely hinders convergence due to gradient instability. Such
a small set provides a high-variance, noisy sample of the data to be forgotten. When minimizing the
Lscrub (see 3.42) with a batch size of 32, this set is processed in only two batches, yielding an unstable
gradient for the forget term (3.41) that provides an erratic and weak signal. At the other extreme,
a large forget set of Nf = 500 introduces a different challenge, which is likely competing gradients.
While the forget signal is strong and stable, it directly conflicts with the retain objective, as both sets
are drawn from the same data distribution. The optimizer is tasked with simultaneously learning and
unlearning similar features, which can cause it to oscillate or stall, thereby slowing convergence.
This trade-off is analogous to the well-known challenges in selecting a batch size for training. Excessively
small batches produce high-variance gradients, while very large batches can alter the optimization
dynamics and affect generalization [Kes+17]. We hypothesize that a sweet spot for the forget set size
exists, which for our setup appears to be somewhere between 50 and 500 samples.
These explanations are speculative, and a complete analysis would require further investigation. For
==PAGE:98==
82
5 Experiments
instance, plotting the loss function’s value between the min and max steps could offer deeper insight
into the optimization dynamics. However, to limit the scope of this thesis, we have deferred a more
detailed analysis.

5.4.2.3
Adjusting Hyperparameters
We next investigate how the hyperparameters α and γ influence the stability of the unlearning process.
These terms anchor the model to the retain set by weighting the KL-divergence between the original
and unlearned model for functional equivalence (α) and the cross-entropy loss for classification accuracy
(γ), respectively.
The parameter α weights the KL-divergence between the unlearned and original model’s outputs on the
retain set, promoting functional equivalence between the models. The parameter γ weights the standard
cross-entropy loss on the retain set, promoting classification accuracy. Given that our unlearning process
starts from a high-performing base model Mθorig, we hypothesized that these two objectives would have
a similar practical effect. This becomes clear when one considers how the formal definitions of the KL-
divergence and cross-entropy are related. Let p, q ∈RC denote a true and predicted probability vector,
respectively, for a classification task. The KL-divergence can then be expressed as:
DKL(p||q) =

LCE(p, q) = DKL(p||q) + H(p)
(5.3)
Note that the cross-entropy term in the SCRUB objective contains the Shannon entropy of the true
probabilities. This remains constant during unlearning, and if it contains hard probabilities, as is often
the case, the Shannon entropy becomes zero.
Letting q(orig) denote the original model’s predicted
probabilities, we can write the KL-divergence term being minimized in the scrub loss as:
DKL(q(orig)||q) =

these become equal, we get that
LCE(p, q) = DKL(qorig||q)
(5.5)
So the gap between the two terms being minimized in SCRUB is directly tied to how close the original
model’s predicted probabilities are to the true probabilities.
==PAGE:99==

83
For a model that is already accurate, preserving its high-confidence outputs (the goal of the α term)
inherently preserves its high accuracy (the goal of the γ term).
Therefore, we predicted that the
model’s resistance to the forget objective would be primarily determined by the combined magnitude
of the retaining force (α + γ), rather than the specific individual values. This is supported by the plots
shown in Figure 5.30. Where we see that
• High Combined Weight: Configurations with a similar, high combined weight, such as α = 20, γ =
2 and α = 2, γ = 20, show similar dynamics. The configuration with the largest combined weight,
α = 20, γ = 20, demonstrated the most prolonged resistance to forgetting.
• Low Combined Weight: In contrast, configurations with a low combined weight, like α = 0.05, γ =
2) and α = 2, γ = 0.05), succumbed to performance degradation significantly earlier whereas the
case with almost no retaining force, i.e. α = 0.05, γ = 0.05), starts degrading almost instantly.
Although it takes a bit longer to degrade.
This analysis demonstrates that the α and γ hyperparameters influence the stability of the objective.
Higher values, weighing the retaining force more, seem to result in delayed catastrophic forgetting.

The insights from this analysis lead to several interesting questions. In these experiments, we start
from a high-performing model, which raises the question: How would SCRUB perform on an initially
suboptimal model? In such a scenario, the α-term, enforcing equivalence to a flawed ”teacher,” might
directly conflict with the γ-term, which would try to improve the model’s base accuracy. Additionally,
we have the final term seeking to destroy the functional equivalence on the forget set. Exploring this
tension could reveal deeper insights into the method’s robustness.
This also touches on a more philosophical point about the goals of unlearning. We currently prioritize
maintaining functional equivalence, but should an unlearning process be allowed to correct errors in the
original model? One could envision an unlearning framework that not only forgets specified data but
also improves the model’s overall performance on the retain set.
Again, the main takeaway is that the unlearning method converges towards catastrophic forgetting and
a collapse of both the forget and retain performance. Additionally, the hyperparameters influence when
this happens, possibly making it harder to select when to stop the unlearning.
==PAGE:100==
84
5 Experiments
5.4.2.4
Evaluating SCRUB on Targeted Forget Sets
Our previous experiments demonstrated SCRUB’s instability on randomly sampled forget sets, where
the forget and retain data share the same underlying distribution. We now take a look at SCRUB’s
robustness in scenarios involving targeted forget requests meant to be more realistic. We draw three
forget sets by choosing an area in the t-SNE embedding space as described in subsubsection 2.2.1.1.
Plots of the datasets and their class distributions can be found in Table 2.1. We analyze the three
distinct forget sets constructed, with the unlearning dynamics for each shown in Figure 5.31.
1. t-SNE Forget Set 1: High Feature Overlap (Figure 5.31(a)). The first forget set contains
data points where two classes (4 and 9) have significant feature overlap (in the t-SNE embedding
space). The resulting unlearning dynamic shows a drop in performance, consistent with our earlier
findings.
2. t-SNE Forget Set 2: Class Outliers (Figure 5.31(b)). The second forget set is composed
of what are likely outliers from several classes. This scenario is particularly insightful because
the retrained model generalizes poorly to this forget set (achieving <80% accuracy). Initially, the
unlearned model maintains high retain and rewind accuracy, suggesting it can forget distinguish-
able outliers without immediate impact on retain. However, here the SCRUB+R rewind proxy
fails. The rewind accuracy tracks the high retain accuracy, not the low accuracy of the retrained
model on the forgotten outliers, before it eventually collapses with the rest of the model.
3. t-SNE Forget Set 3: Specific Class Subset (Figure 5.31(c)). The third forget set targets
a specific subset on the edge of a single class cluster (class 6). This leads to highly unstable and
oscillatory behavior in the rewind accuracy during unlearning. We hypothesize this occurs because
the rewind validation set contains samples from the entirety of class 6, while the unlearning process
is narrowly focused on destroying features specific to the targeted subset. At this point, we do
not have a better explanation of the fluctuation in the rewind accuracy, and it is something that
requires further exploration.

Across the experiments with targeted forget sets, we observe that the accuracies on both the forget and
rewind datasets consistently drop to zero. This is likely explained by the fact that the forget set now
has a distribution distinct enough from the retain set for the model to learn to perfectly misclassify the
forget samples, rather than merely degrading to random performance as seen in the initial experiments.
The main takeaway, therefore, is that the eventual collapse into catastrophic forgetting is a consistent
failure mode, even when tested on these varied forget set heuristics. This indicates that the practical
stability issues of SCRUB are not just an artifact of using a randomly sampled forget set, but are rather
a more general limitation of the method’s optimization process.
==PAGE:101==
5.5 Developing a Novel Alternative to the SCRUB Objective (Teacher Ascent)
85
5.4.3
Conclusion
Our analysis of the SCRUB framework, conducted across varied forget set sizes, hyperparameters, and
data structures, reveals several limitations that challenge its practical application. We can summarize
our findings in two main points:
• The most consistent finding is that SCRUB’s min-max optimization process invariably leads to
catastrophic forgetting without a precise early stopping point. While the original paper suggests
stopping when ”forget error has increased without harming the retain set error”, our experiments
show this point is diﬀicult to pinpoint in practice.
The performance collapse is often abrupt
and irreversible, and its onset is highly sensitive to both the forget set size and the α and γ
hyperparameters. This makes defining a reliable stopping point challenging.
• The SCRUB+R rewind mechanism, a key component for privacy-sensitive applications, is shown
to be unreliable in the case of the t-SNE forget set 2 (Figure 5.31(b)).
The retrained model
generalized poorly, but the rewind proxy failed to reflect this, instead tracking the high retain
accuracy.
This indicates that the proxy might not be robust for some targeted, non-uniform
unlearning requests.
It is important to note that these observations, particularly for the targeted forget sets, are based on
a single run for each scenario and should be considered preliminary. Nonetheless, the patterns are
consistent.
In summary, while SCRUB is a conceptually powerful method, its practical utility is hindered by a
brittle optimization process converging towards catastrophic forgetting, and a rewind mechanism that
is not robust in all cases. To address this convergence towards catastrophic forgetting, we propose a
novel method called Teacher Ascent, which is described in section 4.2 and analyzed in the following
section 5.5.
5.5
Developing a Novel Alternative to the SCRUB Objective
(Teacher Ascent)
As described in section 5.4, a significant limitation of the SCRUB objective is its convergence towards
catastrophic forgetting, making it contingent on a precise early stopping heuristic. To address this
instability, we propose Teacher Ascent, a novel unlearning method. As described in section 4.2, our
approach is inspired by the work of Kirkpatrick et al. [Kir+17], which uses the FIM in its EWC-
regularization (see subsection 3.4.1) to prevent catastrophic forgetting in continual learning, along with
the finetuning approach introduced in SCRUB+R.
This section outlines the empirical development of TA. We evaluate the series of formulations presented
in section 4.2, showing the iterative progression that led to the final objective. We demonstrate that TA
successfully separates the model’s performance by degrading accuracy on the forget set while preserving
it on the retain set, avoiding the convergence issues inherent to SCRUB.
5.5.1
Experiment setup
To ensure a direct and fair comparison with the SCRUB baseline, we evaluate Teacher Ascent using an
identical experimental framework to the one described in section 5.4.
5.5.1.1
Model Architecture and Base Training
We use the same model architecture (Figure 5.24), initial training protocol, and forget set configurations
as in the previous experiments.
5.5.1.2
Unlearning Configuration
The forget set configurations are the same as in section 5.4.
==PAGE:102==
86
5 Experiments
5.5.1.3
Hyperparameter Settings
Our TA objective introduces a single hyperparameter, the regularization strength λ. We set λ = 2 for
all main experiments. As demonstrated in Appendix A.7.2, the method’s performance is robust to this
choice.
5.5.2
Results and Discussion
5.5.2.1
Developing an EWC Inspired Unlearning Objective
As detailed in section 4.2, our goal was to develop a stable unlearning objective using an Elastic
Weight Consolidation (EWC)-inspired [Kir+17] regularization. This section presents the results from
our iterative development process.
Our initial formulations, which combined the EWC regularizarion with either maximizing cross-entropy
LT A1 or Shannon entropy LT A2 on Df, proved ineffective. As shown in Figure 5.32(a) and Figure 5.32(b),
both approaches failed to prevent catastrophic forgetting, with the model’s accuracy on the retain set
collapsing to random. This suggested that the ascent term was overpowering the regularization. To
counteract this, we introduced a cross-entropy minimization term on a single batch of retain data. This
objective, LT A3, successfully mitigated immediate collapse but still resulted in significant degradation
of retain set performance (Figure 5.32(c)).

Each version’s objective is described in section 4.2. We set λ = 2 and use a randomly sampled forget
set of |Df| = 100.
The final breakthrough came from refining the EWC regularization term itself. Inspired by SSD we
replaced the standard FIM with the ratio ˆi(Dr)
j
/ˆi(Df )
j
. This modification, combined with the retain batch
and entropy ascent, yielded our final Teacher Ascent objective (4.7). As Figure 5.33(a) demonstrates,
this formulation successfully destroys performance on the forget set while preserving high accuracy on
the retain set.
We hypothesized that neurons contributing similarly to both Df and Dr might be ”sacrificed” more
readily. Conversely, neurons with a significantly higher information content for Dr compared to Df
should exhibit reduced plasticity.
Despite its success, the final method exhibits signs of ”over-unlearning.” The forget set accuracy of the
unlearned model drops significantly below that of a model retrained from scratch (Figure 5.33(a)). This
is confirmed by the MIA probability (Figure 5.33(b)) and a high JS-divergence on the forget set outputs
(Figure 5.33(c)). Ideally, the unlearned model’s behavior on the forget set should closely resemble that
of a model never exposed to this data (i.e., the retrained model). This divergence suggests a potential
privacy concern, as the unlearning process might make the forgotten samples suspiciously distinct.
To confirm the necessity of our refined EWC term, an ablation study with λ = 0 was performed, which
showed that the method is ineffective without it (see Appendix A.7.2).
5.5.2.2

We further tested TA by varying the size of the forget set. We see in Figure 5.34 that the method seems
to be able to achieve lower performance on the forget set, as the forget set size increases. We see that

5.5.2.3
Different Forget Sets
Next, we evaluated TA on the three structured t-SNE forget sets (Figure A.10).
The results vary
depending on the data characteristics. For the first set, which contains two overlapping classes, forget
accuracy is volatile (Figure A.10(a)). The forget set is likely placed on a decision boundary of the
original model, which may increase conflict between the terms being minimized and maximized in the
TA objective (4.7).
For the second set (Figure A.10(b)), forget accuracy drops close to 20%, near that of random perfor-
mance. Interestingly, we see that the retrained model on this forget set achieves 77% accuracy, much
lower than for the other two t-SNE sets.
However, the forget accuracy of the unlearned model is
more stable compared to the first forget set. This indicates that while the second set lies in a region
where multiple classes are close to each other (Table 2.1), entropy maximization conflicts less with the
remaining objective terms compared to the first forget set.
For the third set, which is a visually distinct and isolated cluster, TA achieves almost 0% accuracy on
the forget set (Figure 5.35(c)). The homogeneity of these samples may make it easier for the model to
isolate their features and unlearn them without impacting the retain set. One thing to note is that for
these datasets, the forget accuracy seems to converge much faster than for the previous random forget
samples. This might indicate that they are easier to distinguish and are likely separated more in the
representation in the network. Again, the retain accuracy stays high, while the rewind set accuracy still
seems to be a bad proxy.

retrained model behaved in these cases. See Figure 5.36.

Figure 5.37: Decision boundaries after retraining the model for cases 1, 2, and 3.
The results of applying TA on each of the cases are seen in Figure 5.37.
Focusing on case 2 (Fig-
ure 5.37(b)), we see that the decision boundary for the purple class becomes smaller in an attempt to
==PAGE:105==
5.6 Experimenting on benchmark data - MNIST
89
forget the purple points, with the decision boundaries of the red and yellow classes creeping up. This
is most likely due to the λ hyperparameter being too low, and not protecting the retain performance
enough. The model is not able to lower the accuracy on the forget set, which is likely attributed to
the limited capacity of the shallow network. We hypothesize that a more capable network could make
a more complex decision boundary and surgically remove the correct classification of the purple points.
This is pure speculation that would have to be confirmed.
In the adversarial case 3 (Figure 5.37(c)), we see that the decision boundary for the red class is decreased
in the same manner as for case 2. Since the unlearning term in the TA loss function is the negative
Shannon entropy on forget set predictions, the resemblance with case 2 behaviour is expected. While
the original model may have lower uncertainty on red observations in case 3, we are guiding the model
towards predicting uniform labels on the forget set at unlearning time. As such, during unlearning,
these two cases can be interpreted in the same manner.
While these two examples are far from the retrained model, it does not break completely, as we, for
example, saw for the proposed BO SSD method. This could indicate that the objective is balanced
enough to retain some performance.
Considering case 1 (Figure 5.37(a)), we see a left shift in the decision boundary between the purple and
red classes after unlearning, which aligns with retraining behavior. However, the change in decision
boundary is slightly more pronounced compared to that of retraining.
Comparing this to what we observed for the SCRUB method, where we saw better results on cases 2
and 3, we restate our concerns as to whether unlearning was actually achieved or the model just stays
close to the original model. It would be interesting to look into how the representation of the network
has changed and maybe come up with a good metric for this. We see this as a crucial step for confirming
unlearning.
Most of these interpretations are speculations, as it is hard to tell which parts of the network are
responsible for which parts of the decision boundary, and how entangled the feature representations are.
An interesting avenue to explore would be splines, which have been effectively shown to give a visual
interpretation of how the decision boundary evolves throughout each layer of the network [BB18].
5.5.2.5
Summary and Future Directions
These experiments show that we have successfully developed an objective, Teacher Ascent, that unlearns
effectively while preventing catastrophic forgetting. Unlike objectives that require careful early stopping
to avoid model collapse, TA converges to a desirable state where performance on the forget set is
degraded while performance on the retain set is preserved.
We argue that this stability makes TA a better starting point than an objective like SCRUB, which
converges towards catastrophic forgetting. However, our method faces its own challenge: the issue of
”over-unlearning”. The unlearned model becomes suspiciously effective at misclassifying the forget set,
creating a statistical anomaly that differs from a truly retrained model.
This observation points toward several avenues for future work. The most critical is to find methods to
calibrate the final unlearned state, for instance by implementing a decay schedule on the ascent term
or establishing a principled early-stopping criterion to prevent over-unlearning.
Another key challenge is verification; the rewind set proved to be a poor proxy for retrained model
performance, highlighting the need for more robust evaluation techniques, perhaps by analyzing the
model’s internal representations. Finally, further exploration is needed to understand how TA performs
on models with lower capacity, where the ability to neatly separate performance on retain and forget
sets may be diminished.
5.6
Experimenting on benchmark data - MNIST
Following the controlled experiments and the development of novel unlearning methods, we now conduct
a benchmark to compare these techniques on a benchmark dataset.
==PAGE:106==
90
5 Experiments
This experiment focuses on the MNIST dataset with a simple FFNN, while section 5.7 will extend the
analysis to a ViT trained on the more complex CIFAR-10 dataset.
The primary goal is to evaluate the practical effectiveness and consistency of these unlearning methods.
We seek to determine whether the qualitative behaviours observed in our controlled studies persist on
MNIST with a larger model. To this end, we quantify the fidelity and eﬀiciency of each technique using
key metrics, such as accuracy, MIA-probability, and execution time, and compare the results against
the gold standard retraining baseline.
5.6.1
Experiment Setup
5.6.1.1
Model Architecture and Base Training
This experiment utilizes the five-layer FFNN with ReLU activations and residual skip connections,
as described in section 5.4 and depicted in Figure 5.24. The hidden layers have a dimensionality of
3136 = 4 · 784. This specific architecture is required by the SAE method but does not inhibit other
techniques. For the SISA method, its constituent models employ the same architecture but with a
reduced hidden layer dimensionality of 392. The original and retrained models are both trained for 20
epochs, and the final model is selected based on the one with the highest validation accuracy.
A special consideration was made for Amnesiac unlearning due to storage limitations. During its initial
training phase, we only stored parameter differences for batches that contained one or more samples
from the forget set. Consequently, the reported training time for the ’Amnesiac original’ model is lower
than it would be in a real-world scenario. To ensure a fair comparison, we programmatically verified
that the weights of this model were identical to the original model used for the other MU methods
before any unlearning was applied.
5.6.1.2
Unlearning Configuration
The experiment is conducted on the subset of the MNIST dataset as described in subsection 2.2.1. To
construct the forget set, Df, we randomly sample 100 images from the model’s training set. Data
preprocessing is performed following subsection 2.2.1.
5.6.1.3
Hyperparameter Settings and Implementation Details
To ensure robust results, each experiment was repeated 10 times with different random seeds. Except
SISA, all unlearning methods are applied to the same, single pre-trained original model. All hyperpa-
rameters used for the unlearning methods are detailed in Table 5.2.
5.6.1.4
Evalutaion
The performance of the unlearning methods is evaluated using several key metrics: Accuracy on the
retain, forget, and validation sets, MIA probability, and execution time. Furthermore, we measure the
JS-divergence between the output distributions of the unlearned model and those of the original and
retrained models. This quantifies the extent to which unlearning has altered the model’s predictions.
Key results are visualized using bar plots for direct comparison, with a full results table available in
Appendix A.8.1.
5.6.2
Results and Discussion
Our analysis begins by establishing the performance of the two key baselines: The original model
and a retrained model.
The original model demonstrates significant performance, achieving nearly
identical, near-perfect accuracy on both the retain and forget sets (Figure 5.39). This performance is
further confirmed by a high MIA probability on the forget set (Figure 5.40). The retrained model’s
performance on the forget set aligns closely with its performance on the validation set. This behavior
is expected, as the model generalises to two unseen datasets from the same distribution.
We also verified the initial states of the method-specific model variants. The Amnesiac original and SAE
original models perform identically to the main original baseline across all datasets. This is anticipated,

Amnesiac unlearning with a repair phase seems the most effective method for replicating the retrained
model’s performance. This outcome aligns with our earlier findings in section 5.1 and is evident by its

forget set accuracy (Figure 5.38(a)) and MIA probability (Figure 5.40(a)), both of which are close to
the retrained baseline.
However, this eﬀicacy comes at a prohibitive computational cost. The method’s core requirement, stor-
ing parameter differences for potential rollbacks, introduces substantial overhead from I/O operations,
as the updates must be continuously saved to and loaded from disk. This makes both the initial model
training and the unlearning process significantly slower than retraining (Figure 5.41). To illustrate the
scale of this issue, preliminary tests showed that tracking updates for just 100 samples on a standard
ResNet can require nearly 50 GB of storage. As mentioned earlier, during the original model training
for Amnesiac unlearning, we only store parameter differences when the batch contains one or more
samples in the forget set. In other words, we know which points to forget before training the model.
Comparing Amnesiac with and without its repair phase, it is evident that the method suffers significantly
without it. Simply rolling back the parameter updates without repair severely degrades model utility,
causing accuracy on the retain, forget, and validation sets to collapse below the retrained baseline
(Figure 5.38). This demonstrates that the repair stage is not an optional refinement but an essential
component for the method to function. In combination with the heavy I/O operations, not only is it
a computationally infeasible unlearning method, but we also have to repair the model for it to work.
Furthermore, the repair mechanism is not described in detail in the original paper, but was discovered in
the source code. The requirement of a repair phase points toward the potential of a method employing
a sort of contrastive loss function, simulating the rollback of gradients and repairing some neurons
concurrently, as done in both SCRUB+R and our novel TA method.

The SISA method, by design, starts from a different baseline than the other methods. Its initial model,
an ensemble of weaker learners trained on disjoint data shards, naturally exhibits lower accuracy than
the single, larger original model (Figure 5.39).
Furthermore, SISA has a significantly lower MIA-
probability.
This is expected since any given forget sample was only seen by one of these models,
the averaged output distribution has higher entropy, making the model inherently more robust to
==PAGE:109==
5.6 Experimenting on benchmark data - MNIST
93
Membership-Inference Attack. However, the goal of unlearning is to replicate a model that was retrained
from scratch. By producing a model with fundamentally different and much lower privacy risk, SISA
becomes ’exempt’ from an accuracy and MIA comparison.
Despite this, the practicality of SISA is challenged by significant computational costs and evaluation
complexities. The unlearning time is comparable to the entire initial training time (Figure 5.41), a
consequence of the large number of samples to be forgotten relative to the sharding configuration
(Table 5.2). These time estimates are generous, as the SISA constituent models were trained with an
early stopping criterion (5.1), unlike the baselines, which were trained for a fixed number of epochs. A
consistent training protocol would likely increase SISA’s execution time beyond that of retraining from
scratch.
For SSD, we generally see that the unlearned model remains close to the original model when looking
at the accuracy and MIA (Figure 5.38 and Figure 5.40). Furthermore, the JS-divergence (Figure 5.42)
confirms that the unlearned model remains significantly closer to the original than to the retrained
baseline. We hypothesize that this failure is due to minimal parameter dampening. Because the forget
set is a random sample of the training data, the estimated FIM is too similar for the retain and forget
sets. Consequently, the importance ratio ˆi(Df )
j
/ˆi(D)
j
rarely exceeds the threshold of α = 5, leaving most
parameters unaltered.
One might assume this issue could be remedied by simply selecting a better α; however, the consistent
failure across all SSD variants, including those using BO, suggests the problem is more fundamental.
For larger networks, the dampening plots used in section 5.2 become diﬀicult to interpret. We therefore
suggest that future work should include a quantitative metric, like the L2 norm of the weight differences,
to assess how much the representation has changed. Interestingly, BO-SSD smooth has much lower
average MIA-probability compared to the other variants with a notably higher standard deviation as
well. However, the accuracy across seeds remain close to the original model. This suggests that the
method increases the entropy of the predicted probabilities on the forget set. However, the increase is
generally not substantial enough to change the class prediction. A plausible explanation for this is the
smooth update rule. Specifically, it is less selective compared to the original SSD update criteria and
has capacity to slightly dampen many of the model parameters. This will make the logits smaller in
magnitude, which makes the predicted probabilities more uniform, similar to temperature scaling. The
large deviation in MIA-probability could therefore be caused by some runs dampening many parameters.
We would have to study this further to conclusively determine that it is the reason.
For ASSD specifically, the failure can likely be attributed to a violation of its core assumptions. The
method is designed for datasets where class frequencies and frequencies of sub-populations within classes
follow a long-tailed distribution [Fel20]. The MNIST dataset, however, is well-balanced and contains
visually homogeneous classes, meaning the statistical conditions ASSD relies upon to identify atypical
or influential data points are absent.
In summary, none of the SSD variants performed effective unlearning in this benchmark. This collective
failure strongly suggests that synaptic dampening methods are fundamentally ill-suited for the common
unlearning scenario where the forget request concerns a random data sample. In such cases, the statis-
tical differences between retain and forget sets, which these methods require to isolate information, do
not exist.
The SAE method, which relies on dampening in a learned feature space, shows a similar lack of eﬀi-
cacy to the SSD variants. The unlearned model remains functionally close to the original, indicating
that minimal forgetting has occurred.
The explanation is likely analogous: Features extracted by
the autoencoder exhibit significant overlap across the retain and forget sets, which likely prevents the
feature-dampening condition in (4.14) from being satisfied for most features.
Interestingly, applying SAE dampening did have a noticeable effect on the MIA probability, reducing
the model’s prediction confidence on the forget set. However, this effect was not substantial enough to
alter the final class predictions, thus leaving accuracy scores unchanged.
As mentioned, the SAE model before unlearning does not seem to alter the original model’s performance
substantially either (Figure 5.39). It does, however, come with notable computational overhead. One
could argue that training an SAE to approximate model activations does serve other purposes besides
unlearning. As such, this cost is not as inhibiting as methods like amnesiac unlearning or SISA.

Figure 5.42: MNIST benchmarking: JS-divergence between the unlearned model and the original
