So far these results might indicate that for this dataset and a randomly drawn forget set, ”surgically”
changing the neurons in the network might not be the best approach as SSD and SAE unlearning both
seem to not be able to find ways to change the original model enough towards forgetting the forget
set. One possible interpretation is that the deep entanglement of information between the retain and
forget sets could complicate efforts to surgically isolate or exactly reverse the influence of specific data
points. Such an entanglement might explain why the dampening approaches (SSD and SAE) struggled
to identify distinct parameters for modification, and it could also be a factor in why the exact update
reversal of Amnesiac unlearning appeared to corrupt model performance, necessitating a repair phase.
An interesting experiment would be to test the methods on the t-SNE datasets to see if the methods
would be able to unlearn better in this setting. Due to time constraints, we defer this to future work.
An approach centered on fine-tuning like SCRUB+R or TA, which aims to actively update learned
information rather than precisely remove it, presents a promising alternative. To further probe the
entanglement hypothesis, future experiments could also test the surgical methods on datasets where
information is designed to be more separable, such as the clustered t-SNE data seen in section 5.4.
However, such an investigation is deferred to future work.
When looking at SCRUB+R we see in Figure 5.38(a) that its forget accuracy resembles that of amnesiac
unlearning with repair and thus overlaps with the performance of the retrained model. The same is
the case for the MIA as seen in Figure 5.40. One thing to note here is that SCRUB was only run for
6 total rounds, 4 of which were forget error maximization steps (Table 5.2). As we did not implement
any automatic early stopping, we chose these hyperparameters based on some preliminary runs. It
might be very likely that we could have run it for more rounds as shown in subsection 3.3.3 before the
model starts catastrophically forgetting. However, by looking at the time consumption of SCRUB+R
in Figure 5.41, it is not far from that of a retrained model, suggesting that more steps would likely not
be worth it compared to retraining. This could be attributed to the fact that each epoch in SCRUB+R
requires the model to iterate over the whole retain set, just as with retraining. Therefore, running
SCRUB+R for around 20 rounds would likely not show any time advantage over retraining.
Here we see a much better time usage for TA, not far from that of the dampening methods, which
do not require any fine-tuning. This is due to the fact that we only iterate over one batch from the
retain set for each epoch. We have not tested extensively how this would work if the dataset were more
complex and the model less capable. In this case, a single batch of the retain data per epoch might not
be suﬀicient.
Moreover, the retain and validation accuracies are generally lower compared to the retrained model’s.
One reason could be that we have run very few epochs, and the loss objective has not yet stabilized.
Although based on a single run, from previous experiments Figure 5.33(a) there are indications that the
retain accuracy initially drops before recovering. This would require further investigation. We also see
that forget accuracy and MIA vary a lot across each run. It would be interesting to work with learning
rate scheduling, lowering the learning rate as we near the objective, so as not to take as big gradient
steps when we get closer to the optimum. As also mentioned in section 5.5, the method could have
==PAGE:111==
5.7 Experimenting on benchmark data - CIFAR-10
95
potential given the fact that it does not converge towards catastrophic forgetting, but some issues have
to be addressed, such as the high variance, slight drop in retain accuracy, and over-unlearning.
5.6.2.1
Part conclusion
We find that none of the methods is universally superior to the others. Amnesiac unlearning and SISA
are both infeasible due to their adjusted original, high time consumption, and, in the case of SISA, weak
performance.
For all the SSD-based dampening methods, we find that the model does not change much from the
original model, which might suggest that not enough dampening happens. We attribute this to the
forget set, which is drawn from the same distribution as the retain set, possibly entangling the internal
feature representation too much to only dampen. SAE unlearning does not, suggesting the SAE is not
able to distinguish the features of the forget and retain sets.
Based on these observations, the most promising methods seem to be SCRUB+R, although we still
keep in mind that it has to be stopped early and as it will otherwise converge towards catastrophic
forgetting.
Our novel method TA has much higher variance across experiments and shows clear signs of over-
unlearning. Although not an ideal unlearning method, we see great potential in the method, given its
better convergence characteristics, i.e., the starting point could be argued to be better than that of
SCRUB+R.
5.7
Experimenting on benchmark data - CIFAR-10
Although MNIST is significantly more complex than the synthetic dataset, most literature compares
performance on larger and more complex real-life datasets, along with larger models. One of the most
common datasets is CIFAR-10. Hence, for this experiment, the goals remain the same, but we swap
the classic neural network for a Vision Transformer and MNIST for CIFAR-10.
5.7.1
Experiment setup
Due to the infeasibility of Amnesiac Unlearning and SISA as shown in the previous benchmarking
experiment section 5.6 and the fact that they require us to train the baseline model in a specific way,
we have left out these two methods from the analysis.
5.7.1.1
Model Architecture and Base Training
The model is a pre-trained vision transformer as described in subsection 3.1.3. We chose this architecture
because preliminary experiments showed that the neural network used for the MNIST experiments
(subsubsection 5.6.1.1) could not generalize well to CIFAR-10. Additionally, since the SAE expects a
residual stream as its input, we needed a model architecture that includes residual skip connections
to ensure a fair comparison. The specific model we use is the tiny variant6 presented in [Dos+21]. It
consists of 12 encoder blocks, a latent dimension, dh, of 192, 3 attention heads, a patch size of 16, and
the hidden dimension of the FFNN within each encoder block is 768.
The original and retrained models were both fine-tuned on the dataset for 20 epochs. The final model
was chosen as the one with the highest validation accuracy. Both models were trained with the AdamW
optimizer [LH19] with a learning rate of 10−4, weight decay set to 10−3. A cosine annealing learning
rate scheduler was used during fine-tuning with the period set to T = 20 epochs. We repeated the
experiment 10 times with different seeds. As such, the initial weights of the classification head, the
forget set, and the order in which batches occur vary for each experiment.
6https://huggingface.co/WinKawaks/vit-tiny-patch16-224
==PAGE:112==

The dataset used in this experiment is CIFAR-10.

preprocessing steps can be found in subsection 2.2.2. Similar to section 5.6, we draw a forget set of 100
randomly sampled images.

5.7.2
Evaluation
The evaluation methodology is identical to that used in the MNIST benchmark (section 5.6). We assess
each unlearning method against the original and retrained baselines across key metrics: Accuracy, MIA
probability, JS-divergence, and execution time. The objective remains to replicate the performance of
the retrained model. We mostly use bar plots for the evaluation, while a table of the results can be
found in Appendix A.8.2.
5.7.3
Results and discussion
Similar to the MNIST results, we see in Figure 5.44 that the original model has near-perfect performance
on both the retain and forget sets. The performance on the validation set is slightly lower, and the
retrained model’s accuracy on the forget set, as seen in Figure 5.43(a), is nearly that of the original
model’s validation accuracy. Again, expected as the forget set comes from the same distribution as the
training and validation sets. We do, however, see that some of the retrained models have nearly 100%
accuracy on the forget set, suggesting that sometimes the model is lucky and gets 100 easy samples to
classify. This will not happen with the validation performance, as the set is much larger. The retrained
model also shows a lower MIA compared to the original model as seen in Figure 5.45.
Generally, most SSD-based models remain too close to the original model, as shown by their high forget
accuracies in Figure 5.43 and their JS-divergences for both forget and retain in Figure 5.47(a) and
Figure 5.47(b). The standard SSD proves to be an exception, but it is highly unstable. With fixed
hyperparameters α = 5, λ = 3, it causes catastrophic forgetting, degrading performance across all data
subsets. This brittleness and high variance are attributed to the critical role of hyperparameters and
the random sampling of the forget set, where different seeds can lead to vastly different outcomes. We
also have to remember that we now have a much larger ViT model, and this α might result in too many
neurons being dampened.

The BO-SSD variants fail for a more fundamental reason. As confirmed by their high MIA-probabilities
(Figure 5.45) and near-zero JS-Divergence from the original model (Figure 5.47(a)), these methods
induce almost no change. This makes sense given their optimization objective. The BO seeks hyperpa-
rameters that preserve the original model’s behavior on a rewind set, Drewind. Since the forget set is
random, this rewind set is statistically identical to the data the model has already generalized well to.
Consequently, the optimization process consistently concludes that the optimal strategy is to leave the
high-performing original model unaltered.
While ASSD emerges as the best-performing SSD variant, it is ultimately too conservative. It’s for-
get accuracy and MIA-probability (0.94 ± 0.03) remain noticeably higher than the retrained model’s
(0.88 ± 0.04), and its low JS-Divergence score between the unlearnd and original model (Figure 5.47)
indicates that it does not suﬀiciently alter the model’s predictions. These results suggest that while
some unlearning occurs, the parameter updates are too small to replicate the state of a truly retrained
model, and maybe the model neurons are too entangled for dampening to work in this case. As with
the MNIST experiments, it could also point towards the assumption of class frequencies and frequencies
of sub-populations within classes following a long-tailed distribution not being met.
Prior to unlearning, the introduction of the SAE does not substantially alter the original model’s
behavior, indicating that the autoencoder accurately reconstructs the intermediate model activations.
However, in line with our MNIST observations, training the SAE introduces a significant computational
overhead, as shown in Figure 5.46.
Furthermore, the unlearning itself, which involves dampening
features in the SAE representation, offers no speed advantage over the SSD methods. The method’s
eﬀiciency could be improved by modifying the importance weight calculation in (4.14). Similarly to
SSD where ˆi
(D) is used instead of ˆi
(D∇) for eﬀiciency purposes, one could pre-compute the mean feature
activations for the entire training set.
Upon applying SAE unlearning, the model’s performance degrades significantly across all metrics. The
MIA-probability drops to 0.69 ± 0.05, accompanied by notable decreases in both retain accuracy 0.81 ±
0.03 and forget accuracy 0.77 ± 0.06. While the precise cause requires further investigation, the results

suggest that the dampening process excessively inhibits the model. The high JS-divergence from both
the original and retrained models, combined with the uniform drop across all accuracies, indicates
that the method does not selectively forget but rather causes widespread damage. A likely culprit
is the dampening hyperparameter α, though a more thorough investigation into the learned sparse
representations would be necessary to draw a definitive conclusion.
Another possible flaw of the method is that we only dampen on a single layer, and we might be unlucky
to have a layer of activations that is harder to separate for the SAE. Much research suggests that
networks learn circuits [Ola+20] and dampening only in a single layer would inherently make it harder
to modify structures across multiple layers of the network.
Mechanistic interpretability is still a very active field of research and the use of SAE’s is still a quite
new idea in this usecase [Lea+25], not to mention that we do not know of anyone, who has tried to
combine it with synaptic dampening in the context of unlearning.

For SCRUB+R we see a drop in both the forget accuracy (0.91 ± 0.01) along with the retain accuracy
(0.95±0.01) and validation accuracy (0.89±0.01). Additionally, we see a MIA-probability of 0.80±0.02,
which is slightly lower than that of the retrained model. This indicates that we break the model slightly.
We hypothesize that this performance drop is not an inherent flaw in the method but rather a conse-
quence of suboptimal hyperparameter selection. The learning rate and number of training rounds were
chosen based on a few initial trials rather than a rigorous tuning process, likely leading to this slight
model degradation. This highlights a critical challenge in practice: a method’s eﬀicacy is deeply tied
to the diﬀiculty of finding its optimal hyperparameters, a factor that complicates direct comparisons
between unlearning approaches.
Furthermore, the computational cost of SCRUB+R is considerable, with an execution time approaching
that of full retraining, as seen in Figure 5.46. This is expected, as the method iterates over the entire
retain set in each epoch. Consequently, its primary advantage in terms of eﬀiciency relies on its ability
to unlearn in significantly fewer epochs than would be required for retraining from scratch.

((b)) Retain JS-divergence.
Figure 5.47: CIFAR-10 benchmarking: JS-divergence between the unlearned model and the original
model (how much has unlearning changed the model?), and JS-divergence between the unlearned model
and the retrained model.
On CIFAR-10, our novel TA method demonstrates improved stability and performance compared to
the MNIST experiments, which we partly attribute to a lower learning rate and an increased number
of epochs. The method successfully replicates the retrained model’s performance on the retain and
validation sets. Furthermore, its forget set accuracy (0.94±0.06) and MIA-probability (0.89±0.08) are
closely aligned with the retrained baseline’s (0.98 ± 0.02 and 0.88 ± 0.04, respectively).
However, these results represent a delicate balance.
As shown in our earlier analysis (section 5.5),
running TA to full convergence would likely lead to ”over-forgetting,” where performance on the forget
set drops well below the retrained target. The number of epochs was intentionally limited to mitigate
this, but the high standard deviations suggest that some experimental runs degrade performance more
rapidly than others, indicating a need for a more principled early stopping criterion.
A significant advantage of TA is its computational eﬀiciency. As seen in Figure 5.46, its execution
time is substantially lower than that of SCRUB+R and is only surpassed by the far less effective SSD
variants, making it a very promising and practical alternative.
5.7.4
Part conclusion
In this experiment on CIFAR-10 with a Vision Transformer, the fine-tuning methods proved more
effective than surgical dampening approaches, though no method proved superior.
Similar to the MNIST benchmarking results, none of the SSD variants seem to work very well. Standard
SSD was unstable and led to catastrophic forgetting, the BO-SSD variants induced no change due to
a flawed optimization objective for this scenario, and ASSD, while the best of the three, was too
conservative to match the retrained baseline. This failure might suggest that synaptic dampening is
ill-suited for unlearning random data points where information is entangled.
The SAE-based approach also failed, causing significant, widespread damage to the model’s performance
rather than selectively unlearning. This is likely due to a combination of suboptimal hyperparameters
or the method just not being a good approach for unlearning.
The SCRUB+R also seemed to degrade the overall model performance on the retain and validation
sets, likely due to a sensitive and non-rigorous hyperparameter selection. Its high computational cost,
approaching that of full retraining, also limits its practical advantage.
Our novel TA method emerged as the most promising approach. It successfully replicated the retrained
model’s performance on all key metrics while being significantly more computationally eﬀicient than
SCRUB+R. Its main drawback is the risk of ”over-forgetting,” highlighting the need for a principled
early stopping criterion to manage its high variance and ensure consistent results.
==PAGE:116==
100
==PAGE:117==
CHAPTER 6
Discussion
In the introduction of this thesis found in chapter 1, we presented Machine Unlearning as a pressing
and increasingly practical challenge, motivated by real-world demands for data control, regulatory com-
pliance, and model integrity. Using the metaphor of the “red elephant” and a set of fictional scientists,
we illustrated the diﬀiculty of removing information from a model once it has been internalized.
Within this framing, we defined three central objectives (see section 1.2):
RQ 1: What challenges and failure modes does parameter dampening in a neural network present, and
how can the method be improved to address them?
RQ 2: How does the existing fine-tuning objective in SCRUB+R perform in practice, and can a more
eﬀicient objective be developed for this unlearning paradigm?
RQ 3: How can insights from mechanistic interpretability, particularly regarding the challenge of pol-
ysemanticity in neural networks, inform the development of a novel unlearning approach?
While we have discussed the results in the experiments chapter 5, this chapter revisits our findings in the
context of these goals and questions. We evaluate the broader implications of our results, the trade-offs
between forgetting effectiveness and model utility, and the computational feasibility of the approaches
studied. We also discuss the generalizability of our results across cases and datasets, and reflect on the
strengths and weaknesses of current empirical metrics. Finally, we point to future directions, including
more realistic unlearning scenarios and improved evaluation protocols.
6.1
RQ 1: The failure cases of SSD and extending the method
A key observation regarding SSD is that the method is highly sensitive to the choice of its hyperparam-
eters. Setting them too low results in catastrophic forgetting as seen in both the controlled experiment
setting (section 5.1 and section 5.2) as well as when experimenting on CIFAR-10 (section 5.7). Con-
versely, setting them too high results in performing no model update at all. To further complicate
this, our results suggest that the appropriate choice of α and λ for SSD is dependent on the forget
set.
An extreme example of this can be found in Figure 5.18 where we see in case 4 that setting
α = 0.85, λ = 0.67 results in no model update. On the contrary, in section 5.1 we observe that SSD
with α = λ = 1 collapsed the decision boundary on all unlearning scenarios other than case 4.
Part of this thesis set out to address the limitation of manual hyperparameter selection in SSD. We
propose a novel method, BO-SSD, which estimates values of α and λ using Bayesian optimization.
Specifically, the parameters are chosen such that the unlearned model behavior on the forget set is
similar to the original model’s behavior on validation data. Variations of this method, BO-SSD paired
and BO-SSD smooth, are also proposed to address some potential limitations. While evaluating the BO-
smooth variant, we found little evidence for it to be an actual contribution, other than its adherence to
the underlying BO assumptions. Hence, in these curated, simple cases, we called the method a ’failure’.
During the preliminary case experiments, we identified several failure cases across the BO-SSD methods.
In section 5.3, we see that all BO-SSD variants wholly or significantly collapse the decision boundary
in adversarial unlearning settings. We attribute this to the objective function formulation along with
the procedure for constructing the repair dataset. A straightforward way to address this shortcoming
could be to construct the rewind dataset based on the predicted rather than the true labels. This would,
however, not necessarily work in cases where forget observations are positioned directly on the decision
==PAGE:118==
102
6 Discussion
boundary. In such settings, the original model may have a higher uncertainty on the forget set than on
the validation set.
While BO-SSD breaks in adversarial settings, the method is generally also too conservative in unlearning
settings that it was designed to cover. We see this both in section 5.3, where no model updates are
performed for multiple cases, as well as when experimenting on MNIST and CIFAR-10. This reaﬀirms
that the objective function design itself is suboptimal. Perhaps a better objective function could be
inspired by [Chu+23b], where one trains a ”bad teacher” smaller than the original model on the retain
set and guides the model during unlearning to behave similarly to the teacher on the forget set. While
this could yield better results, it introduces its own set of problems, such as less eﬀiciency, and choosing
an appropriate bad teacher is not straightforward.
Another critique of BO-SSD is that the procedure when sampling the rewind data can introduce infor-
mation leakage. This occurs in cases where the original model’s behavior on the rewind set is governed
by what it learned through forget observations during its initial training. We hypothesize that this
could be partially addressed by constructing the rewind set with validation samples whose FIM has
minimal overlap FIM on the forget set.
Here, overlap in FIM values refers to matrices containing
non-zero elements in the same positions.
In summary, the case experiments and the benchmarking results on MNIST and CIFAR-10 point towards
SSD being very dependent on the forget set and how it is distributed compared to the retain set.
6.2
RQ 2: The robustness of SCRUB and TA
While we were ”unlucky” when choosing the initial parameters of SSD in the preliminary experiments
(section 5.1), SCRUB+R showed strong performance.
However, the controlled setting experiments
did not give a clear answer as to whether SCRUB mimicked the behavior of the retrained model or
the original model. Additionally, since we only experimented with a single set of hyperparameters, it
gave little indication as to how sensitive the method is to changes in these parameters. This led us
to investigate SCRUB further in section 5.4 with emphasis on the method’s behavior in convergence,
varying the location and size of the forget set, and its sensitivity to the regularization strength.
A key takeaway from this experiment is that SCRUB converges towards catastrophic forgetting with
random performance on both retain, forget, and validation. We observe this behavior consistently both
when varying the forget set location and size as well as α and γ, the coeﬀicients for the retain terms in
the SCRUB objective. Additionally, once the model is suﬀiciently degraded, we are not able to recover
its performance. Although in practice, one would not run an unlearning algorithm longer than the time
it takes to retrain. This illustrates the method’s sensitivity to the chosen min-max epochs. Figure 5.27
illustrates this particularly well, where we see that a difference of just 5 epochs means the difference
between recovering the model performance and converging to catastrophic forgetting.
That being said, Figure 5.27 does illustrate that if the number of min-max epochs is chosen correctly, we
reach an appropriate final model. However, determining when to stop appears to be non-trivial. If we
follow the authors’ recommendation to stop training as soon as the retain set error decreases [Kur+23],
then in some of the cases shown in Figure 5.27, we would end up with a model that has higher forget
accuracy and MIA probability than the retrained model.
Grounded in the observed shortcomings of SCRUB, we set out to develop an unlearning method that
could address these. Our primary focus was to obtain an unlearning method that avoided the afore-
mentioned convergence towards catastrophic forgetting and was more eﬀicient than SCRUB. This led
to the development of TA, which, as seen in section 5.5, mitigates catastrophic forgetting on retain
and validation data. This behavior was consistent across the considered forget set locations and sizes.
Additionally, the method is significantly more eﬀicient compared to SCRUB, as shown by the time
performance on MNIST and CIFAR-10. While TA addresses key deficiencies in SCRUB, the method
degrades the performance of the forget set beyond what a retrained model would. We hypothesize
that this can be effectively recovered by including a repair phase, omitting the Shannon entropy term
in the TA objective function for some retain batches. Preliminary experiments not included in this
thesis indicate that this intuition may hold merit. However, this requires an in-depth analysis to assess
==PAGE:119==
6.3 RQ 3: Polysemanticity and machine unlearning
103
whether the unlearned model would recover information about the forget set that a retrained model
would not be able to generalize.
Additionally, for TA the accuracies seem to have a high variance across each epoch. We suggest look-
ing into learning rate scheduling, lowering the learning rate as we get close to the optimum, as we
hypothesize that this might stabilize it.
Although over-unlearning and a more varying accuracy are clear limitations of TA, we argue that it
remains a stronger starting point for developing a state-of-the-art unlearning approach compared to
SCRUB.
6.3
RQ 3: Polysemanticity and machine unlearning
We postulate that SSD would fail, even if ideal hyperparameters were chosen, in settings where the
model contains polysemantic neurons. However, investigating this hypothesis in practice proved more
diﬀicult than initially anticipated. Enforcing polysemanticity in neural networks remains a challenging
task for researchers, but is a necessary step to study this proposed failure case in a principled manner.
With that said, we did end up developing a novel approach based on mechanistic interpretability using
the SAE. This method shows limited success, albeit comparable to other baseline methods. One of the
clear downsides of this approach is the dependency of training the SAE for signal reconstruction. This
introduces greater complexity compared to the other methods, with the SAE in some cases requiring
more training time than a full retrain. This challenges the feasibility of the approach, given that we
argued MU is primarily relevant as a retraining substitute. However, since the SAE does not need to
be trained at unlearning time, one could argue that its training cost/time might reasonably be treated
as an exogenous factor.
Furthermore, we opted for a dampening strategy close to that of SSD except calculating the βj based
on Dr instead of the full training set D as done in SSD. SSD used D instead as they could then
calculate the FIM once and only have to calculate it for Df subsequently. Hence, our SAE approach
is now exposed to this ineﬀiciency. Albeit this was deemed a minor issue due to the other challenges.
Concurrent with designing the SAE method, we discovered just how reliant the SSD approach is on
hyperparameter selection, demonstrating more fundamental limitations of the method.
Although a
significant amount of time in the early phase of this project was spent on understanding mechanistic
interpretability, polysemanticity, and SAE’s, the later experiments do not reflect this.
Our benchmark results show that the SAE unlearning method suffers from the same failure modes
as SSD, either failing to produce any change or catastrophically breaking the model. This suggests
that decomposing features into an allegedly more monosemantic representation has little effect when
the retain and forget sets are drawn from the same distribution. If the learned dictionary features
have significant overlap across both sets, the dampening condition in (4.14) is rarely met, preventing
effective unlearning. This dependency on data distribution, coupled with uncertainty about whether a
truly monosemantic representation was achieved, makes the approach prone to the same fundamental
limitations as SSD.
As mentioned in section 4.3, this approach is better described as output suppression or guardrailing
rather than true unlearning. Since the original model weights remain untouched, an adversary with
suﬀicient access could simply remove the SAE layer to restore the original model. While we acknowledge
this limitation, a promising path for future research would be to leverage concepts from mechanistic
interpretability to perform genuine parameter modification, thereby achieving irreversible unlearning.
6.4
Future work
The field of machine unlearning remains in its early stages. In this thesis, we focused our scope on
a subset of methods identified through the literature review. Nonetheless, even within this narrower
scope, new ideas continuously emerged throughout the project, largely due to the young nature of the
field. Many of these ideas arose in response to experimental results and the discussion, while others
represent logical continuations of our work that could not be pursued within the available timeframe.
==PAGE:120==
104
6 Discussion
This section curates these ideas into concrete directions for future work. For clarity, we present them
as a list, beginning with the more fundamental and broadly impactful avenues we believe would most
benefit the field.
• The development of a benchmarking dataset for machine unlearning is extremely valuable. In
our project, we used random and t-SNE-segmented forget sets, but we hypothesize these are poor
proxies for real-world unlearning scenarios. This is critical, as we have shown that most methods
are highly sensitive to the forget set’s location, yet the full extent of such failure cases is rarely
covered in the literature.
We recognize a discrepancy between current academic approaches,
including our own limited exploration beyond random sampling, and the nature of unlearning
requests companies receive. A well-recognized benchmark with diverse forgetting scenarios would,
therefore, create immense value for the field.
• Our proposed SAE-based unlearning method is motivated by the assumption that SSD would
fail when dampening polysemantic neurons. While our experiments suggest SSD struggles when
forget and retain sets are very similar (section 5.6, section 5.7), we have not explicitly demonstrated
that polysemanticity is the cause of this failure, although it remains plausible [Ola+20; Cam+21;
OMS17; Elh+22]. Even though SAEs have very recently1 faced critique [Smi+25; HH25], the
underlying challenge of neurons encoding multiple concepts persists.
Thus, regardless of the
specific tool, there is a need to rigorously demonstrate and prove the failure case of dampening
such entangled representations.
• Our evaluation has focused on functional equivalence, treating the model as a black box by only
comparing its final output to that of a retrained model [Kla+23]. Future work should also assess
representational equivalence by analyzing the model’s internal structure. A promising avenue is to
use splines, drawing on research by Balestriero and Baraniuk [BB18] and methods like SplineCAM
[Hum+24]. This approach could enable the extraction of decision boundaries and their evolution
across layers. We could then apply functional analysis to these spline representations to rigorously
quantify the internal changes between the original, unlearned, and retrained models.
With the main future work directions established, we now outline several ablation studies that we had
planned but were unable to conduct due to time constraints.
• The objective function used for BO-SSD shows limitations, particularly in adversarial unlearning
scenarios. An important area for improvement is to modify this objective to leverage the model’s
own predictions on the rewind set, rather than the ground truth labels, which may better guide
the optimization process.
• The eﬀiciency of BO-SSD is currently hindered by its reliance on a fixed hyperparameter search
space. A more dynamic approach could use the FIM to define relevant search bounds for each
specific unlearning task. The success of this, however, would likely depend on the aforementioned
improvements to the optimization objective.
• To properly assess the advantages of using BO, its sampling eﬀiciency should be rigorously bench-
marked. A comparison against a baseline method, such as a systematic grid search, would be
essential to quantify the actual performance gains offered by BO for tuning SSD hyperparame-
ters.
• When working with BO it would also make sense to compare it with a baseline sampling method
such as a grid search over hyperparameters to better asses the sampling eﬀiciency of BO and its
objective.
• The optimization dynamics of TA could be improved to address the high variance in accuracy
observed during training. Future work could investigate methods to stabilize convergence, such
as implementing learning rate scheduling or adopting an alternating optimization scheme for the
1During the writing of this thesis, the use of SAEs for mechanistic interpretability was de-prioritized by prominent
research groups, including at Google DeepMind [Smi+25].
==PAGE:121==
6.4 Future work
105
minimization and maximization objectives, similar to the approach in SCRUB. In this context,
we would likely need to omit both the entropy and EWC term from the TA objective to avoid
over-unlearning as well as recovering information that a retrained model cannot generalize to.
• A rigorous analysis of the EWC-regularization term within the TA objective is also warranted.
Systematically studying the impact of its weighting on unlearning performance would provide a
deeper understanding of the trade-offs involved and could reveal avenues for further refinement of
the method.
• The relationship between data distribution and TA’s performance presents another key research
direction. One could measure the statistical divergence between the forget and retain set distri-
butions and examine its correlation with the model’s final accuracy on the forget set. This would
clarify how the separability of the data impacts unlearning eﬀicacy.
• A valuable future experiment would be to re-run the MNIST benchmarks using our t-SNE-
constructed datasets.
This would create scenarios where the forget and retain sets are distri-
butionally distinct, providing a more rigorous test for unlearning algorithms. We hypothesize
that synaptic dampening methods would be particularly effective in this setting, as the dissimi-
larities between the sets offer a clearer signal for what to forget. This approach also addresses a
conceptual limitation of random-forget experiments: When the forget and retain sets are similar,
the necessity of unlearning is debatable, as a retrained model’s generalization performance would
likely be unaffected.
Apart from these ablations and ideas for further studies, we also came up with some ideas for exploring
the behavior of the baselines further:
• For SISA [Bou+21], it would be interesting to explore whether the slicing actually has a beneficial
effect. We assume the performance will be harmed slightly by training on only small amounts of the
data at the beginning of the slices instead of just training on all the data. It would be interesting
to explore the tradeoff between the loss landscapes and the expected cost of retraining on slices.
We speculate that the method might not actually make sense, as we might lose performance due
to slicing, which might not be worth the performance gain. At least, it is not explored that well
in the SISA paper.
• In amnesiac unlearning, it could be interesting to, based on prior knowledge, place samples that are
more likely to be forgotten in the same batches. We will most likely get more noisy gradients, as a
batch will now (probably) represent a subpopulation of the dataset and not the whole population,
but it is an interesting idea. Additionally, a weighted repair mechanism could be investigated
for amnesiac unlearning. During the repair phase, higher importance could be assigned to retain
set samples that co-occurred in batches with the now forgotten data, potentially accelerating the
model’s performance recovery.
• Future work could explore alternatives to the FIM for deriving parameter importance scores. One
promising approach is to employ causal mediation analysis [Pea13]. Inspired by its use in tracing
information flow through networks [Vig+20], this technique could identify which components
are most influential and should therefore be targeted for dampening. While this method would
not in itself solve challenges such as polysemanticity [Elh+22], it could offer a principled, causal
framework that could, in turn, yield deeper insights into network dynamics during unlearning.
==PAGE:122==
106
==PAGE:123==
CHAPTER 7
Conclusion
As we draw this work to a close, it is worth remembering the red elephant in the room, quite literally.
Like our hypothetical data contributor who one day decided they were no longer a red elephant, we
have spent this thesis untangling how a stubborn model might come to share that realization. Machine
Unlearning remains a challenging field, with the current methods showing clear drawbacks. Out of
four baseline methods, SISA, Amnesiac Unlearning, SCRUB+R, SSD, and its extension ASSD, we
found that most work well, except for the original SSD. This method showed a detrimental sensitivity
to its hyperparameters (RQ1).
Through rigorous case-driven experimentation, we developed three
novel extensions to the method, BO-SSD, BO-SSD-paired, and BO-SSD-smooth. Albeit evaluated on
the benchmark datasets MNIST and CIFAR-10 as well as in a controlled setting, their eﬀicacy for
unlearning remained inconclusive (RQ1).
Concurrently, inspired by signs of robustness in preliminary experiments, we explored SCRUB+R exten-
sively. We found that like SSD, SCRUB+R was similarly sensitive to its hyperparameters, specifically
the number of min-max steps to take before initiating its repair phase (RQ2). Without proper hy-
perparameters SCRUB+R converged towards catastrophic forgetting. Grounded in these findings, we
developed a new gradient ascent-based fine-tuning approach called Teacher Ascent. This method bor-
rows principles from EWC and demonstrates promising convergence properties, successfully forgetting
target data while maintaining high performance on the data to retain, although it varies a lot across
epochs (RQ2). Future work includes exploring whether we could repair some of its performance and
schedule the learning rate, which might help stabilize the high variance accuracy across epochs. Finally,
we postulated that a clear failure case for neuron-level machine unlearning methods, such as SSD, would
be polysemanticity. To this extent, we propose a novel unlearning method that leverages a Sparse Au-
toencoder (SAE) to extract more disentangled, ”monosemantic” features from a network’s activations
(RQ3). Although this was a later addition to our project and not as extensively analyzed, this approach
allows for dampening to occur in a more meaningful feature space, representing a promising direction
for future research.
All in all, while the journey to help our researchers unlearn the red elephant proved more intricate
than expected, the lessons drawn from these experiments shine a light on promising directions. Teacher
Ascent and the sparse autoencoder approach offer encouraging steps forward, even if some results remain
inconclusive for now.

APPENDIX A
Appendix
A.1
Transformer Block Architecture
As mentioned in subsection 3.1.3, the core of the ViT is a series of identical transformer blocks. Based
on Figure 3.4, this section will detail the individual parts of the transformer block, i.e., the layer
normalization, multi-head attention, and the feed-forward network with residual skip connections.
A.1.1
Layer Normalization
Layer normalization [BKH16] is applied in multiple places of the transformer pipeline to stabilize train-
ing. For a sequence of patch embeddings z1, . . . , zN, the operation normalizes each patch embedding
individually as:
˜zi = γ zi −µzi
p
σ2zi + ϵ + β
(A.1)
where
µzi = 1
dh
dh
X
j=1
zj
(A.2)
σ2
zi = 1
dh
dh
X
j=1
(zj −µzi)2
(A.3)
and γ, β ∈Rdh are learnable parameters. The term ϵ is a small constant (e.g., 10−5) added to the
variance for numerical stability, preventing division by zero in cases where the variance is very close to
or exactly zero. As such, each patch embedding is normalized to have a mean close to β and a variance
close to γ2.
A.1.2
Attention
The backbone of the Transformer architecture is the attention mechanism. Informally, it enables the
model to relate every patch to every other patch in an input image Let W Q ∈Rdh×dk, W K ∈Rdh×dk,
and W V ∈Rdh×dk denote learnable projection matrices for the queries, keys, and values, respectively.
The first step is to project z using set matrices as:
Q = ZW Q
K = ZW K
V = ZW V
(A.4)
Each row of Q, K, V corresponds to a token (or patch) in the sequence and represents the query, key,
and value vectors, respectively. A key component of the attention mechanism is the attention weight
matrix:
A = softmaxrow
 
QKT
√dk
!
(A.5)
The matrix product QKT results in a N ×N matrix, and the (i, j)’th element measures how similar the
query vector of the i’th patch is to the key vector of the j’th patch. The scaling factor
1
√dk is included
to scale down the magnitude of the dot products, which can push the softmax function into regions
==PAGE:140==
124
A Appendix
with small gradients [Vas+17]. The softmaxrow(·) function is the canonical softmax (subsection 3.1.1)
applied row-wise to the matrix. Putting this together, the i’th row of A will include elements in the
range [0, 1] that contain a relative importance weighting of the i’th patch with every other patch.
Finally, the attention output is computed as:
Attention(Q, K, V ) = softmax
 
QKT
√dk
!
V
(A.6)
Each row of the resulting matrix is a weighted sum of the value vectors, where the weights come from
the corresponding row in A.
Vaswani et al. [Vas+17] found it beneficial to perform the attention operation h times using different
query, key, and value matrices and concatenating the outputs. As such, we have a set of projection
matrices {(W Q
i , W K
i , W V
i )}h
i=1. Applying this results in a matrix AO ∈RN×h·dh on the form:
M =
Attention(Q1, K1, V 1)
Attention(Q2, K2, V 2)
. . .
Attention(Qh, Kh, V h)
(A.7)
To preserve dimensionality between inputs and outputs to multi-head attention, M is projected via a
learnable matrix W O ∈Rh·dh×dh. Putting this together, the Multi-head Attention operation becomes:
Multi-head Attention = MW O
(A.8)
A.1.3
Feed-forward Network and Residual Skip Connection
The final component of the transformer encoder is a two-layer neural network with GeLU as the
activation function1 and dff as the latent dimension.
As such, the first layer contains parameters
W (1) ∈Rdh×dff , b(1) ∈Rdff and the second layer consists of W (2) ∈Rdff ×dh, b(2) ∈Rdh. The
forward pass of the network thus becomes:
Z′ = GeLU

ZW (1) + b(1)
(A.9)
ˆZ = Z′W (2) + b(2)
(A.10)
The last building block of the ViT is residual skip connections, which serve to stabilize model training.
Generally, for a function f(·) residual skip connection refers to the operation:
y = f(x) + x
(A.11)
A.2
Derivation of SAE Loss from a Laplace Prior
In subsection 3.2.3, we stated that the l1 regularization term in the SAE objective arises from imposing
a Laplace prior on the latent variables
The derivation begins by considering the joint probability distribution of the visible variables x and the
latent variables z:
p(x, z) = p(z)p(x|z)
(A.12)
Here, p(z) represents the model’s prior distribution over the latent variables, encoding the model’s
beliefs prior to observing x.
This differs from the usual ”prior” referring to the distribution p(θ)
encoding our beliefs about model parameters before seeing training data [GBC16]. The log-likelihood
of the visible variables can be written as follows by marginalizing out the latent variables:
log p(x) = log
X
z
p(z, x)
(A.13)
1https://docs.pytorch.org/docs/stable/generated/torch.nn.GELU.html

log p(x) ≈log max

A.3.2
Regularity conditions and proof that the Expected Score is Zero:
Ex∼p(x|θ) [s(θ)] = 0
To demonstrate that the expectation of the score function is zero, and for other desirable properties in
statistical inference such as the Cramer-Rao Lower Bound (CRLB), certain regularity conditions regard-
ing the Probability Density Function (PDF) (or PMF for discrete variables) p(x|θ) and its derivatives
must be met [CB07, sec. 2.4]. These conditions, drawing from the work of Kullback [Kul97, p. 26-27]
and Cramer [Cra99, p. 500-501], can be summarized as follows:
RC1 The support of p(x|θ) (i.e., the set of x values for which p(x|θ) > 0) does not depend on the
parameter θ. This ensures that the limits of integration (or summation) are constant with respect
to θ. For instance, this holds for a Gaussian distribution whose support (−∞, ∞) is independent
of its mean µ or variance σ2, but not for a uniform distribution U(0, θ) whose support [0, θ]
depends on θ.
RC2 For almost all x and for all θ in the parameter space, the first three partial derivatives of the
log-likelihood function, log p(x|θ), with respect to θ exist. That is,
∂log p(x|θ)
∂θ
,
∂2 log p(x|θ)
∂θ2
, and
∂3 log p(x|θ)
∂θ3
are well-defined. This implies the existence of the corresponding derivatives of p(x|θ)
itself, provided p(x|θ) > 0.
RC3 The operations of differentiation with respect to θ and integration (or summation) over x can be
interchanged for p(x|θ) and its first two derivatives with respect to θ. Specifically for the first
derivative, this means
d
dθ
R
p(x|θ) dx =
R
∂
∂θp(x|θ) dx. This condition is satisfied if, for example,
the absolute partial derivative
 ∂
∂θp(x|θ)
 is bounded by an integrable function F(x) over the
==PAGE:143==
A.3 Derivations for the Fisher Information
127
entire support of x, i.e.,
 ∂
∂θp(x|θ)
 ≤F(x) such that
R
F(x) dx < ∞. Similar conditions apply
for higher-order derivatives if needed [Cra99; Kul97].
The property that the expectation of the score is zero, i.e., Ex∼p(x|θ) [s(θ)] = 0 (Equation 3.47 from the
main text), is a critical result that follows directly from these regularity conditions, particularly RC1
and RC3 enabling the key step in the proof. In Kay [Kay93], the condition for the score to have zero
expectation is effectively that the order of integration and differentiation is interchangeable. We see the
above conditions as providing more granular prerequisites. These conditions are also foundational for
the derivation of the CRLB. [Kay93; Van07].
Given these regularity conditions, the proof that the expectation of the score is zero proceeds as follows:
Ex∼p(x|θ) [s(θ)] = Ex∼p(x|θ)
 ∂
∂θ log p(x|θ)

Definition of s(θ)
=
Z

 ∂

Interchange of diff. and int. (RC1 and RC3)

(A.24)
This is an example for the continuous case. The proof is similar for the discrete case [Van07, sec. 3.2].
Thus, under the stated regularity conditions, the expected value of the score function is zero. This
result is crucial for establishing that the Fisher information is the variance of the score function.

A.3.5
Explaining howˆi(D)
j
/ˆi(Df)
j
can be less than 1
Suppose we have a highly unique sample in our dataset (˜x, ˜y) that we need to forget. During training,
the model has allocated a specific parameter θj (i.e., the parameter at index j) to ”memorize” this
sample. Let Df = {(˜x, ˜y)} be the forget set containing only this single observation. We compute the
vector of diagonal empirical FIM elements for this forget set as ˆi
(Df ) = ˆiDf (θ). Let’s assume that the
vector ˆi
(Df ) is sparse, and its k-th element ˆi(Df )
k
is given by:
ˆi(Df )
k
=
(
δ
k = j
0
k ̸= j
This means that only the j-th diagonal element of the empirical FIM for the forget set, ˆi(Df )
j
, is non-zero
and equal to δ. If we compute the vector of diagonal empirical FIM elements for the entire training
==PAGE:146==
130
A Appendix
dataset D as ˆi
(D) = ˆiD(θ), we can imagine that the j-th parameter θj is entirely isolated from the
influence of other samples when considering its contribution to the FIM, scaled by the dataset size.
Under this simplified assumption, its corresponding FIM element might be approximated as:
ˆi(D)
j
≈
δ
|D|
Under this light, it is clear to see how the ratio of the j-th elements, ˆi(D)
j
/ˆi(Df )
j
, can become smaller
than 1 (specifically, ≈1/|D|) even though Df ⊂D. However, for the diagonal of the theoretical FIM,
does it hold that its k-th element satisfies I(D)
k
= I(Df )
k
+ I(Dr)
k
? If that were the case, then if we
could somehow compute the true FIM diagonal, SSD (when comparing FIM for Df against D) would
not work as expected unless parameters important for Df were not important for Dr. An alternative
comparison might be between Df and Dr.
If the true underlying data generating distributions for
Df and D are different (even if Df is a subset of the samples in D), then the k-th elements of their
theoretical FIMs, I(Df )
k
and I(D)
k
, are derived from expectations over potentially different distributions.
In such a scenario, the ratio I(D)
k
/I(Df )
k
can be smaller than 1. This is because the expectation for the
theoretical FIM is computed with respect to the true data generating process for the respective dataset.
A.4
Exploring the decision boundary (case driven experiments)
This appendix details the experiments conducted for the analysis presented in section 5.1. The hy-
perparameters used for these experiments are listed in Table 5.1. A description of the synthetic data
generation for each case is provided in section 2.1. The visual results for the five cases are presented in
order in Table A.1, Table A.2, Table A.3, Table A.4, and Table A.5.

A.7
Teacher Ascent Experiments
A.7.1
Teacher Ascent variants
In Figure A.4 and Figure A.5, we have shown all the tested variants of Teacher Ascent as explained in
section 4.2 and section 5.5.
A.7.2
Experiments with different λs
We have tested setting λ = 200000, λ = 2000, and λ = 0 as seen in Figure A.6.
We also try setting λ = 0 as seen in Figure A.6(a). Here we see that the model breaks, indicating that
our EWC regularization term does indeed have an effect. In conclusion, TA does not seem to be very
sensitive to λ, but we do need the term. The reason could be that the term already has a much greater
magnitude than the other terms.

A.7.3
